# The Lucid Hours

# Chapter 1

The air at Lakeview Gardens didn't move in August. The building's central AC had been serviced in June. Carmen had seen the work order on Cliff Moorehead's desk, signed and filed, problem solved on paper. But the vents in Wing A pushed out a thin, tepid breath that did nothing against the heat stored in the walls and the linoleum and the bodies of sixty-three residents who hadn't been outside since the Fourth of July barbecue in the courtyard, the one with the grocery-store sheet cake and the aide who burned the hot dogs. The smell was the first thing about Lakeview Gardens and the last thing. Pine disinfectant laid over urine was the base note, the constant, the thing visitors noticed and staff stopped noticing after a week. On top of it: the yeasty warmth of skin that didn't get enough air, institutional chicken from the dinner trays stacked on the cart outside the kitchen, hand sanitizer from the dispensers mounted every fifteen feet, and under all of it, close and sweet and impossible to name precisely, the smell of people who lived in a building. Whose skin and hair and breath and the lotions rubbed into their bedsores and the talcum powder shaken into their skin folds had become part of the building's own air.

Carmen Reyes pulled on a pair of nitrile gloves and knocked on the doorframe of Room 110.

"I'm going to help you turn now, OK?"

The moaning didn't change. It never did. The man in 110 was ninety-one and contracted into a permanent C-shape, a contracture that had set in years before Carmen started — his knees drawn to his chest, his hands curled against his sternum, his spine a fixed curve that no amount of physical therapy was going to straighten. He moaned on every exhale. A low, steady sound, not distressed exactly, more like the sound a building makes when the pipes are running — constant, mechanical, part of the room. Carmen had been at Lakeview thirteen months. She didn't hear it anymore unless she stopped and listened on purpose.

She put her hands on his shoulder and hip, braced her feet on the linoleum, and rolled him toward her. He weighed sixty-eight pounds according to the care plan and she could believe it — he was terrifyingly light, a body that had shed everything it didn't absolutely need. She repositioned the pillow between his knees, tugged the draw sheet smooth under his back, and checked his sacrum. She pressed two fingers against the skin at the base of his spine and watched for blanching, for the redness that meant pressure was building toward a wound. The skin was intact. She noted the time on the positioning log clipped to the wall: 4:15 PM. He needed to be turned every two hours. The six o'clock turn was supposed to be the aide's job, but Devon had called out sick for the second time this week, and the evening was short-staffed, which meant Carmen would either do it herself between med pass and dinner trays or find Tammy, who was already covering both wings alone.

She stripped the gloves, dropped them in the bin by the door, pumped sanitizer from the wall dispenser into her palms, and rubbed them together as she walked. The sanitizer was cold for half a second, then warm, then gone. Her hands were dry and cracked at the knuckles from it. Thirteen months of hand sanitizer and handwashing and nitrile gloves, and her hands looked ten years older than the rest of her.

Room 112A. Lola Fernandez was sitting up in bed with a game show on, the volume turned low. Her roommate was asleep behind the half-drawn privacy curtain — a thin woman who weighed less than the man in 110 and slept twenty hours a day. Lola's silver hair was loose on her shoulders. She hadn't been put to bed yet. She held a coffee cup in both hands, and the tremor was visible from the doorway — a fine, constant shake that made the surface of the coffee ripple under the overhead fluorescent. The gold crucifix at her throat caught the light from the television.

"Mija," Lola said. She smiled — warm, loose, present but not entirely tracking. "Come keep me company for one minute."

"How are you feeling tonight, Mrs. Fernandez?"

"Oh, these hands are being rude." Lola lifted the saucer a fraction and watched the coffee shiver. "Tammy'll fuss if I spill on the blanket again. She fusses nice, though. Is this the one with the doors? No, no, that's the other one. My daughter sends those church cards with the saints in blue robes. I keep meaning to write back." She trailed off, still smiling. The tremor made the cup rattle faintly against the saucer on her lap.

"Tammy can handle a little coffee."

"Yeah. She handles everybody."

Carmen wrapped the blood pressure cuff around Lola's left arm, pumped, watched the gauge, released. She clipped the pulse oximeter onto Lola's index finger and waited for the number. She wrote the vitals on her clipboard. Lola's Parkinson's was in the chart — the neurologist's notes from six months ago described early-stage disease, resting tremor predominant, cogwheel rigidity in the right wrist. It was real. The tremor made Lola's handwriting a kind of seismograph, turned her signature on the meal-preference form into a jittery line that barely resembled the version from her admission paperwork a year and a half ago.

"Tammy's going to come by and help you get ready for bed, OK?"

Lola nodded, already turning back to the television. A woman from somewhere in the Midwest was trying to guess the price of a washer-dryer set. The host's voice was bright and urgent and had nothing to do with anything in the room.

---

The medication pass was supposed to take sixty minutes. It took ninety when the evening was fully staffed and longer when it wasn't. Carmen pushed the med cart down the hall of Wing A, room by room, the cart's bad wheel catching on every rotation with a click that followed her like a metronome. She'd put in a maintenance request for the wheel three weeks ago and again last week. The wheel still clicked.

Each resident had a labeled slot in the cart's drawers. Each dose was cross-referenced against the medication administration record on her clipboard — drug name, dosage, route, time, her initials, her license number. She poured pills into paper cups, handed them over with water, watched them swallow, checked mouths, documented. The routine was the same every shift. The routine was the job.

Agnes Chen, Room 108A. The room was dim. Agnes's roommate was asleep on her back with her mouth open, her dementia so advanced that she occupied the bed the way furniture occupied a room — present, unaware, requiring maintenance. Agnes sat in her wheelchair by the window, her back straight, facing the wall. She was small and thin, gray hair cut short, wire-rimmed glasses reflecting the slant of hallway light. She didn't turn when Carmen came in. She didn't acknowledge the cart or the pill cup or the water or Carmen.

"Mrs. Chen? I have your evening medications."

Nothing. The wall held Agnes's attention with an intensity that suggested it was showing her something no one else could see.

"You can take them when you're ready, or I can come back before end of shift."

Agnes didn't move. Carmen set the cup and water on the bedside table and documented the refusal on the MAR — *Patient declined, medications left at bedside per patient preference.* It was Agnes's right to refuse. Some nights she took them. Some nights she sat in the dark like this, silent and upright, and Carmen would find the pills untouched at the next check. The physicians had noted it. Nobody had changed anything about it.

Room 116A. Earl Washington was in the recliner by the window, a magazine open on his lap — something from the facility's waiting-room stack, months old, a copy of *Reader's Digest* with a cover about Mediterranean diets. Earl was tall even folded into the chair, with hands large enough to make the magazine look like a pamphlet. He had the settled, still expression of a man who had spent decades in rooms where other people talked and he listened and when he spoke the room paid attention because it happened so rarely. He looked up when Carmen came in. He nodded once.

"Evening medications, Mr. Washington."

He took the cup, swallowed everything with one pull of water, and handed the cup back. He didn't speak. Carmen checked the time, documented, and moved on.

Burt Kovacs occupied the other bed in 116A. He was in it, which was where Burt usually was by this hour — propped up on pillows, his wispy white hair pressed flat on one side, the blanket pulled up to his chest. His hands lay on top of the blanket, long and thin, with the fine bones of someone who'd done precise, delicate work. They shook — not the rhythmic tremor of Parkinson's disease but something looser, a general trembling that came from everywhere at once, as though his body couldn't quite hold itself steady. Carmen checked the fentanyl patch on his left shoulder, pressed down an edge that was starting to curl, and offered his evening oxycodone in the paper cup.

"How's the pain tonight, Mr. Kovacs?"

He blinked at her. His eyes moved across her face without settling. Something tried to organize itself behind them — a recognition, a thought — and didn't make it.

"Erzsébet?" The name came out with a soft Hungarian vowel on the first syllable. His wife. She'd been dead five years.

"It's Carmen. Your nurse tonight."

He kept looking at her, or past her, at something the room contained that she couldn't see. His mouth worked for a moment without producing sound. Then he took the pills with the mechanical compliance of a man who no longer tracked what he was swallowing or why. Carmen gave him water, checked his mouth, adjusted his blanket. She touched his shoulder briefly — not procedure, just contact — and he didn't seem to register it.

She moved on. The cart's wheel clicked behind her down the hall.

---

At seven o'clock, Tammy Briggs was in the supply closet restocking her cart with disposable briefs and bed pads. Her scrubs carried the damp-laundry smell of someone who'd been lifting and turning and cleaning for four hours in a building where the air conditioning was a polite fiction. Her ponytail had loosened to the point where it was less a hairstyle than a reminder that one had existed earlier. She looked tired, but the tiredness on Tammy had settled into the grooves beside her mouth, the set of her shoulders, the flat efficiency of her movements. Carmen couldn't remember ever seeing her look rested.

"Devon called out," she said, not looking up from the supply shelf.

"I know."

"So I'm running both wings by myself, which, yeah." She pulled a package of underpads from the shelf and set it on her cart with the economy of someone who'd done this motion so many times her hands knew the weight and trajectory without her brain's involvement. "Mrs. Ramos was in the same brief she was in at three o'clock when I got to her. I changed her right after, but that's what it is. That's what happens."

"I'll take the turns on Wing A if you can handle the bed routines."

"Already done. Mrs. Fernandez is set: brushed her hair, got her teeth soaking, water on the nightstand. Two sugars and milk for the morning coffee. Not cream, milk. She'll tell you it doesn't matter but it matters." Tammy said it the way she said everything, flat and factual, the information delivered without warmth or complaint, just the accumulated knowledge of fifteen years of showing up. She knew Lola Fernandez's coffee order the way she knew which call bells were real emergencies and which were loneliness and which were the button getting pressed by accident when a resident shifted in bed. "Her roommate's done too. Mr. Patel's done. I'll get the rest of B wing after I restock."

Carmen's badge had twisted on its lanyard. She straightened it without thinking so the LPN credential, her photo, and the Lakeview Gardens logo all faced forward. "Did Cliff come by?"

"Five o'clock. Wanted the fall-risk numbers for the quarter."

"What'd you say?"

"I told him they were in the system. He said he couldn't find them. I told him they were in the system." She paused, looked at Carmen with an expression that contained an entire performance review of Cliff Moorehead's administrative capabilities. "He went away."

Carmen had seen Cliff earlier, briefly, passing through the front hallway between the lobby and his office at around 4:30. He'd been moving with the soft, hurried posture of a man shaped by his desk. He was fifty-two, round in the way that suggested long hours sitting and lunch eaten in front of a computer, wearing a purple polo that meant he'd come from some kind of meeting. He'd nodded at Carmen without stopping. He cared about survey scores and census numbers and documentation that looked correct from a distance, which was different from documentation that was correct, a distinction Carmen had learned to navigate without anyone explicitly teaching her.

"He wants the metrics," Tammy said, pushing her cart toward the door. "He doesn't want to know what they cost."

She paused at the hallway junction and glanced back. "Oh — I put in a request for more medium gloves. We're out again. The smalls don't fit anybody and the larges are like putting your hands in grocery bags. So." She shrugged and turned the corner. Her cart's wheels didn't squeak. Tammy's equipment worked because Tammy fixed things herself rather than waiting for maintenance to ignore the request.

At 7:35, as Carmen parked the cart outside Room 114 and lined Harry Zielinski's blister cards against the raised lip of the med-cart top, Tammy called from 110. The man in 110 had slid crooked against the rail and soaked through the brief and pad. Carmen locked the cart, snapped on fresh gloves, and went in. Between the two of them they rolled him, cleaned him, changed the brief, checked the sacrum again, and hauled the draw sheet flat under his spine. By the time Carmen stripped the gloves, her badge was stuck to her chest, the med pass was forty minutes behind, and Harry's open MAR was still waiting on the cart where she'd left it.

Room 114A was a single. Harry was eighty-four, a widower, with dementia charted as progressive and moderate-stage in the neurologist's last assessment from April. Every evening he asked about his wife Eleanor. The questions came in the same bewildered order, as though her death were news being delivered to him for the first time. He was angular and thin, with pale eyes that watered under the hallway fluorescents and a faded blue cardigan he wore to every interaction Carmen had ever had with him. He smelled like facility laundry and the close, stale warmth of a room he almost never left.

She checked the MAR again. Alprazolam, one milligram. Tramadol, one hundred milligrams, two tablets. Lisinopril. Simvastatin. Before Tammy called her away, she'd started punching one tramadol through the foil and only half of it had snapped free into the cup. When she came back sweating and behind, her brain registered only that she hadn't finished. She punched out two whole tablets and carried the paper cup into the room.

Harry was already working himself up about Eleanor when she came in, fingers plucking at the blue cardigan. "She doesn't know where I am. Did you call her? She had the appointment book. She always kept the appointment book."

"Let's get your evening meds in first, Mr. Zielinski."

He took the cup, looked at the pills, and his eyes changed.

It did not happen gradually or like a fog thinning. It happened all at once, like somebody had reached behind his face and thrown a switch. The watery confusion went out of him and something precise and impatient took its place. He looked at the cup, then at Carmen.

"That's two-fifty milligrams. Tramadol's two hundred. You put in an extra half-tab."

Carmen looked down. Two whole tramadol tablets and the extra broken half sat among the rest of his evening meds. The dose was fifty milligrams over. It was a caught-before-swallow med error, no resident harm done, the kind of mistake nurses made when the shift was already chewing on them.

Except she hadn't caught it. He had.

She took the cup back, plucked out the extra half-tab, and checked the blister card against the MAR one more time because her own eyes had stopped feeling reliable. "You're right. Thank you. Let me fix it."

And then he was gone. It lasted two seconds, maybe three. His hands went back to the cardigan. His eyes slid out of focus. He looked past her shoulder into the hallway or into another year entirely.

"Eleanor?" he said. "Did you tell her? She gets upset when nobody tells her things."

Carmen gave him the corrected dose. He swallowed with the automatic compliance of a man who no longer tracked what he was swallowing or why. She checked his mouth, documented the administration, and left the room with the extra half-tab wrapped in a medication cup on top of the cart because she was going to need a witness for waste and didn't have one yet. She didn't document the moment itself. There was nowhere in the charting system for *patient with moderate dementia corrected my math before I did.*

At the next three rooms she checked every cup twice before she let it leave her hand. The med pass, already late, dragged later. Harry's sentence had gotten into her hands.

---

By 10:30, the building had settled into its night register. The overhead lights in the hallway were at half-brightness, the institutional dusk that was supposed to promote sleep and mostly promoted a flat, shadowless dimness that made everything look slightly unreal. The sounds of the day shift had dropped away: therapy sessions, visitors' voices, the clatter of meal trays, call bells answered quickly because there were enough people to answer them. What remained was the building's permanent underneath. The ventilation hummed. A wheelchair squeaked on linoleum somewhere on B wing. The TV in Room 104 was still on, Mrs. Cho's Korean dramas running until she fell asleep with the remote in her hand and someone on the overnight shift turned it off around two in the morning. And from Room 110, steady and unchanging, the moaning, which at this hour carried further because there was less noise to absorb it.

Carmen did her end-of-shift controlled substance count in the medication room. The room was small and smelled like alcohol wipes and the faint chalky dust of pills that had been counted and poured and counted all day. She opened the locked cabinet, pulled each bottle, and counted. Oxycodone five-milligram tablets: she tapped each one onto the counting tray with her fingertip, moving them from one side to the other. Alprazolam, one milligram. Tramadol, one hundred milligrams. She recorded the numbers, compared them to the 3 PM starting count, cross-referenced against the MAR for every dose she'd dispensed. The math was clean. She signed the log, got the wasted half-tab witnessed, and placed the sheet on the counter for the countersignature.

The night nurse was a registry temp named Keisha whom Carmen had never seen before. She was already at the station, phone in hand, a travel mug of something next to the keyboard. Carmen gave report: the fall risks, the NPO orders for morning labs, which residents were restless and might ring, which ones hadn't voided and should be checked.

"Got it," Keisha said.

"Room 110 needs to be turned at midnight. The positioning log is on the wall."

"Okay."

Carmen almost added something about Harry in 114 and stopped. There was no handoff version that didn't sound either ridiculous or self-incriminating. She washed her hands for twenty seconds, the birthday song in her head, the duration on the hand-hygiene poster so deeply internalized that she did it at home, at Gloria's, at the gas station bathroom, then pulled off her badge and clocked out.

The parking lot was dark and held the day's heat in the asphalt. Late August in central New Jersey didn't cool down at night so much as it stopped getting worse. The air was heavy and still and sat on her skin like something with weight. Two of the lot lights still worked. The rest had been out since spring. Carmen's car, a 2019 Honda Civic with a cracked passenger-side taillight from backing into a concrete bollard in a parking garage three months ago, a repair she still couldn't afford, was parked under the farther of the two working lights. She sat in it with the engine idling and the AC blowing directly on her face, the sweat cooling in her hairline and in the small of her back where her scrubs had been sticking to her skin since six o'clock. She sat there for a minute, maybe two, and then she put the car in drive.

---

Gloria opened the door at 11:20 in a housecoat, holding a mug of chamomile tea. The house smelled the way it had smelled Carmen's entire life: garlic, Fabuloso, and the votiva candle on the kitchen counter next to the framed photograph of Carmen's father. He'd been dead eight years. The candle burned new every Monday.

"He ate his rice. He didn't eat the beans."

"He never eats the beans, Mami."

"I made them soft, with the ham pieces. The way he likes."

"He's seven. He doesn't like beans."

Gloria's face compressed into an expression that held several firmly maintained opinions about childhood nutrition, the decline of home cooking, and the general state of a generation that would rather eat chicken nuggets than *frijoles negros* made with love and a ham bone. "He's in the back."

Reggie was asleep on the foldout couch in what had been Carmen's bedroom and was now Gloria's sewing room. He was curled on his side with one sock on and one sock off, and a library book about sea turtles lay open on the pillow next to his head. He was small for seven, forty-eight pounds at his last pediatric checkup. The doctor said that was fine, he'd catch up. When he slept, his face still had the soft, unfinished quality of a much younger child, the cheekbones not yet sharpened into the structure that was going to be Carmen's.

She picked him up. He stirred, mumbled something about a whale shark, and dropped back to sleep with the absolute surrender of a child who had never once worried about where he would wake up. She carried him down the hall past the kitchen with its garlic smell and its candle and its counter lined with the same spice jars that had been there when Carmen was in high school.

"Mija, there's a plate for you in the fridge. Take it."

"I'm fine."

"You're not fine. Estás trabajando demasiado."

"I'm picking up a double tomorrow. I'll bring him Friday morning."

"Friday," Gloria said, in the tone of someone deciding whether to argue and then deciding it wasn't worth it. She stood in the doorway and watched Carmen strap Reggie into his booster seat. For a moment her face held something, maybe worry, or maybe the knowledge that worry was useless and she was going to do it anyway, before she raised a hand and went inside.

Carmen drove. Fifteen minutes from Gloria's to the apartment, with the late, thin traffic that existed only after eleven on a weeknight in this part of New Jersey: a few trucks on Route 18, the occasional set of headlights coming the other way, the strip malls dark except for the signs. Reggie slept in the back with his head tilted against the booster seat's wing. She didn't turn on the radio. She drove in the specific silence of a person whose entire day had been sound: call bells, moaning, the med cart's clicking wheel, Tammy's flat commentary, Lola's game show, the ventilation hum, Keisha's "got it." She needed the quiet the way her lower back needed to not be lifting anyone for the next six hours.

The apartment was on the second floor of a two-story building on a street of two-story buildings, in a neighborhood that had been built for young families in the 1980s and now rented to whoever could make the number work. The first-floor tenant cooked at all hours. The stairwell smelled like cumin and something else, maybe fenugreek or cardamom, that Carmen had never identified and didn't dislike but couldn't escape. She carried Reggie up the stairs, his forty-eight pounds heavier than the sixty-eight-pound man in 110 because Reggie was warm and alive and shifting in her arms, and her shoulders were already stiff from the turns she'd done on Wing A. She got him into his bed, pulled off the remaining sock, pulled up the sheet.

The apartment was two bedrooms, a kitchen she could cross in three steps, a bathroom with a stall shower and no tub. She'd found it by spending three days on her phone between patients, calling every listing under fourteen hundred a month within a twenty-five-minute drive of Lakeview. Twenty-five minutes was the limit. It was the farthest she could push the commute and still make the pickup from Gloria's and back in time for the next shift. The only place under the line had this second-floor walk-up, a shower stall with cracked caulk, and rent she could cover as long as the Civic didn't need anything.

She ate standing at the counter: arroz con pollo she'd cooked Sunday and portioned into containers for the week, each one the same size, each one enough. She washed the container, dried it, put it in the cabinet for next Sunday's batch. She set her alarm for 1 PM. The double started at three, straight through the overnight. She needed the hours, and someone would call out, and Lakeview always needed a body. She brushed her teeth. She stripped off her scrubs and dropped them in the hamper. Her other pair, the only other pair, was hanging on the shower rod where she'd washed them by hand two nights ago because the laundromat was $3.50 a load and the math didn't always include it.

She got into bed. The sheets were clean from Sunday. The window AC unit kicked on with a rattle and a hum, and the apartment settled into its own nighttime quiet: the tenant downstairs, the occasional car on the street, the distant steady sound of Route 18 that never fully stopped.

---

The thing that kept her awake was not that Harry had been clear for three seconds. Lucid intervals happened. What kept her awake was the way he'd looked at her before the fog came back: not startled or grateful, irritated almost, like she'd made a stupid mistake in front of somebody who expected better.

She rolled onto her side. Her pillow smelled like her shampoo and the faint astringent residue of hand sanitizer she could never entirely wash out of her hair. She had a double in fourteen hours and Reggie needed new sneakers and the car payment was due on Friday and the student loan payment was on the fifteenth. Six hundred eighty dollars to Navient left her with exactly enough for groceries and gas and nothing else, no margin, no slack, no room for a question like why a man with moderate dementia could clock a tramadol overage from one glance into a paper cup. If she wrote it up, she'd be writing her own name under it too: the extra half-tab, the caught-before-administration error, the fact that she'd fixed the dose and kept moving.

She told herself it was sundowning, or a lucid interval, or one of those ugly little flares a deteriorating brain sometimes threw. She told herself that by three o'clock there'd be another count and no room left for this. But the sentence stayed in the room with her, exact as a label: *That's two-fifty milligrams. Tramadol's two hundred. You put in an extra half-tab.*

She didn't believe herself, but she slept anyway.


# Chapter 2

The oxycodone was short before Carmen opened the cabinet.

She'd known it the way she sometimes knew a patient had spiked a fever before taking their temp — something in the air of the medication room, in the stillness of the count log left open on the clipboard where Denise, who covered the 7-to-3, had signed her end-of-shift count in handwriting so loose it looked like she'd done it while walking. Carmen uncapped her pen, ran her finger down the column for Schedule II narcotics, and started counting.

Oxycodone 5mg: the log said sixty-two tablets. Carmen counted the blister packs twice. Fifty-nine.

She counted again, slower, peeling back the foil edges to make sure she wasn't doubling a punch-through. Fifty-nine. She wrote the number on a sticky note and moved to the alprazolam.

The alprazolam was five short.

The medication room was a closet off the central station, barely wide enough for one person and the locked cabinet. It smelled like alcohol wipes and the faint chemical sweetness of pill dust that collected in the cabinet's corners no matter how often they wiped it down. Carmen's scrub top stuck to the small of her back. The AC had been cycling on and off all week — Cliff had put in a maintenance request, which meant nothing would happen until after Labor Day.

She wrote the alprazolam number on the sticky note beneath the oxycodone number. Three short, five short. She checked the tramadol and the lorazepam. Both matched.

She found Pat Sorvino at the nurse's station, eating string cheese and scrolling through something on her phone with her free hand. Pat was the charge nurse tonight, an RN who'd been at Lakeview eleven years and had developed the specific equanimity of someone who'd seen enough medication discrepancies to classify them by probable cause before anyone finished the sentence. She wore compression stockings under her scrubs for the varicose veins she blamed on twelve-hour shifts and two pregnancies, and she kept a tube of Biofreeze in the pocket of her scrub jacket that she applied to her lower back every two hours with the regularity of a med pass.

"Count's off," Carmen said. "Three oxy, five alprazolam."

Pat looked up from her phone. She took a bite of string cheese and chewed it slowly, and Carmen could see her running the mental calculation — how much of a problem this was, who it was going to land on, whether it was worth the paperwork.

"Did you count the returns?"

"There aren't any returns logged since Tuesday."

"Hm." Pat set her phone down. "Was Denise's count off when she signed out?"

"Her count matched. It's my count that doesn't."

"Yeah, that's what I mean. It's probably a charting error. Denise isn't — you know how it is. The overnight girls don't always recount. She might've carried Brenda's number forward."

"That's eight pills, Pat."

"I know what it is." Pat pulled a strand of cheese apart. "Document it. I'll flag it for the DON in the morning. Put it in the incident log, write up the discrepancy form, and make sure you note that the prior shift count was unverified."

"It's going under my name."

"It goes under whoever found it, which is you, which is also who's on the floor right now, which is also you." Pat wasn't being cruel. She was stating the architecture of accountability as she understood it. "Look, I'm not saying don't worry. I'm saying this happens. Two, three times a year we get a count that's off and it's always a charting issue or a pharmacy delivery that didn't get logged. Document it right, and if it comes back, it comes back with your documentation showing you caught it."

Carmen went back to the medication room and filled out the controlled substance discrepancy form. The form asked for the dispensing nurse's name and license number. She wrote her name and her LPN license number, the one she'd memorized the day she got it, the number that was her. If anyone ever pulled this form — a surveyor, an auditor, the Board of Nursing — the first thing they'd see was Carmen Reyes, LPN, and a count that didn't match.

She filed the form in the red folder at the back of the binder, behind the tabs for incident reports and fall assessments, where discrepancy forms accumulated like parking tickets nobody planned to pay.

---

The evening med pass was the longest part of her shift. Forty-seven residents on Wing A, each with a medication administration record specifying exactly what they took, when, and how. Carmen loaded the cart at 5:00, started at Room 100, and worked her way down the hall toward 120. Most of the residents were routine — she could do this in her sleep, and on some nights when she'd picked up a double, she nearly had. Hand the cup, watch them swallow, check the mouth, chart it, move on. The cart's left wheel squeaked, a rhythmic complaint she'd reported twice and stopped mentioning.

Lola Fernandez was in 112A. Carmen knocked, entered, and found Lola in the recliner by the window with the blanket tucked smooth over her knees, like she'd settled in expecting company. Lola smiled the way she always smiled, wide and warm, a smile that turned the entire hallway into a kitchen where someone was making coffee for you.

"Mija, you're here. Is it time already?"

"It's time, Mrs. Fernandez. Three tonight." Carmen set the paper cup on the tray table: lisinopril, sertraline, the alprazolam.

Carmen watched.

She'd watched hundreds of patients take medications. Most of them — the real ones, the ones whose cognition had thinned to lace — approached the pill cup with wariness or confusion. They picked pills up and looked at them. They put one in their mouth and forgot the others. They chewed when they should've swallowed. They tongued pills into their cheeks and Carmen would find them later, pasty and half-dissolved, on the pillow or in a tissue. Giving medications to genuinely impaired patients was messy, imprecise, and frequently required a second attempt with applesauce.

Lola picked up the cup. All three pills went into her mouth in one motion. She drank the water Carmen held out, swallowed, and opened wide before Carmen had to ask. Her tongue lifted. Her cheeks were clear. Nothing was pocketed.

It took four seconds. There was no hesitation, no fishing a tablet back with her tongue, no pause to remember the cup. The swallow was cleaner than most alert residents managed. It was too clean.

Carmen marked the MAR. Lisinopril, check. Sertraline, check. Alprazolam, check. She paused on the alprazolam.

Five short in the cabinet. Lola swallowing like she knew exactly what Carmen checked for.

"Thank you, mija," Lola said, and patted Carmen's hand. The tremor in Lola's fingers was real — Carmen could feel it against her skin, the fine irregular vibration of early Parkinson's — and the warmth in her grip was real too, and those two real things made everything else harder to question.

"You're welcome, Mrs. Fernandez. Goodnight."

Carmen rolled the cart to 108A. Agnes Chen's room. Agnes's roommate, Doris Okonkwo, who hadn't spoken in eleven months and received nutrition through a PEG tube, lay motionless in the far bed. Agnes sat on the edge of her own bed, looking at nothing.

"Mrs. Chen, I have your evening medications."

Agnes didn't respond. She didn't look at Carmen. She sat with her hands flat on her thighs, palms down, as though she were steadying something that wasn't there.

"Mrs. Chen? Your medications."

Agnes turned her head. Her wire-rimmed glasses caught the overhead fluorescent, blanking her eyes for a moment. Then she said, quietly: "Not now."

"You're refusing tonight?"

"Not now," Agnes said again, which was either confirmation of the refusal or a repetition of the phrase that was all she had. Carmen had heard both from patients. She waited. Agnes looked away.

"Okay. I'm going to chart that you refused. You can ask for them later if you change your mind."

Agnes didn't respond. Carmen marked the MAR — lisinopril refused, rosuvastatin refused, aspirin 81mg refused — and noted the refusal in the chart. Agnes had the right. Every resident had the right. But Agnes hadn't refused in the four months since Carmen started working this shift, and Carmen added the refusal to the sticky note in her head next to the short count and Lola's four-second swallow.

She finished the med pass at 7:40, twenty minutes behind because Mr. Gruber in 104 had needed to tell her about the Cincinnati Reds game and Mrs. Okafor in 118 had refused her blood pressure medication, then changed her mind, then refused again, which was completely normal for Mrs. Okafor and meant nothing except that Mrs. Okafor liked having someone to talk to for a few extra minutes.

Carmen clocked out at 11:15. In the parking lot, sitting in her Civic with the windows down because the car had been baking in the August sun for eight hours and the seats burned through her scrubs, she called the scheduling office and picked up the overnight shift. Eleven to seven. Eight more hours on top of eight.

"You sure?" the scheduler said. "That's — you just worked a full."

"I'm sure. Sixteen hours, yeah."

She needed four hundred dollars by Friday. The car payment. Her mother couldn't watch Reggie past midnight, so she called Gloria and asked — not asked, negotiated, because her mother worried and the worrying came out as conditions.

"Just tonight, Mami. He's already asleep. I won't wake him. I'll pick him up at seven-thirty."

"You work too much, Carmen."

"Yeah."

"You sound tired."

"I'm fine."

"That's what your father used to say, and then he'd fall asleep at the table."

"Mami, I have to go."

She sat in the car for another minute. The parking lot was half-empty, lit by two sodium lamps that turned everything yellow-orange. She could see Lakeview's flat roof and the HVAC units that hadn't been serviced since March. She could see the windows of Wing A, dark now except for the blue glow of televisions in two rooms. Room 110, where Mrs. Delacroix moaned through the night in a way that the staff had learned to hear as ambient. Room 116, where Earl Washington and Burt Kovacs shared a room and never seemed to interact, one barely responsive and the other genuinely somewhere else.

She went back inside.

---

Devon Watts was the overnight aide, twenty-six years old, working toward his associate's in something he changed every semester. He was a good kid who cared about the residents and was terrible at staying awake past midnight. By 12:30 he was at the nurse's station with his earbuds in and his head propped on his hand, scrolling through his phone with the decreasing coordination of someone losing a fight with sleep. By 12:45 the phone was on the desk and his chin was on his chest. Carmen didn't wake him. She'd need him if someone fell or pulled a call bell hard enough, and he slept light — she'd tested this. But she didn't need him for rounds.

The overnight shift at Lakeview was a different building than the day. The fluorescents in the hallways dimmed to half, casting a light that was less illumination than announcement — we are still here, the power is still on, this is still a place where people are being cared for. The sounds compressed: no call bells, no television chatter from the common room, no meal trays, no visitors. What was left was the building itself. The ventilation system's hum, deeper at night. The click of the thermostat that controlled the AC that didn't work. Somewhere in Wing B, a toilet running. The soft shuffle of Carmen's sneakers on linoleum that had been waxed so many times it was more wax than floor.

She did her first round at midnight. Checked the fall-risk patients: Mr. Gruber in 104, who sleepwalked and had a bed alarm that went off twice a night; Mrs. Okafor in 118, whose new blood pressure medication made her dizzy and who'd already fallen once, catching the bedrail on the way down and bruising her entire forearm a deep eggplant purple that her daughter had photographed and emailed to Cliff; Burt Kovacs in 116, whose dementia made him unpredictable at night, though tonight he was asleep in a position that looked uncomfortable — half on his side, one arm dangling, his mouth open and his breathing slow and ragged and old.

She charted vitals on the two residents who required overnight monitoring. She restocked the supply cart. She ate a granola bar from her bag and drank water from the fountain outside the medication room, the water warm and tasting faintly of copper.

At 1:10 she started her second round. Wing A stretched ahead of her, the hallway narrowing in the half-light. She passed 100, 102, 104. Usually by this hour she'd be in the Civic headed to her mother's, not still inside Lakeview. Whatever happened on Wing A after midnight usually happened without her. Tonight the car payment had bought eight extra hours, and at the far end of the hall she heard why the room sounded different: not a television, but people trying and failing to keep a conversation down to a whisper.

A voice said, sharp enough to carry, "She wrote it down."

Another voice came back immediately, lower and rougher. "Then stop saying it so loud."

Then a wet cough scraped through the quiet, followed by voices tripping over one another. It wasn't a TV left on low. It wasn't the loose, looping talk of residents sundowning. Someone made a point. Someone else snapped back.

The TV room was at the far end of Wing A, past the last rooms, around the corner from the hallway where the sub-station sat empty. Carmen's sneakers didn't squeak. She came around the corner and looked through the open doorway.

Three residents sat in the vinyl chairs around the card table. The television was off. The overhead light was off. The floor lamp had been dragged in from the corner and set close to the table. Papers were spread between them. A pen lay uncapped by one hand.

She recognized Lola's silver hair in the lamplight. She recognized Harry Zielinski in the wheelchair, sitting forward with his chin up and both hands planted on the armrests. The third person turned just enough for Carmen to catch the profile. Earl Washington.

Carmen's foot hit the doorframe. She didn't mean to. Her toe caught the aluminum strip where the carpet met the hallway linoleum, and the sound was small, a tap, barely there, but the room went quiet like she'd fired a gun.

Earl moved first. His hand came off the table, gathered the papers in one sweep, folded them once, and slid them under Harry's wheelchair cushion into the dark gap between the vinyl seat and the metal frame.

Harry dropped first. His shoulders rounded, his head went down, his face slackened, his hands loosened in his lap. Lola let her mouth fall open and her eyes go soft. Earl lowered his chin to his chest and closed his eyes.

The floor lamp hummed. Lola blinked at Carmen and said, "Is it morning, mija?" The slur in her voice wasn't the one Carmen heard after dinner or baths, when fatigue and tremor pulled her words apart. This came out slow but shaped.

"It's one in the morning, Mrs. Fernandez. Let me help you back to your room."

"Oh." Lola pushed at the chair arm as if she'd only just realized where she was. "I was looking for the bathroom. Ay, I thought I had time."

She stood, and warm urine ran down her calf and onto the tile.

The smell hit at once, sharp ammonia under the room's old-upholstery odor. Carmen caught Lola under both elbows before she could slip. The vinyl chair gleamed dark where she'd been sitting.

"Okay," Carmen said automatically. "It's okay. I've got you."

Over Lola's shoulder she saw Earl get to his feet and take the lamp by the stem. Harry's chair had already edged back from the table. The folded papers were gone under the seat cushion, and then Lola's full weight tipped into Carmen and there was no way to keep watching without letting an old woman fall in her own piss.

Carmen walked Lola to 112A with one arm around her waist and the other holding the wet hem of her nightgown off the floor. Lola apologized the whole way in a soft, embarrassed murmur that might have been real or might have been another layer of the act; Carmen couldn't tell anymore. In the bathroom she got Lola onto the toilet, peeled the soaked brief down, wiped urine from her thigh and calf with a washcloth from the sink, found a clean brief in the second drawer, and changed her. By the time she had Lola back in bed with a dry gown and the call bell tucked against her hand, Harry and Earl had had minutes, not seconds.

Lola patted Carmen's wrist. The tremor was there, real and irregular under the skin. "Good girl," she said. "Goodnight, mija."

"Goodnight, Mrs. Fernandez."

Carmen went back to the TV room. It was empty. Harry's wheelchair was gone with the papers hidden under its cushion. Earl was gone. The floor lamp was back in its corner. The card table had nothing on it except a half-empty box of tissues and a copy of People magazine from March, set square in the center as if nothing else had been there.

She stood in the room and listened to the building breathe: the ventilation hum, the toilet in Wing B, the distant murmur she'd taken for a television.

Sundowning. Her brain reached for it anyway, because it was a chartable word and chartable words made things smaller. She'd seen sundowning on this floor: people up at three in the morning insisting they had to catch a bus, arguing with a husband dead twenty years, wandering half-dressed toward an exit that stayed locked. Mr. Dietrich in 106 had once put on his shoes and tried to get to a job he'd retired from in 1998.

Sundowning meant loops, fear, the same wrong fact over and over. It was not papers on a card table. It was not an uncapped pen. It was not one resident warning another to keep his voice down and all three of them going slack before she'd taken a second step into the room.

Carmen walked back to the nurse's station. Devon was asleep. She sat in the chair next to his and looked at the count log and the red folder with the discrepancy form that had her name and license number on it and the sticky note in her pocket with two numbers — three and five — and she added them together with what she'd just seen and the total didn't make sense in any clinical framework she owned.

Three oxycodone. Five alprazolam. Lola's four-second swallow. Agnes refusing for the first time in four months. Harry correcting her tramadol count two shifts ago with the precision of a man reading a number off a page. And now this: a room full of people who were supposed to be gone, sitting up in the dark, doing something that looked like work.

She woke the station monitor and opened the overnight nursing notes. For Lola she wrote the smallest true thing she had: resident found awake in TV room at 0115, redirected to room, no distress noted. Then she checked 114A and 116A, found Harry and Earl back in bed, and put the same narrow truth in each chart: awake in common area during rounds, in room on recheck, no injury, no acute complaint.

That was chartable. The part that mattered wasn't. She had not heard a single clean sentence, had no papers in her hand, no witness besides Devon asleep in the next chair with his mouth open. If she called Pat at one in the morning and said three residents who spent all day in some version of confusion had been holding a meeting in the dark, Pat would hear the count discrepancy first and the story second. Eight missing controlled pills under Carmen's license, and now a report she could not defend beyond I know what I saw. That was how you got yourself treated like the problem before you had anything solid enough to prove there was one.

So she didn't file an incident report. She didn't page the DON. She left the minimal notes in the chart because they were true, and kept the rest in her head, where it felt less defensible and somehow more dangerous. A theory was useless. Proof was what counted. She sat at the nurse's station with Devon breathing beside her and the dark hall in front of her, and listened for wheels on linoleum.


# Chapter 3

Devon's podcast was about serial killers. Harry could hear it from his room — thirty feet down the hall, a tinny voice describing some crawl space in Ohio, and beneath it Devon's breathing, slow and even. He was asleep by quarter past eleven most nights, which was close enough to dependable.

Harry checked his watch. Timex, steel band, the one Jack had given him for his eightieth birthday. Eleven thirty-two. Close enough.

He wheeled himself into the hallway. The overhead fluorescents were dimmed to half for the night shift, buzzing at a frequency that had bothered him for three months when he'd first arrived and now just sounded like the building breathing. Room 110 was making the sound again, the low continuous moan that never quite rose to a volume anyone could justify investigating. Harry rolled past. The linoleum had a buckle outside Room 112 that caught his left wheel every time, and he corrected for it without thinking, the way a driver steers around a pothole on a road he drives twice a day.

The nurse's station was dark except for the monitor glow. Devon was in the rolling chair, head tilted back, mouth open, arms folded across the Lakeview polo they made him wear. Twenty-six years old, night shift three times a week, phone face-down on the medication cart playing true crime at a volume that would wake no one. He was paid fourteen-fifty an hour to watch thirty-two residents and he spent most of that time asleep. Harry had done the math on Devon's value to the facility: at current staffing levels, even an unconscious body in the chair met minimum regulatory requirements for overnight aide coverage. The system didn't need Devon alert. It needed Devon present. Devon, to his credit, was reliably present.

Harry wheeled past, watching Devon's chest rise and fall. He wouldn't surface until three, when the podcast ended and his phone alarm went off for a single wellness check before dawn shift arrived. Harry knew this because he'd watched Devon for four months before he trusted the pattern. Four months was a reasonable sample size.

The TV room was at the far end of Wing A, past the supply closet and the handicapped bathroom, as far from the station as the building allowed. During the day it smelled like microwave popcorn and the vinyl cleaner the CNAs sprayed on the chair seats. At night it smelled like the rest of Lakeview: disinfectant and warm skin and the faint chemical sweetness of urine that no amount of mopping ever fully extracted from institutional carpet. The window unit hummed and rattled and pushed air that was cooler than the hallway but not by much. Late August in central New Jersey, and Lakeview's HVAC budget was roughly what Harry would have spent on a mid-tier filing system in 1994.

He wheeled in, checked the hallway behind him — habit, not paranoia — and stood up.

His knees cracked, both of them, left then right. He pressed his palms against his lower back and stretched until something deep in his spine shifted and the pressure released. He was eighty-four and everything hurt in the mornings and most of it hurt at night, but his legs worked. His legs had always worked. The wheelchair was overhead — the cost of running the operation, a prop he'd spent six hundred hours in over the past eighteen months — and the numbness that spread through his left thigh every time he stood was the price of sitting in a chair his body didn't need.

He walked to the card table, pulled the chain on the floor lamp Agnes preferred because the overhead fluorescents gave her headaches, and set up the folding chairs. His faded blue cardigan hung on the back of his wheelchair where he kept it, and he pulled it on because the window unit made the room colder than the hallway and because he was eighty-four and his body didn't hold heat anymore. He positioned the wheelchair at the table's head — he'd sit in it for the meeting, same as always, because the height was right for the work and because standing for two hours was a cost his knees wouldn't cover.

Agnes arrived first. She always arrived first.

She came in without greeting, carrying the pillbox organizer in one hand and two notebooks in the other — the blue one for inventory, the black one for buyer accounts. The bent arm on her glasses kept them sliding low on her nose, and she moved through the room with the authority of forty-two years at the Walgreens in Edison, as if everything in it was slightly misplaced and she was the only person who knew where it should go.

She sat down. She opened the blue notebook. She uncapped a pen.

"You are four minutes late."

"I'm right on time. You're early."

"That is the same thing." She arranged the pillbox organizer in front of her and began opening compartments, her precise hands setting each small lid against the table with a hard plastic click a shade louder than the work required.

Lola appeared in the doorway, moving quietly for a woman of seventy-nine with a tremor. She'd come from 112, the room with the buckled linoleum outside it, and she was already in position mode: shoulders squared, eyes on the hall, the warm confusion dropped like a coat she'd hung by the door. In the daytime, Lola was everyone's favorite resident — the one who called you mija and told you a story about her husband that circled back on itself three times before it landed somewhere gentle and unexpectedly sharp. At night she was the best lookout Harry had ever worked with. Different business, same value in a person who noticed everything and looked harmless doing it.

She took her post by the door frame, leaned against it, and held her crucifix. Her hand trembled — it always trembled, and the Parkinson's medication managed the large movements but not the fine ones, so the gold chain shivered against her nightgown in a way that looked like devoted prayer and was actually a neurological condition being put to practical use.

"Coffee?" she said.

"After," Harry said.

"It was your turn to bring it."

"I brought it last week."

"You didn't. Earl brought it because you forgot."

"I didn't forget. I prioritized."

"Ay, prioritized." Lola shook her head. "You prioritize like my husband prioritized the yard. Never."

Earl came in pushing Burt's wheelchair. He didn't announce himself. He moved straight for the table.

Burt was asleep. Mostly. His chin rested on his chest and his fingers — musician's fingers, Agnes called them — twitched against the wheelchair armrests in some rhythm only Burt could hear. He might have been dreaming about a piano. He might have been dreaming about nothing. Burt Kovacs was eighty-eight and his dementia was the real thing. The rest of them put on confusion for staff. Burt sank into his and came back when he could, and Harry kept that cost on the books.

Earl parked Burt near the table, pulled the canvas bag from the back of the wheelchair, and sat down. He nodded at Harry. The nod covered hello, status, and anything else that needed saying. Earl talked when talk was needed. Otherwise he sat there with the kind of silence that made everybody else sound wasteful.

"How's he doing?" Harry asked. He meant Burt.

"Same."

Earl reached into the canvas bag and set a hardcover book on the card table — Burt's Hungarian-English dictionary, the big one with the cracked spine and the wine stain on the front cover. Burt's wife Erzsébet had given it to him in 1986, or 1987, Harry could never remember which, and Burt still reached for it on his nightstand some nights, running his thumb along the binding the way you'd touch a hand you hadn't held in years. He hadn't opened it to read in a long time. He didn't need to. Agnes had hollowed the center block of pages with a razor blade five months ago and sealed the cavity with packing tape, and the dictionary now held sixty-eight tablets in four pharmacy-grade bags — reserve stock, the buffer that kept their supply chain from running dry between cycles.

Agnes reached across and opened the dictionary without ceremony. She began transferring the bags to her sorting station, arranging them by type along the edge of her blue notebook. Harry watched her hands — precise, economical, the left one slightly slower from the stroke residual but compensating with efficiency. Agnes did not waste gestures.

"Inventory," Harry said.

Agnes didn't look up. "Eighty-two oxycodone, five milligram. Mostly Burt's breakthrough stock. On patch nights he does not use the full order, and if he asks for it, he gets it. One hundred twenty alprazolam, one milligram. Lola's when Lola can spare it, plus the B Wing bedtime refusals the nurses chart before they bother to watch anybody swallow. Forty-five tramadol, fifty milligram. Your card, two rehab leftovers nobody sent back to pharmacy, and fifteen pulled because they expire on the twenty-ninth." She turned the blue notebook so Harry could see the columns — numbers in handwriting so clean it looked typeset. "Non-controlled: metformin, forty count, five hundred milligram. Lisinopril, thirty, ten milligram. Rosuvastatin, twenty, five milligram. The metformin is from Dr. Prasad's most recent round of renewals. She increased Burt's dosage, which means the surplus margin is wider this month."

"Street value on the controlled?"

"Approximately three thousand two hundred, at market rates. At our pricing — with the adjustments for Rosa and the standing discount for Janine — twenty-three hundred, give or take."

"Give or take."

"Give or take forty dollars. I would need to confirm whether Janine wants the full order this month." She uncapped a second pen, red, and made a notation in the margin. The red pen was for corrections and adjustments. The black pen was for confirmed data. Agnes ran two pens the way other pharmacists ran two registers — one for the real count and one for the questions.

Earl had opened his envelope and was spreading cash across his end of the table: fives, tens, twenties, a few fifties. He sorted bills into stacks with the ease of a man who'd counted union dues for three decades. His hands moved through the money the way Agnes's moved through pills — muscle memory outlasting the context that had made it.

"Last month," Earl said. "Three thousand eight hundred forty."

Harry noted it in his own notebook — pocket-sized, pencil, because pencil could be erased and Harry had learned in forty years of accounting that permanence was a liability. That was four hundred off the month before. The gap bothered him, even though he knew the reason.
He slid the notebook into his breast pocket and, with the same motion, checked the hard little rectangle sitting in the lower cardigan pocket. Harry trusted backups more than anybody else's memory.

"Down because Derek missed his last pickup," Earl said. "Marcus says Derek's insurance kicked back in. Something temporary, through his wife's job."

"Good for Derek." Harry marked four hundred off the month. If insurance had covered Derek for a few weeks, that was four hundred dollars they didn't have to bill him. He moved to the next line.

Earl coughed. It came from somewhere deep in his chest — a wet sound that rattled up through his throat and ended in a wheeze that made Lola turn from the doorway. Harry looked too. The cough had a weight to it, a thickness that was different from a throat-clearing or a cold. He'd been hearing it for a few weeks — maybe six, maybe eight, he hadn't been counting precisely, which was unusual for him and which he noted now as its own data point.

"You all right?" Harry said.

Earl cleared his throat and waved the question away with one hand. He went back to sorting bills.

Harry watched him for another second, then let it go. Earl's dismissals were non-negotiable, and pushing past them wasn't a move that worked with a man who'd spent his career deciding what deserved a response and what didn't. Harry filed the cough. He filed everything.

"Envelopes," he said.

Earl began the separation. Twelve hundred for Marcus's thirty percent — the distribution fee, the cost of having legs that could leave the building. That went into the pickup envelope Marcus would collect during his next visit as Lola's nephew. Four hundred for Earl, which he'd get to Denise in Trenton by way of a story about a VA supplemental benefit that didn't exist. Three hundred eighty for Harry, which he'd send to Jack's business account through a wire transfer he'd route as a drawdown from an investment fund that also didn't exist. Jack's landscaping company was carrying fifteen thousand in startup debt and Harry was paying it down in increments small enough that Jack wouldn't ask questions. Agnes's share — four hundred — went into a plain white envelope she'd take back to her room and slide under the mattress, where it would join the other envelopes in a stack she never counted. She had no one to send it to. She had Helen, who had sold her house and put her here, and Helen didn't need money — Helen needed to be forgiven, and Agnes was not extending that service. Lola's share went to a church in Miami that ran a free clinic and didn't ask where donations came from. Burt's paid for things Medicare wouldn't: the hearing aid that actually fit his ear canal, the fentanyl patch copays when Part D dropped into the coverage gap, the lidocaine patches that kept his lower back from locking up by dinner.

"The lisinopril margins are thinning," Agnes said, still sorting. She held a tablet up to the lamp and read the imprint through her glasses. "The generic price at pharmacy level has dropped again. We should consider replacing it in the lineup with something that has a wider spread. Losartan, possibly. I will check the numbers."

"Check them," Harry said.

"I said I will." She set the tablet down with pointed precision.

Harry let the silence end the exchange. Agnes didn't need prompting and she didn't tolerate it. He turned to the cash on the table and continued recording the distribution in his notebook — small, careful pencil marks, the numbers squared and legible, each entry dated, each amount verified against Earl's count. He'd done this kind of work for forty years in offices with fluorescent lighting not so different from this, except the offices had been in Bergen County and the clients had been small businesses with tax problems and the only criminal liability had been the occasional undeclared cash payment, which was a misdemeanor, not a federal charge carrying a mandatory minimum.

Burt stirred. His eyes opened halfway and drifted across the card table and the people sitting around it without quite finding them. He blinked. His mouth worked for a moment before the words arrived.

"The Blue Note," he said.

Nobody responded. Harry was writing. Agnes was sorting. Earl was stacking envelopes. Lola glanced over from the door but held her post.

"Nineteen sixty-four," Burt said. The words came out clean. "Bill Evans was on the bill. I wasn't. I was hauling gear for Billy Martello — his bass amp, the big Ampeg with the blown speaker that buzzed on the low notes. Billy gave me twenty dollars and two joints for the night, and I sold both joints to a white kid from Connecticut in the men's room for fifteen dollars each."

"You told us," Earl said. There was nothing unkind in it. He stated facts once, evenly, and left them there.

"Thirty dollars for two joints in 1964," Burt said. "You know what that's worth now?"

"Two hundred eighty-seven dollars," Agnes said without looking up. "Approximately. I checked the last time you told this story."

Burt stared at her. Something moved behind his eyes. "That's right," he said slowly. "How'd you know that?"

"Inflation calculator. I verified it."

"I told this story before?"

"Three times." Agnes set down a bag of alprazolam and looked at Burt directly, her glasses low on her nose, her expression clinical. "The details shift. The first time, the joints cost the man ten dollars each. The second time, twenty. Tonight, fifteen."

Burt considered this. Confusion crossed his face, then amusement, then something sadder that was gone almost at once. "Well," Burt said. "Maybe it was different nights."

"Maybe," Agnes said, and went back to her sorting.

Burt's eyes drifted toward the middle distance. His fingers kept their rhythm on the wheelchair arm, tapping out a song or a memory of a song or nothing at all. Harry watched him for a moment, then wrote a number in his notebook and moved on.

With Burt, Harry got thirty seconds at a time: a room name, a year, the price of two joints in 1964. Then Burt's eyes went loose again and his fingers returned to the armrest, tapping at something the rest of them couldn't hear.

"Coffee," Lola said from the doorway. It wasn't a request anymore. It was a ruling.

"Fine." Harry stood — his left knee caught, and he paused until it released — and walked to the counter at the back of the TV room, where a coffeepot sat next to a sleeve of Styrofoam cups and a canister of Folgers decaf that Lola had taken from the kitchen two weeks ago. The facility's coffee was bad. This coffee was the same brand and therefore equally bad, but it was theirs — pilfered and brewed on their schedule, in their room, and Harry had learned at eighty-four that ownership mattered more than quality. It had probably always mattered more than quality. He just hadn't noticed when he'd had both.

He filled the pot from the water cooler and turned it on. The machine was older than Devon and sounded like it. The gurgling was loud enough that Harry paused and listened — Devon's podcast still droning, no change in the hallway, the building settling into its late-night sounds.

"Harry." Lola's voice, low and pointed.

He turned.

"Your socks."

"What about my socks."

"You're not wearing them."

He looked down. She was right. He'd forgotten the compression socks again, and his ankles were swollen enough that the skin puffed over the elastic band of his slipper socks. Dr. Prasad would have called it edema. Harry had been ignoring it for weeks.

"They itch," he said.

"They work. Go put them on."

"We're in the middle of business."

"We're in the middle of coffee. You have time."

"Lola."

"Harry."

He went back to the table without putting on compression socks. Lola made a sound — not quite a word, more like a small, definitive judgment pressed through her teeth — and turned her eyes back to the hallway. The coffeepot sputtered behind them.

Agnes had finished her sorting. The bags were lined along her notebook's edge, each one positioned over the buyer code it corresponded to — letter-number combinations, never names, keyed to the account system in the black notebook. The black notebook was the one that mattered. The blue one was inventory — defensible in the abstract as a retired pharmacist's habit, an old woman who couldn't stop counting. The black one held buyer medical histories, drug interaction warnings, and dosing notes for sixteen people who needed medications they couldn't afford at pharmacy prices. If anyone found the black notebook, the operation was over and the evidence was organized well enough to convict them.

"We have a problem," Harry said.

The room tightened. Earl's hands stopped on the cash. Lola went still at the door. Agnes held her pen above the page.

"The new nurse," Harry said. "Carmen."

Agnes put her pen down. "The one who caught the alprazolam count."

"She flagged a discrepancy — eight pills short across two substances. Reported it to the charge nurse." He paused. "Charge nurse called it a documentation error. Carmen logged it anyway." At Lakeview that was how shortages survived: one nurse signed what she expected to see, the next counted the card by habit instead of tablet by tablet, and the hole kept moving dressed as clerical error. Carmen had counted the pills and watched the throats.

"But she did not move on," Agnes said.

"No." Harry opened his notebook and flipped back three pages. He'd been tracking Carmen Reyes since her second week at Lakeview — shift patterns, documentation habits, how she interacted with residents, how long she spent on medication counts versus how long the average nurse spent. Thirteen months on the job. She worked the 3-to-11, picked up doubles when she needed money, and she needed money often enough that Harry had a rough model of her financial situation that he didn't feel great about having built. Single mother, LPN working toward an RN. Good with residents — genuinely good, not the efficient-but-detached good that most of the staff managed after a year. And she was better with medications than anyone Lakeview had employed in the four years Harry had been here.

"Last week. Med pass." Harry looked at Lola. "She watched you swallow."

Lola shifted at the door. "I was—"

"You were fine. She wasn't checking the chart. She was watching your throat."

"How much attention?" Earl asked.

"Deidre was here nine months. Sixty-three pills short and she never blinked." Harry closed his notebook. "Carmen caught eight in two weeks."

"She picked up a double last Thursday," Agnes said — faster now, the words compressing. "I saw her past midnight."

"One in the morning." Harry watched their faces — Agnes gone still, Earl's expression unchanged but his hands motionless on the envelopes, Lola closing her hand over the crucifix until the tremor transferred to the chain. "She walked to the TV room. Looked in. Three of us sitting up. Talking."

"Dios mío," Lola said, very quietly.

"We performed. She backed off." He folded his hands on the table. "But she saw it."

"Sundowning," Agnes said.

Harry looked at the doorway again: Carmen in the frame, three people sitting up straight around the table, every mouth closed at once. "She saw a meeting."

"Can you handle it?" Earl said.

"I don't know yet." Harry had been thinking about Carmen Reyes for two weeks. Report, ignore, come in: those had been the tidy categories. Tidy categories were not the same thing as a plan. "She's not Deidre. She's counting on her own shifts. Nobody asked her to."

"What about the count?" Agnes said.

"The eight came from direct pulls," Harry said. "Derek missed pickup, Janine doubled, and Marcus wanted the bags full."

"That was bad arithmetic," Agnes said.

"It was temporary."

"It was theft from live cards," Agnes said. The words came faster now, facts packed tight. "The safe stock is Burt on patch nights, Lola when she can spare the alprazolam, your tramadol when you decide you can stand the hip another night, and the rehab leftovers the pharmacy forgot to reclaim. Everything else comes out of somebody's pain control or sleep and leaves a hole behind. I will not touch the fentanyl patches. I will not bag oxycodone and alprazolam together. And I will not leave Burt hurting because Marcus made promises."

"So what do we do?" Lola asked from the door. Her voice had lost the warmth of the coffee argument and the compression-sock lecture. Quieter now, stripped of stories and digressions, just the question.

"Three clean days," Harry said. "No controlled pickups. Marcus gets told we're dry."

"He'll hate that," Lola said.

"He can hate it somewhere else."

"Good," Earl said.

"On Carmen's shifts, nobody palms anything," Harry said. "If the pill goes in your mouth, it goes down. Agnes moves nothing off live cards."

"That should already be the rule."

"It is now." He turned the pencil once between his fingers, then set it flat on the table. "And I make the counts cleaner before dawn."

Agnes looked at the clock on the cable box. "Before three. Devon's alarm."

"Before three," Harry said.

"And Carmen?" Lola asked.

"If she lets three clean days go, she wanted the numbers fixed. If she keeps digging after clean cards, she's after us."

The coffeepot beeped. Harry stood and poured five cups, including one for Burt, who was dozing but would want it if he surfaced. All decaf. He added two sugars to Lola's — she liked it with milk and sugar, but the kitchen didn't stock milk in single-serve containers and nobody was going to make a trip to the walk-in refrigerator at midnight, so sugar was the concession. He brought it to her at the door. She took it without looking away from the hallway.

"Gracias," she said.

"De nada," Harry said, and his pronunciation was terrible, as always: eleven words of Spanish in sixty years, every one of them mangled. Lola smiled when he tried — genuine warmth, absolutely no respect for his vowels.

He set Agnes's on the table — black, no additions, six inches from her notebook, never closer, because Agnes would not risk a spill near her records. Earl's he placed near his hand. Earl nodded once. Harry sat back down with his own cup and the room eased into the late-meeting rhythm, the business done, the coffee turning the remaining time from an operation into something closer to a visit.

Burt opened his eyes. He looked at the Styrofoam cup in front of him with the slow recognition of a man discovering something he'd wanted without knowing he'd wanted it. He picked it up. His hands shook — that tremor was always real — and coffee tilted toward the rim but didn't spill. He drank. He set the cup down.

"Is this the Blue Note?" he said.

"It's Lakeview, Burt," Earl said. His voice was different from the efficient briefness he used during business — something more careful, pitched to guide rather than inform.

Burt looked around the table. He seemed to register each of them, or something that corresponded approximately to each of them. "I knew a girl here once," he said. "Played flute." He paused. "No. That was somewhere else."

"Drink your coffee, mijo," Lola said from the door.

Burt drank his coffee. The room sat with him while he did, and nobody said anything, and the silence was the kind that didn't need filling — the silence of people who spent their nights together in a room that smelled like vinyl and Folgers and the permanent warm sweetness of a building that held eighty lives and their smells inside its walls. Burt finished his cup and set it down with a small decisive motion, as though he'd made a judgment about the coffee or the evening or the decade, and then his eyes went elsewhere, and that was the last clear thing he said that night.

Harry checked his watch. One fifty-eight.

"We're done," he said.

Agnes repacked the bags into the dictionary and closed it. She stacked her notebooks, capped both pens — black first, then red, always in that order — and tucked everything under her arm. Earl collected the envelopes and slipped them into the canvas bag. Lola checked the hallway: a long look in both directions, her body still against the door frame, the tremor in her hand pausing as if even the Parkinson's understood what was operationally required.

"Clear," she said.

Agnes left first. She moved down the hall with the economy of a woman who had closed a pharmacy counter six thousand times and knew how to leave a space without leaving a trace. Earl went next, pushing Burt's wheelchair, the rubber tires making their small sticky sounds on the linoleum. Burt's head lolled against the headrest. Earl steered with one hand and held the canvas bag against the armrest with the other, and Harry watched them go — Earl's stooped back, his large hands steady on the chair, his roommate asleep in front of him — and thought that whatever Earl Washington's reasons were for doing this, they were not Harry's reasons, and they were probably better ones.

Lola waited for them to clear the hallway, then went. Her slippers whispered on the floor. One hand traced the wall for balance — the medication helped with the big movements, the walking and the standing, but the small adjustments, the corrections that kept her from listing left, those she managed with her fingertips on the wainscoting. She disappeared around the corner toward 112 and didn't look back.

Harry was the last to leave. He always was. He turned off the lamp, rinsed the coffeepot in the small sink behind the counter, dropped the cups in the trash, wiped the card table with a napkin from the stack by the microwave, and pushed the chairs in until they lined up. He stood alone in the dark TV room and listened: Devon's podcast droning its murderous details, the ventilation rattling in the walls, Burt's wheelchair receding down the hall, and beneath everything the deep institutional quiet of a building where most of the people inside it were dying at various speeds and the rest were being paid not enough to notice.

He sat down in his wheelchair. The adjustment was physical and deliberate — a loosening of his spine, a drop of his shoulders, a slackening around his eyes and jaw that he'd practiced in front of the bathroom mirror until he could hold it for hours. He let his hands go soft on the armrests. He let his mouth hang slightly open, the lower lip loose, the expression of a man who was looking at something that wasn't in the room. He became Harold Zielinski, Room 114A, dementia patient, the one who asked about his dead wife Eleanor.

He wheeled himself into the hallway. The fluorescents hummed. Room 110 was still moaning. Devon was still unconscious. The building was exactly as he'd left it two hours ago, and no one awake in it knew that five of its residents had just conducted a business meeting that moved thirty-eight hundred dollars through a distribution network that none of them could have imagined existing in a place that smelled like this and looked like this and paid its night staff fourteen-fifty an hour to sleep through it.

Harry stopped at his door — 114A, the single room, a privilege earned by seniority and the administrative assumption that he needed quiet for his condition. He wheeled in. He transferred to the bed, the movement efficient even though no one was watching and the performance was for empty walls. He pulled the thin blanket to his chest. The window unit rattled. The room was warm and close, the air thick with late August and the stale air of a space one person lived in and never left.

Carmen Reyes sat in his head like a number that refused to reconcile. A report would have brought supervisors, forms, a round of disbelief, the confusion defense. Carmen would work quieter than that. She could start recounting on her own, stay through med pass and watch Lola swallow every pill, ask why Burt came up short on oxycodone before his refill date, close one small gap after another until the extra tablets vanished from the margins.

Harry opened his eyes. By the time anyone upstairs understood what she was looking at, the cards could be clean and the cash already gone.

Outside his window, the parking lot was empty except for Devon's Civic and the overnight nurse's minivan. The streetlight at the lot's edge cast a yellow circle on the asphalt. He had just under an hour before Devon's alarm and less than that before Carmen started turning the night into paperwork.

Waiting had sounded reasonable at the table. In bed it looked like the sort of arithmetic people defended right up until the audit.

Harry sat up. The mattress springs complained. Three clean days required cleaner numbers than the ones they'd left on the books.

He pulled on the cardigan, took the bath towel from the chair, and went back into the hall.


# Chapter 4

The charting system at Lakeview was a hybrid — half-digital, half paper — that nobody had fully committed to in either direction. Carmen sat at the Wing A sub-station at 2:37 AM, entering vital signs from her 1 AM rounds into the computer while the paper backup waited on a clipboard beside her. The terminal was old enough that it hummed. The screen cast a blue-white wash over her hands and the laminate counter, which was peeling at the corner where someone had picked at it during hundreds of similar overnight hours.

She'd taken the double for the same reason she always did. The car insurance was due in nine days and she was $140 short. The overnight differential was $3.50 more per hour, not enough by itself to close a $140 hole. The whole double would do it and still leave gas money if she didn't eat out. She hadn't eaten since the turkey sandwich from the vending machine at 10 PM, and she could feel the deficit in her concentration, a lag between noticing something on the screen and understanding what it said.

The building made its night sounds around her. The HVAC cycled on with a low shudder she felt in the counter surface before she heard it.
