Room 110 — Mrs. Delacroix — produced the same low, continuous murmur she'd been making for the seven months Carmen had worked this wing, a sound Carmen had stopped hearing the way you stop hearing a refrigerator. A toilet flushed somewhere in B Wing, probably Devon using the staff bathroom, which meant he was at least awake, which was an improvement over last Tuesday when she'd found him face-down on his folded arms at the nurse's station with a podcast about cryptocurrency playing through one earbud.

Carmen finished the vitals entry for Room 112A — Lola Fernandez, blood pressure 128 over 76, pulse 72, temp 97.8 — and tabbed to the next field. Her eyes were dry. The building's air was always dry, but at night the dryness concentrated, and by 3 AM every shift her contact lenses felt like they'd been sitting on a dashboard in July.

She'd been thinking about the pill count. It kept surfacing with a splinter's timing: absent until she moved wrong, then suddenly all she could feel. Three oxycodone, five alprazolam. The charge nurse's dismissal. Lola's mouth opening for the pill check with the smoothness of someone who'd practiced the motion. The voices in the TV room — four, five people talking in complete, coherent sentences at one in the morning, and then silence and slack faces the moment she appeared in the doorway.

She couldn't make the pieces add up to anything she was ready to name. The count was wrong, and the patients were wrong, and those two facts pointed in a direction she didn't want to follow because following it meant paperwork and phone calls and an investigation that would land on her shifts, her counts, her license number on the dispensing log.

She clicked save on the vitals screen and stretched her neck. The sub-station sat at the elbow of Wing A's L-shape, which meant she could see down the short leg of the hall — six rooms, all doors closed except 118, whose occupant, Mr. Petrelli, insisted on sleeping with his door open because he'd been a fireman and believed in egress — but the long leg, where the sicker residents and the singles were, angled out of sight past the linen closet.

Movement registered in her peripheral vision before she consciously looked. A shape in the long hallway, just past the angle.

She turned her head.

Harry Zielinski was walking.

Everything familiar about Harry's gait was gone: the shuffle she'd guided through a hundred med passes, the arthritic list, the wall-seeking hands she'd documented in his mobility assessment three months ago when she marked him as requiring assistance for ambulation.

He was walking. Upright. His spine was straight, or as straight as eighty-four years would allow — a natural curve instead of a hunch. His feet moved with deliberate spacing, each step placed and lifted, heel to ball, like a man who trusted his knees. His cardigan hung open over his pajama top and swung lightly with each step. In his right hand he carried something wrapped in a white bathroom towel, pinned close to his thigh and meant to disappear.

He was thirty feet away. The hallway was at half-light — every other fluorescent panel was on, the facility's overnight energy-saving measure that turned the corridors into alternating bands of pale light and weak shadow. He moved through them steadily.

Carmen's hands were on the keyboard. She didn't move them. She watched with the full attention she gave a patient whose numbers had slipped out of range. Alarm hadn't caught up yet.

Harry reached the intersection where the long hallway met the short one, where the sub-station was, where she was sitting in the blue-white light of the monitor with her name badge clipped to scrubs she'd been wearing for twelve hours, and he looked up and saw her.

He stopped.

They looked at each other across the linoleum.

It lasted three seconds. In the first second, Harry's face was open — unguarded, the face of a man who'd been alone in a hallway doing what he needed to do, and the expression on it was ordinary irritation at being interrupted, the look you'd give someone who walked in while you were on the phone. In the second second, she saw him recognize what she was seeing and what it meant. His eyes, which she'd spent a year documenting as confused and intermittently oriented, were clear and sharp, moving over her face, her badge, her hands — taking stock, making calculations, reaching conclusions. In the third second, his jaw set, and something crossed his face that she would later decide was resignation.

Then he put his left hand on the wall. His weight shifted. His shoulders dropped forward into the familiar hunch, and his knees bent slightly, and his right foot turned inward, and she watched him rebuild the performance from the ground up, piece by piece, like an actor stepping back into costume — the posture first, then the grip loosening on the towel-wrapped object, then the face going slack, the eyes losing focus, the lower lip dropping.

"Eleanor?" he said. His voice was cracked and querulous, the voice she heard every shift. "Is that you? Eleanor, I was looking for — where did you — the bathroom. I was trying to find the bathroom."

Carmen stood up. Her badge swung against her chest and then settled.

"It's Carmen, Mr. Zielinski." Her resident voice came out — steady, clear, warm enough. "Let me help you back to bed."

She crossed the thirty feet between them. The linoleum bit cold through her sneakers; the HVAC always dropped at night. His breathing was even. He wasn't winded or confused. He held it a little shallow, like he was bracing for what she might do next.

She took his left arm. The forearm was thin — bone easy to feel through skin and pajama cotton — but the muscle under it was live and ready. At the same moment his right hand tightened around the towel-wrapped object against his thigh. The terry cloth puckered under his fingers.

For a second she thought about reaching for it. Peeling the towel back. Calling Devon over and making him look. But then she saw the whole stupid scene as it would happen: an aide half asleep, an old man folding toward the floor, her own voice getting louder while Harry stared past her and asked for Eleanor. Whatever was in the towel would still be whatever she said it was.

"Here we go, Mr. Zielinski. Back to bed."

That choice seemed to register. The weight he gave her was exact — enough dependence to occupy both her hands, not enough to actually need her strength. He was making her hold the act with him.

"Eleanor makes the bed," he said, mumbling. "She always makes the bed. I can't — the corners. She does the corners."

She walked him into Room 114A and guided him to the bed. Room 114A smelled like him — warm, stale skin, the sweet-sour note of medications processed through old kidneys, the faint ammonia ghost that never fully left any room where someone lived in a diaper. He sat on the edge, and when she lifted his legs onto the mattress he went limp a beat too neatly, controlled even in surrender.

The towel disappeared under the blanket with him. By the time she pulled the covers to his chest, his hand was already resting on it, and his knuckles were white.

"Good night, Mr. Zielinski."

He'd closed his eyes. His face was slack.

She stood in his doorway for a moment. The hallway was empty and half-lit and silent except for Mrs. Delacroix's distant murmur and the HVAC cycling down to its lowest register, a sound like the building exhaling.

She looked at the man in the bed and understood that the three seconds in the hallway had not ended in the hallway. He had seen her see him, then walked her all the way back here and let her decide whether to make this a scene with nothing in her hands. The towel was under the blanket because she'd let it get there. The night three shifts ago when she'd heard clear voices in the TV room. The pill count. The tramadol correction — two-fifty, not two hundred, said with the quick certainty of someone who'd been paying attention. Every separate anomaly she'd been collecting clicked into the same shape.

She went back to the sub-station and sat down. The vitals screen had gone to sleep. Her reflection stared at her from the dark monitor — tired eyes, hair escaping the ponytail, the particular gray look that twelve hours of fluorescent light put on brown skin.

She sat there for eleven minutes. She knew it was eleven minutes because when she looked at the computer clock it said 2:52, and when she stood up it said 3:03. She wasn't blank. She was putting the order together: Harry first, then the cabinet, then whatever came after.

---

The medication room was off the central nurse's station, between the two wings. It was the size of a large closet — one door, no window, a locked cabinet bolted to the wall, a counter with a sink, and a plastic chair nobody sat in because there was barely room for it. The room smelled like rubbing alcohol and the fine, dry dust that came off pill bottles when you handled hundreds of them.

Carmen locked the door behind her, which was protocol for controlled substance counts. She pulled the keys from her hip — the medication key was the small brass one on the ring with seven others, and by now she could find it by touch alone.

She opened the cabinet. The controlled substances were on the top two shelves, each in its own labeled container, each with a count sheet clipped to the front. The count sheets were simple: date, shift, count, initials. Two signatures per shift — outgoing nurse and incoming nurse, verifying the same number. Carmen's initials were on most of the evening entries. The overnight nurse's initials — Maria or Dolores or whoever they'd pulled from the float pool that week — were on the rest.

She started with the oxycodone. Five milligram tablets, prescribed to four residents on Wing A and two on Wing B. She removed the container, opened it, and counted. She counted slowly, touching each tablet with the tip of a tongue depressor as she'd been taught in pharmacology, pushing each pill from one side of the tray to the other. Eighty-six tablets should have been in this container according to the last verified count plus pharmacy deliveries minus dispensed doses.

She counted sixty-seven. She counted again. Sixty-seven.

Nineteen short.

She wrote the number on the back of a count sheet and moved to the alprazolam. One milligram tablets, prescribed to three residents. Expected count: one hundred forty-four. Actual count: one hundred twenty-eight.

Sixteen short.

The tramadol was next. Fifty milligram capsules, four residents. Expected: eighty-eight. Actual: eighty.

Eight short.

She opened the hydrocodone container. Ten milligram tablets, two residents. Expected: thirty-six. Actual: thirty-two.

Four short.

She stood at the counter with the numbers on the back of the count sheet. Nineteen, sixteen, eight, four. Forty-seven pills.

The ventilation hummed above her. She could hear, faintly through the closed door, the rise and fall of Mrs. Delacroix's murmur and the building settling into its own weight, a ceiling creak every few minutes, then a soft tick in the ductwork.

Forty-seven pills across four controlled substances. Not concentrated in one medication, which made a single patient's hidden stash harder to believe. It suggested movement. More than that, she was still inferring.

She checked the count sheets again. Her own initials appeared at the start of each evening shift, and at the end, the numbers matched — she'd counted correctly every time. Which meant the pills were leaving between her departure and the start of the next verified count. Overnight. When Devon slept through his shifts and the hallways belonged to whoever was awake in them.

She thought about Harry's walk. The posture, the stride, the clear eyes. She thought about the rolled towel pressed against his leg. If the towel held pills, that would be proof. She hadn't seen inside it.

The tramadol correction from her first week surfaced. Two-fifty, not two hundred. The exact number didn't prove he was keeping inventory. It proved he knew the counts better than he should have. Combined with the missing pills and the walk, it pointed at him hard enough that looking elsewhere would have been a lie.

Forty-seven pills. Dispensed under her license. Missing from her shifts.

She put the containers back in the cabinet. She locked it. She folded the count sheet and put it in her scrub pocket, then took it out, looked at the four numbers she'd written, folded it again, and put it back.

She unlocked the medication room door and stepped into the nurse's station. Devon's phone was propped against the monitor now, the screen showing a man in a garage talking about engine rebuilds. Devon was tipped back in the office chair with his mouth slightly open, not quite asleep but not registering her either.

Carmen walked past him. She went to the supply closet, got a fresh set of vitals equipment she didn't need, and carried it back to the Wing A sub-station, where she sat down and set it on the counter beside her. If Devon looked up, he'd see routine. If Harry noticed anything at all, it would look like routine too.

One call to the supervisor would turn routine into a discrepancy form with Carmen's initials at the top. She needed another nurse's hands on the cabinet before that happened.

She didn't do it at three in the morning. Not with forty-seven pills missing under her initials and nothing that tied them to Harry except a hallway, a towel, and her word that an eighty-four-year-old resident had walked thirty feet like he belonged there. By the time the supervisor got here, Harry would be back in bed asking for Eleanor. Devon would say he hadn't seen anything. The log would still point first at Carmen.

So she sat on it until dawn and let the night keep happening around her. At four she made her scheduled rounds. The rounds gave her a reason to keep moving through Wing A without announcing that she was watching it. Most of the wing was asleep. Mrs. Delacroix's murmur leaked out of Room 110. Mr. Petrelli snored through his open door with one foot hanging off the mattress, and Carmen tucked it back under the sheet without waking him. In Room 112A, Lola Fernandez was curled on her side with one hand under her cheek and the other wrapped around her gold crucifix, and she looked like someone's grandmother, which she was.

In Room 114A, Harry Zielinski was on his back with the blanket pulled to his chin and his eyes closed. No towel in sight. He looked like a frail old man dreaming about his dead wife, and Carmen stood in his doorway and watched his chest rise and fall and knew he was awake and knew he knew she was there and knew the performance was still the only thing either of them could afford to do in the dark.

She finished her rounds and went back to the sub-station. She charted, answered two call bells, helped Devon turn a patient at five, and drank the coffee she'd brought in a thermos that was now cold and tasted like the inside of the thermos. The folded count sheet stayed in her pocket until the sky at the end of Wing A went from gray to a weak pink and the first breakfast cart rattled out of the kitchen.

The day nurse, Melanie Russo, came in at 6:53 with wet hair at the nape of her neck and a Dunkin' cup sweating in one hand. She set the cup down, looked at Carmen's face, and didn't bother with good morning.

"What happened?"

"I need a narc count before you touch anything."

That got her moving. Carmen took her into the medication room, shut the door, opened the cabinet, and slid the count sheets into the light. She read off the expected numbers while Melanie counted with the tongue depressor, pushing each pill into a neat line, lips moving a little as she went.

"Sixty-seven," Melanie said.

"Count it again."

Melanie counted again. "Still sixty-seven."

Carmen pulled the alprazolam, then the tramadol, then the hydrocodone. Melanie counted all three twice. The numbers didn't move.

"One twenty-eight. Eighty. Thirty-two." Melanie set the tongue depressor down and looked at Carmen. "Jesus. That's nineteen short on the oxy, sixteen on the Xanax, eight tramadol, four hydro."

Now someone else had said it out loud: forty-seven pills.

Melanie reached for the count sheet and wrote the actuals in hard block print. 67. 128. 80. 32. She initialed beside each line, then flattened the page with her palm like maybe the numbers would change if the paper stopped buckling.

"You already call Janice?"

"Not yet." Carmen heard how flat her own voice had gone. "I wanted the count witnessed first."

Melanie looked at her for a second too long, then back at the sheet. Through the door came the beginning of day shift: wheels, voices, somebody laughing too loud at the station, Mrs. Delacroix's low murmur still working through the wall like a motor that never shut off.

The shortage sat on the page in Melanie Russo's handwriting now. Harry could still fold himself back into the hunch and the dead-wife questions. The pills were no longer only Carmen's word.


# Chapter 5

Melanie's hard block numbers sat in Carmen's head all through the next day.

That night, Carmen waited until Devon left the station.

He did this every night around twelve-thirty — walked to the kitchen, ate whatever the dietary aide had left out, came back in twenty minutes smelling like butterscotch pudding. She'd never had a reason to track his schedule before. Tonight she had timed it on her watch, standing at the sub-station pretending to chart, marking the moment he pulled out his earbuds and shuffled toward the kitchen in his untied Nikes. Twelve thirty-one. She gave him two minutes' head start.

The hallway was dim at this hour, half the fluorescents off, the others buzzing at a frequency she could feel at the base of her skull. Wing A stretched ahead of her — a single corridor of closed doors and the faint ammonia smell that never fully left the linoleum, no matter how many times the overnight aide mopped. The AC had been struggling all week. Early September in central New Jersey and Lakeview Gardens was still holding heat like a kiln, the building's cinder-block walls radiating warmth back into the hallways hours after dark. She could feel it through the soles of her sneakers.

She carried her clipboard. She didn't need it — she wasn't charting, wasn't rounding, wasn't doing any of the things a clipboard authorized her to do at twelve-thirty in the morning. But the clipboard was habit, the same way the badge clipped to her scrub top was habit. The tools of a nurse who belonged in this hallway at this hour. That was cover.

She passed Room 110 and heard Mrs. Delacroix making her sounds — a low continuous murmur that rose and fell with her breathing, each exhale catching just enough in her throat to turn air into voice. Carmen had checked on her at eleven. She was fine, or she was the same, which was what fine meant in Room 110.

The TV room sat in the blind spot around the bend from Devon's desk. That was the point — the ring's point, she understood now, the reason they met here and not in someone's room.

The door was open. The television was off. Harry sat in his wheelchair at the card table, his back to the hallway, a newspaper folded in his lap. The only light was the green-white glow of the emergency exit sign above the far door, casting the room in a color that made everything look submerged.

Carmen stepped inside and closed the door behind her. The latch clicked.

"Mr. Zielinski." She stayed standing. "I need to talk to you."

Harry didn't move. His head was tilted forward the way it always was when he was sleeping — chin to chest, mouth slack, the posture of a man who'd lost track of the room and the hour and possibly the year. His breathing was slow, wet, heavy. A thin shine of drool on his chin.

"Harry."

She used his first name. She had never done that before — not in thirteen months of med passes and evening check-ins and gentle redirections when he wandered toward the fire exit asking for Eleanor. He was Mr. Zielinski, the way all her residents were formal names, because the formality was part of the care and Carmen did not cut corners.

He held the performance for two more seconds. She counted — one one-thousand, two one-thousand — watching for the seam, the place where the act would start to peel. She'd seen him break character once, in the hallway, and it had been fast, a light switch. But that had been accidental, a caught moment. This was something else.

Harry lifted his head. His spine straightened — not dramatically, he was eighty-four and his back didn't snap to anything, but the slouch was gone, the way a folded thing unfolds when you pick it up and shake it out. His shoulders squared against the back of the wheelchair. His chin came up. And his eyes, which she'd watched drift and search and lose focus a thousand times during med pass, locked onto her face with a precision that was almost physically uncomfortable. Like a flashlight pointed directly at her when she'd been expecting candlelight.

He wiped the drool from his chin with the back of his hand. One clean motion. Quick and economical, thumb catching the wet shine at the corner of his mouth. A man cleaning himself up because the costume was off and he didn't need the prop.

"Close the other door," he said.

His voice was different. She'd heard him coherent before — the tramadol correction, the hallway at 2:40 AM. The confused Harry spoke in fragments, rising intonations, questions that dissolved before they finished. This Harry's voice was flat and level. He finished what he said.

"It's closed," Carmen said.

"The other one." He nodded toward the far wall, where a second door led to the back hallway. She hadn't noticed it was cracked open — two inches, enough for sound to carry.

She crossed the room and pulled it shut. When she turned back, Harry had folded the newspaper and placed it on the card table. She could see the crossword from here. Completed, in ink, in handwriting that was small and square and precise — nothing like the shaky scrawl in his chart, the trembling Harold Zielinski who couldn't remember how to spell his own last name for the intake assessment.

He waited. He didn't ask why she was there. He sat in his wheelchair with his hands folded on the table, his faded blue cardigan pulled tight across his narrow chest. The room thinned out around that silence until it felt like she'd have to break it herself.

Carmen stayed standing. The clipboard edge bit into her palm. She loosened her grip before the plastic cracked.

"I know what you're doing," she said.

"Okay."

"The medications. I ran a full manual count last night — you're forty-seven pills short across four controlled substances. Oxycodone, alprazolam, tramadol, and hydrocodone." She heard herself falling into med-pass language, the clinical vocabulary that made everything procedural and clean. She stopped. "You're stealing pills. You and the others. Agnes, Lola, Earl — I've seen the meetings, Harry. The TV room, after midnight. I've seen you walk. Heel to toe, steady, at two-forty in the morning with a towel under your arm. You're clear-headed. You know exactly what you're doing. You're running something."

Harry's expression didn't change. He didn't deny, didn't flinch, didn't offer the scrambling panic she'd half-expected. His attention sharpened, but only by a hair. It wasn't surprise. It was confirmation.

"Sit down," he said.

"I don't want to sit down."

"Then stand." He pulled the vinyl chair nearest to him away from the table, angling it toward her. The metal legs scraped on the linoleum, a sound that in the daytime would have been nothing but at this hour cut through the quiet like something tearing. "But you're going to want to hear this sitting."

Carmen sat. The chair exhaled under her weight. Old vinyl, cracked along the arms, the cushion held together with facility-issue tape that had gone amber and stiff. She could feel the split upholstery through her scrubs.

"How long," she said.

"Eighteen months. Give or take."

"How many of you."

"Five. Plus distribution."

"Names."

"You know the names."

She did. She'd known since the hallway, since the TV room at 1 AM weeks ago when the animated voices had gone slack at the sight of her, since the count sheets that kept coming up short on her shifts and nobody else's. She knew. She was asking him to say it because saying it made it something she could document, respond to, process through the professional channels that were supposed to exist for moments like this.

"Why are you here, Carmen?"

"Because forty-seven pills are missing and they were dispensed under my license number."

"I know that. That's not what I asked."

He watched her. His pale eyes — she'd always thought of them as watery, unfocused, the eyes of a man looking for someone who wasn't in the room. They weren't. They were sharp and light and specific, the color of old ice.

"Why are you here. In this room. Talking to me."

Carmen pressed her palms flat on her thighs. The fabric of her scrubs was damp — the room's heat, or her own, she couldn't tell.

"Because you need to understand what you've done to —" She stopped. Started again, lower. "Every pill that went missing came through my med pass. My name on the count sheet. My license number." She leaned forward. Her voice was getting quieter, which was what happened when she was truly angry — she didn't get louder, she got smaller, more exact. "If the state board pulls my records, I am the one they see. Not you." Her hand found the badge and she turned the clip between her fingers, the sharp laminate edge biting her thumb. "You're an eighty-four-year-old man in a wheelchair. A judge looks at you and it's supervised care, it's a facility failure. Me, I lose my license. I go to prison." She swallowed. "I lose Reggie."

The room was quiet enough that she could hear the ventilation system laboring two hallways away. The AC compressor kicked on and off in cycles — she'd timed those too, during long overnight shifts with nothing to count but the intervals between mechanical sounds. Right now it was off, and the air in the TV room sat heavy and still.

Harry hadn't moved. He let her words hang there in the heat until she could feel sweat cooling under her arms.

"You done?" he said.

"Don't talk to me like this is a board meeting."

Something shifted in his face. His mouth tightened a fraction. Acknowledgment, maybe. The recognition that a number was real.

"Fair enough," he said. "You're scared. I hear that." He waited. "But you need to hear me now."

"I'm looking at a federal crime."

"You're looking at five people." He counted them on his fingers, not for drama but the way he counted everything — precisely, by the unit. "Me. Agnes. Lola. Earl. Burt. Burt's eighty-eight. He's got actual dementia — not like me, actual. Agnes is seventy-six with stroke residuals. Lola has Parkinson's. Earl's an eighty-one-year-old veteran." He stopped there, the way he'd stopped before when he'd started to say something about Earl. Whatever it was, he kept it. "We live here. We don't leave."

"That doesn't change what you did."

"It changes what happens if you report it." He pressed his hands flat on the table. She could see his fingers clearly in the exit sign's light — long, thin, steady. She'd watched those same hands tremble over a medication cup, shake while reaching for a glass of water, fumble a spoon in the dining room. They weren't shaking now. "You call the police. The state. The DEA. Pick one. What happens."

"They investigate."

"They investigate. Then they charge. Possession with intent — Schedule II, federal mandatory minimums." He let that sit. "For five nursing home residents. One of whom can't tell you what year it is."

"I know what the charges would be, Harry."

"I'm not talking about charges." He pressed his thumb against the edge of the table. "Agnes in a courtroom, a hundred and ten pounds. Lola shaking. Burt not understanding why someone put handcuffs on him."

"So your plan is — what, look pathetic?"

"We don't need a plan for that." He shifted in the wheelchair. His left knee popped — she'd heard it before, always the left, and now she wondered how long he'd been performing the wince that went with it when actually it was just a knee.

He unfolded the newspaper. Under the crossword were three business envelopes, a pharmacy notice folded into quarters, and a money-order receipt clipped to one of the envelopes with a silver paper clip bent almost straight. He slid the receipt across the table with one finger.

"Read it."

Carmen looked down.

*Denise Washington. Trenton, New Jersey. $400.00.*

The name hit her from Earl's chart before it hit anywhere else. Emergency contact. Daughter. One 609 number, no alternate.

"Earl's daughter," Harry said. "First Monday every month. He tells her it's a VA benefit because that's easier than telling her her father sells oxycodone out of a nursing home."

Carmen looked up.

"His pension pays the building and leaves him fifty dollars. Five-zero. That's the whole number. You report tonight, that envelope doesn't go out. Denise gets nothing from him this month except a call she can't use."

"You're trying to buy me with somebody else's daughter."

"No." He tapped the receipt once. "I'm telling you the damage has a name on it." He slid the receipt back under the newspaper. "Then there's the facility. Cliff's running a building where five residents moved pills for eighteen months under his nose."

"And Lakeview gets cited."

"Shut down. Maybe." He watched her. "A hundred and twelve residents who had nothing to do with this get relocated."

He let that settle.

"You know where they go," he said.

"Wherever has beds." Carmen heard herself say it flatly, without inflection, because she did know. She'd worked per diem at three other facilities before Lakeview. She knew what wherever-has-beds looked like — the places that smelled worse, where the call bells rang three times before anyone answered, where the food was Sysco institutional and the medication errors weren't careful accounting like this but genuine neglect.

"Wherever has beds," Harry repeated. "That's on you. If you report."

"That's not fair."

"No."

They sat with that. The institutional clock on the wall — round, white-faced, never accurate — read twelve-fifty-one. Carmen could hear, very faintly, Mrs. Delacroix's murmuring from down the hall. A continuous sound, almost restful if you didn't think about what it meant.

"What do you actually want?" Harry said.

She opened her mouth. She didn't know. That was the problem — she'd come in here with a fact, with forty-seven pills and a count sheet and a license number, and she'd expected the fact to tell her what to do. The fact didn't tell her anything. It just sat there: forty-seven pills, and no next step attached.

"I want it to stop," she said.

"Okay. We stop. Flush everything, shut it down." He paused. "Then what."

"The count stabilizes. I go back to charting normally."

"And the forty-seven pills already in the system?" He watched her. "Your name, your shift, your signature. The three oxy from six weeks ago, the five alprazolam the week after." He waited. "You flagged those. And moved on."

Carmen's stomach shifted. She'd been so focused forward — what else could go missing, what else could go wrong — that she hadn't looked at the trail behind her. The count sheets she'd already signed. The discrepancies she'd reported to the charge nurse and then accepted the charge nurse's dismissal of. Months of documentation that showed, at minimum, sustained failure to catch what was happening on her shifts under her license.

"Even if we stop tonight," Harry said, "you're exposed. Any audit pulls your dispensing records. Chronic shortages on your shifts, documented and signed." He paused. "That's not a charting error."

"So you're telling me I'm already screwed."

"I'm telling you that calling the police puts your license number on the first line." He watched her. The exit sign's glow made his face look carved from something pale and cold, but his eyes kept moving, catching every shift in her mouth, every breath she took too fast. "Give me a week."

"No."

"One week. Let me show you the whole picture. The buyers, their situations. Where the money goes. Who depends on it. Then you decide."

"I've already decided."

"No you haven't." His voice was quiet. His hands stayed still on the table. "You didn't go to Cliff. You didn't call the hotline."

She started to speak.

"You came here at midnight," he said. "And you shut both doors."

Carmen's hands stopped moving. Her nails were sunk into the split vinyl arm of the chair, caught there without her noticing. She pulled them free and felt the tacky drag of old tape against her fingertips.

He was right.

She had come here. Not to Cliff's office, not to the phone in the break room where the state board hotline poster hung in red block letters. She had come to the TV room. At midnight. She had closed the door behind her and sat down when he told her to. None of that belonged to the clean version of this story, the one with a report and a supervisor and somebody else taking over. She was in a warm dark room making private terms with a resident, and she could not tell anyone that without making herself part of it.

"One week," she said.

It came out rough, forced through something in her chest that didn't want to let it pass.

"One week. You show me whatever you think I need to see. Then I decide." She leaned forward. "And nothing moves in that week — no pills sorted, nothing packaged, nothing goes out. The operation stops while I'm looking at it."

Harry considered this. The pause stretched. She could almost feel him sorting inventory, timing, the people already waiting on the other end. His face didn't change.

"Okay," he said.

Carmen stood. Her scrubs peeled away from the chair's vinyl with a sound like tape pulling off skin. She picked up her clipboard from the floor where she'd dropped it — she hadn't noticed dropping it, didn't remember the moment, which bothered her because Carmen noticed things, that was the whole problem, she noticed too much.

"We had trouble with the pharmacy," Harry said. "Few years back."

He said it flat, with no heat in it at all. Carmen had her hand on the door.

"Cost savings. Half our prescriptions got delayed or substituted." He paused. "That's not the whole story. But that's where it started."

"I didn't ask how it started."

"I know," Harry said. "But you will."

Carmen pulled the door open. The hallway's half-light poured in, fluorescent and thin. The TV room's green glow stayed in her eyes for a second after she stepped out. She walked away without looking back.

---

She sat in her car in the parking lot with the engine off and the windows down.

September hadn't broken the heat yet. The air at one-fifteen was as heavy as it had been at noon, damp enough to sit on her skin. The dumpster smell reached her parking spot anyway, sweet rot under hot asphalt. Crickets worked the strip of weeds by the curb hard enough to sound mechanical.

She kept seeing his back come up straight. The crossword filled in ink. The hand that had trembled over pill cups wiping his chin clean in one pass. The bent paper clip. Denise Washington, Trenton. Four hundred dollars. Eighteen months. Five people. Forty-seven pills under her name.

Her phone buzzed. She'd forgotten she had it — she must have grabbed it from the medication room on the way out. Her mother: *You okay mija? Reggie had bad dream, he's asleep now. Don't worry. Call me tomorrow.*

The text was forty minutes old.

Carmen looked down at her hands. Crescent marks from the chair arm still striped her fingertips. Her license number sat under every shortage she'd signed.

She opened the message thread and typed, *Got stuck finishing paperwork. Kiss him for me.* She stared at *paperwork* until the word looked stupid and harmless. Then she sent it anyway.

*You didn't go to Cliff. You didn't call the hotline. You came here at midnight. And you shut both doors.*

She could still report it in the morning. Cliff came in around eight-thirty if he bothered to come in at all. The state board hotline number was on the poster by the break-room microwave, red block letters above a coffee stain shaped like Florida. She knew what the call would sound like because she'd made versions of it before — not about diversion, never that, but about pressure ulcers nobody had charted and a kitchen aide working with a fever and a med cart left unlocked during shift change. Date. Time. Nature of concern. Witnesses. Immediate risk to residents.

This one fell apart halfway through.

She had the manual count. She had the discrepancies on her shifts. She had a private conversation in a dark room with both doors shut and no witness but Harry Zielinski, who by breakfast would be back in his wheelchair asking for Eleanor. She had an addressed envelope she hadn't confiscated, a name from Earl's chart that was now attached to a number and a city. If she wanted to make the clean report, she'd have to leave Denise out of it. If she wanted to tell the whole truth, she'd have to explain why she knew about Denise in the first place. Either version made her look dirty.

She leaned back against the headrest and closed her eyes. The vinyl was hot against her scalp. Somewhere inside the building Mrs. Delacroix would still be making that low engine sound in her throat. Devon would come back from the kitchen sleepy and sugared, sit at the station, maybe put one earbud back in. Earl would be in bed or pretending to be in bed, coughing into the dark with fifty dollars to his name after Medicaid took its share. Denise would be asleep in Trenton or not asleep, either one, waiting on money she thought came from the government. Reggie was across the river with his grandmother, seven years old, having bad dreams about whatever seven-year-olds dreamed about when the grown-ups around them were tired all the time.

She opened her eyes.

The thing that made her sick was not that Harry had an argument. It was that he had paperwork. Numbers. Names. The shape of a system. Carmen knew how much damage a form could do once someone filled in the blanks. She also knew how fast a person turned into a line item when the form got far enough from the body. The forms never had a box for who could absorb the hit and who couldn't. They just had signatures.

She knew exactly how Cliff would write it if this hit his desk before it hit the state. Controlled-substance discrepancy. Education provided. Follow-up audit. Then the questions in that dry administrative voice of his: Did you secure the cart? Did you leave the cabinet open? Did you document refusals immediately? Each one neat and bloodless and aimed straight at her hands. He wouldn't write that five residents had been moving pills under overnight staffing so thin the whole wing had blind spots. He wouldn't write Denise Washington in Trenton or Burt blinking at handcuffs he couldn't understand. He'd write Carmen Reyes failed to maintain narcotic accountability on Wing A. Maybe Melanie's witness initials somewhere under it. By the end of the page, Carmen's badge would be the only thing anyone was looking at.

And one week gave her language she could hide inside. Not protecting them. Looking into it. Not helping. Verifying. Seven days sounded responsible, the kind of pause you took before escalating something serious. It sounded different from what she'd actually done, which was make a deal in a locked room and carry it out to her car. She could call it caution until morning. She could call it due diligence, patient safety, anything that would survive a form. It would still be the same week she'd handed Harry Zielinski with both doors shut.

She set the phone face-down on the passenger seat and started the car. The AC coughed hot air at her knees before it cooled. In the rearview mirror, Lakeview stayed low and lit against the dark. She didn't go back inside for the number on the poster. She drove.


# Chapter 6

The visitor badge was still warm from someone else's body when Marcus clipped it to his shirt pocket. At 2:14 PM on a Thursday, he signed the front-desk log in the same blocky capitals he'd used every week for twenty months while the girl at the desk — new, maybe twenty-two, acrylic nails clicking on her phone — didn't look up. He wrote *Lola Fernandez* in the resident column and *nephew* in the relationship column.

He knew this building the way a surgeon knows a body he's opened before. The turn past the activities room where three women sat in wheelchairs watching a cooking show at full volume, the host shouting about risotto while one woman slept and another picked at the vinyl armrest with her thumbnail. The smell shifting as he passed the kitchen — today it was some kind of fish, the institutional version where the breading was thicker than the fillet. The hand sanitizer dispensers every fifteen feet, most of them empty or jammed. He'd reported the empty dispensers twice when he worked here. The second time was part of the complaint.

Lola's room was 112A, shared with Betty Ostrowski, who had severe vascular dementia and spent her days facing the window. Betty's daughter had taped photographs to the wall beside her bed — grandchildren, a dog, a house with a flag out front — but Betty looked through them the way she looked through everything, her eyes tracking something the rest of them couldn't see.

"Mijo!" Lola said when he came in, and her face did the thing it always did, this warm crumpling that made her look ten years younger. She was sitting in the vinyl recliner with a magazine in her lap, *People* from three months ago. Her left hand trembled against the armrest. Some days the tremor was barely there. Today it was visible from the doorway.

He bent down and kissed her cheek. She smelled like the coconut lotion Tammy helped her put on in the mornings, and underneath that, the warm close smell of someone who lived in a room that never fully aired out.

"You look skinny," she said. "You eating?"

"I'm eating fine."

"You look skinny. Sit, sit." She waved at Betty's chair, which Betty wasn't using because Betty was in the dining room, or the hallway, or wherever the aides had wheeled her for the afternoon. Marcus sat. The chair's cushion was split along one seam, foam poking through. He'd put in a maintenance request for that chair nine months before he was fired. It was the same chair.

"How's your week been?" he asked, because this was how it always started — fifteen minutes of visit, of being Lola's nephew who came on Thursdays, of the performance that was also not entirely a performance because she was, in the ways that mattered, the closest thing he had.

"Ay, you know," Lola said. "Same. The chicken on Tuesday was okay. Wednesday they gave us something they called a casserole, but I don't know what was in it. I asked Tammy and she said, 'Mrs. Fernandez, I wouldn't eat that if I was on death row.' Tammy's funny. She's — she's having a hard time, mijo. The husband."

"Ex-husband."

"Ex-husband, right. She doesn't say much but I can tell." Lola touched the gold crucifix at her throat, a gesture so automatic Marcus wasn't sure she knew she did it. "Anyway. I saved you some cookies."

She said this loud enough for the hallway. Marcus smiled.

The cookie tin was on her nightstand, between a framed photograph of Tomás and a plastic cup of water with a bendy straw. It was a blue Danish butter cookie tin, the kind you could buy at any dollar store, and Lola kept it stocked with actual cookies on top — pecan sandies from the commissary, three or four, enough to make the tin look right if anyone glanced inside.

Below the cookies, separated by a folded paper towel, were four ziplock bags. Agnes's handwriting on each bag in black Sharpie: the drug name, milligram, count, expiration date. Oxycodone 5mg x 24. Alprazolam 1mg x 30. Tramadol 50mg x 18. Metformin 500mg x 40. The bags were flat, pressed carefully, no air. Agnes packed them like she was sealing evidence, which, Marcus supposed, she was.

He picked up the tin with one hand, popped a pecan sandie in his mouth with the other. "Lola, these are good."

"The ones from the commissary aren't bad. Not like the ones I used to make. Tomás liked the ones with the — what's it called, the almond flour. I can't get almond flour here."

"I'll bring you some."

"You don't have to do that."

"I want to."

She looked at him with that expression she got sometimes, where the warmth turned into something sharper, something that saw him more clearly than he liked. "You're a good boy, Marcus."

He was forty-four years old and she called him a good boy and it still landed somewhere in his chest that he couldn't defend against. He put the tin in the canvas tote bag he'd brought — the bag also contained a paperback western he'd gotten for Earl, a sleeve of Ritz crackers Harry had requested, and a pair of reading glasses from the dollar store that Lola had asked for last week because her prescription pair had a broken arm.

"I gotta get going," he said. "But I'll call you tonight, okay?"

"Okay, mijo. Drive safe. It's hot out there."

It was. Early September and the parking lot asphalt radiated heat through the soles of his sneakers. Beyond the low hedge at the edge of Lakeview's property, Route 27 shoved its afternoon line of box trucks, landscaper trailers, and impatient sedans toward New Brunswick. Lakeview sat a little back from the road, as if forty yards of scrub grass could disguise what the building smelled like once you got inside. His car was a 2009 Honda Accord with 187,000 miles and a passenger window that didn't go all the way up, leaving a quarter-inch gap that let in exhaust fumes and highway noise. The AC worked on the first and third settings but not the second. He put it on third and sat for a moment with his hands on the wheel, the tote bag on the passenger seat.

He talked to himself in the car. He'd always done this — his mother used to say he came out of the womb in mid-conversation — but it had gotten worse since she died, or maybe not worse, just more noticeable because there was nobody else to answer back.

"Okay," he said. "Rosa first, then Derek, then Janine. Rosa's off at three, so she'll be there by three-thirty. Derek said he's coming straight from work." He checked his prepaid phone — a Nokia flip phone from 2019 that Harry had insisted on because it couldn't be hacked, which Marcus didn't think was true but didn't argue about because you didn't argue with Harry about operational details. One text from Derek: *running 15 late*. One from Rosa: a thumbs-up emoji, which was the only emoji Rosa used.

The strip mall was twenty minutes south on Route 1. He took Route 27 long enough to catch the turnoff, then merged into the wider, dirtier stream of traffic past used-car lots with flapping pennants and the storage facility where he'd kept his mother's furniture for three months before admitting he couldn't afford the unit and donating everything to Goodwill. The plaza was a half-dead stretch of retail anchored by a Dollar General and a nail salon. The parking lot was big enough for the Kmart that used to be there and now sat empty, its sign removed, leaving a ghost outline on the facade. Marcus parked in the back corner near the dumpsters, where two other cars were already waiting.

He knew these people by refill rhythm and parking habits — who backed in for a fast exit, who kept the engine running, who wanted the bag passed low between the seats like that changed what it was. At Lakeview he'd known blood pressures, fall risks, which shoulder somebody favored when you helped them out of bed. Out here he knew who was late on cash, who checked the count in the lot, who needed to be told every single time not to say the drug name out loud.

Rosa Gutierrez got out first, one hand hooked over the top of the door while she levered herself upright. Lupus had made every movement look negotiated. The first time she'd bought from him she'd apologized before she named the drug, as if need itself was rude. Hydroxychloroquine had kept the swelling out of her knuckles and the kidney numbers from getting ugly until the dry-cleaning plant in Perth Amboy closed and her insurance went with it. CVS wanted $412 a month for the same manufacturer Agnes trusted. Marcus got it to her for fifteen dollars a refill and one standing instruction: keep the old prescription bottle in the car and don't say the name in the lot.

"Hey, Rosa."

"Marcus." She came over with her purse tucked high under one arm. Grocery coupons stuck out of the zipper. "Same as last time?"

"Same manufacturer. Agnes checked."

She nodded and tucked the bag away without opening it. Good. Marcus said, "You still got the bottle with you?"

Rosa patted her purse. "I do."

She handed him an envelope. Sixty dollars — two months' worth, because she'd missed last time when her car broke down. She'd written *thank you* on the outside in pencil. Marcus looked at it, put it in his back pocket with the others, and said, "You good on refills? You got enough for the month?"

"Yeah. The metformin, I can get some of that at Walmart for cheap, but the Plaquenil—" She stopped there, where she always stopped when a sentence threatened to sound too much like begging. "Yeah. I'm good."

"All right. You need anything else, you text me."

"I will. Thank you, Marcus."

She drove away in a Nissan Sentra with a dent in the rear quarter panel and a child's car seat in the back. Granddaughter, Marcus thought. Or niece. He didn't ask. Rosa's part of the day was the clean part, the version of this he could explain to himself without much strain: a woman got the pills that kept her hands from closing up on her and he got sixty dollars and some temporary relief from the idea that every exchange he touched had to be filthy all the way through.

Derek pulled in twelve minutes late and backed his F-150 into the slot beside Marcus a little too fast, one brake squeal and then silence. Music thumped once before he cut the engine. He came over still wearing a navy polo with a logo Marcus didn't recognize, an ID badge clipped to his belt, and steel-toed boots powdered white with warehouse dust. The boots told the truth faster than the shirt did: warehouse first, then whatever else paid after. On his second pickup Derek had told Marcus he'd been type 1 since he was eleven and was sick of people acting like insulin coverage meant he was covered, period. The insulin came through work. The metformin his endocrinologist added for secondary glucose management didn't. Every January the deductible reset like a threat with a date on it.

"Yo, what's up." Derek leaned in close as always, as if the quickest way through shame was speed. Marcus handed him the ziplock low between their bodies and Derek started peeling it open with his thumbnail before Marcus had his hand back.

"Don't do that here."

"I'm just checking."

"Check at home."

A white pickup with *PLAZA MAINTENANCE* stenciled on the door rolled slow past the empty Kmart and toward the dumpsters, the driver doing that bored sweep people did when they wanted to remember license plates later if somebody dumped a mattress or started shit. Marcus took the bag right back out of Derek's hands and dropped it between his seat and the console.

"The fuck, man."

"Exactly," Marcus said. "You want to count, you count at home."

The pickup idled long enough to make both of them look straight ahead. Then it kept moving.

Derek exhaled through his nose. "My bad."

Marcus handed the bag back. Derek shoved it into his pocket this time and pulled out a folded twenty.

Marcus looked at it. "It's thirty."

"I know it's thirty. I got twenty." Derek's jaw worked. "They cut us to thirty-two hours. That's part-time, man. I'm doing DoorDash after the warehouse and it's still not—" He stopped and looked out toward the road. "I'll have the rest next time. Plus the full thirty."

Marcus took the twenty.

"I'm good for it," Derek said.

"I know you are."

Derek stayed where he was, not leaving, which Marcus had already started to worry meant another kind of problem. "Hey, my wife's got migraines. Bad. Laying in the dark for two days at a time. Her doctor gave her some shit that doesn't work and she can't get back in for three weeks. My mom had leftover Imitrex but she's almost out. You got anything like that?"

"I don't have sumatriptan." Marcus kept his voice low. "I can ask, but no promises."

"Yeah." Derek rubbed the back of his neck. "She missed two shifts last week. Her boss said one more and she's done. It's not even dramatic. She just throws up and can't see right." He gave a short laugh with nothing funny in it. "American healthcare, baby."

"Don't text me about it. If I hear something, I'll let you know."

"I wasn't gonna text you."

"I'm saying."

Derek nodded once. "Sorry about the ten."

"Don't worry about it."

But Marcus did. A buyer who opened bags in the lot and came up short was exactly the kind of problem Harry liked solving by subtraction. Derek got back in his truck and pulled out fast enough to throw grit against Marcus's door. Marcus watched him go and already knew he wasn't telling Harry about the shortage. If Harry asked, Marcus would either lie or make the missing ten disappear with his own money. He had not decided which one felt worse.

Janine was last, late enough that the shade had moved and left half the lot bare again. She pulled in at 4:10 in a station wagon with a handicap placard hanging from the rearview. Janine Coates, sixty, got out the way she always did, one hand on the seat, one on the door, jaw tight until she was fully upright. Marcus knew the story because Janine told it in updates, a little more every pickup: packaging plant in Edison, line work, L4-L5 blown out, workers' comp decent until the claim closed. Forty dollars a physical therapy session even with her supplement. Eight hundred for an epidural that bought maybe three decent weeks. Gabapentin made her dizzy. The oxycodone didn't solve anything. It gave her enough room to sleep flat and stand at the stove long enough to make eggs.

"Sorry I'm late," she said. "I stopped at CVS first. Wanted to see if the price had magically become my business."

"How much?"

"One eighty-six for twenty-four." She let out a dry little laugh. "The girl at the counter said it like she was reading me the weather."

Marcus held out the ziplock. Oxycodone 5mg, twenty-four tablets. Janine looked at it and left it in his hand.

"Before I put that in my purse, tell me if you can do next Thursday."

"I can't tell you that because I don't know."

"Then ask tonight." She kept one hand on the roof of the car, saving herself the effort of standing there unsupported. "If it's no, fine. But I need the no while I've still got enough left to plan for it. I can ration on purpose. I can't do surprise pain. Surprise pain means I fall, or I don't sleep, or I sit in that recliner and hate everybody."

"I'll ask."

"Tonight, Marcus."

"Yeah. Tonight."

She took the bag then and handed him a red envelope — a hundred and twenty dollars, exact as always. Two other envelopes sat on the passenger seat beside her, windowed and mean-looking. Electric, water. Maybe the credit card she kept swearing she was gonna kill and somehow never could.

"If it's no," she said, settling herself back against the seat, "then I need to know before I start deciding which days I don't leave the house."

"I said I'll ask."

"I heard you. Pain makes me repetitive." She shut the door, then rolled the window down again. "My dog needs his teeth cleaned too, so apparently everybody in this car is falling apart at once."

"What kind of dog?"

"Beagle mix. Thirteen. Breath like a murder weapon." She almost smiled. "Call me when you know. Not tomorrow if you can help it."

"I will."

She drove away. Marcus sat in his car and counted the money twice: sixty from Rosa, twenty from Derek, a hundred and twenty from Janine. Then he pulled his own wallet from the glove box, peeled out a ten he'd meant to keep for gas, and folded it over Derek's shortage so Harry would see a clean thirty if the envelopes got checked. Two hundred and ten in the stack, only two hundred of it actually from buyers. His cut of the larger operation — the portion Harry tracked with his accountant's precision — came to roughly twelve hundred a month, counting all the buyers, not just these three. The twelve hundred covered rent on his basement apartment in North Brunswick, a space he'd found on Craigslist that turned out to be exactly as bad as a nine-hundred-dollar-a-month Craigslist basement apartment in central Jersey would be: low ceilings that he hit with his knuckles when he stretched, a window at ground level that showed him feet and car tires, a bathroom with a shower that drained slow enough that he stood in two inches of water by the time he was done. The remaining three hundred covered gas, food, the phone bill, and whatever was left over, which was nothing.

He sat there another minute, then went into Dollar General because the apartment had milk that had started to smell wrong and nothing in it that qualified as dinner unless you were being generous. He stood in the checkout line behind a woman buying cat litter and grape soda, doing subtraction in his head before each item hit the belt. He put the ibuprofen back once, then took it again because his knees had started talking on stairs. The cashier asked if he wanted the receipt. Marcus said yes because once money got this tight you kept proof of stupid things like dish soap. Twenty-two dollars and some change. Derek's missing ten had just become cereal and half a tank less than he needed.

He drove home. Past the strip mall, past the Wawa where he sometimes bought a hoagie for dinner when he was feeling irresponsible, past the apartments on Livingston Avenue that were nicer than his. Seeing those buildings always made him think of the job that used to keep him just this side of broke. Seven years at Lakeview, CNA work, not nurse money but steady: lifting, turning, cleaning, feeding, the hands-on part the charts turned into neat little lines. He'd been good at it. Quietly good. He knew which residents wanted the lights on at night, which needed the pillow angled for their GERD, who got agitated if you approached from the left, who needed to be talked through the transfer and who needed you to shut up and just help.

What got him fired was giving a shit out loud. June 2024, Wing A was running one aide for twenty-two residents on overnight when the state minimum was one for every ten. Devon was covering both wings alone. Waiting, in a place like that, meant piss, a fall, an oxygen cannula twisted into trouble. Marcus found Mrs. Patterson on her bathroom floor at four in the morning, one side of her scrub top soaked through, hip swelling under skin thin as paper, call light tangled in the sheets where she couldn't reach it. She'd been there an hour. He wrote the complaint that night.

Cliff thanked him for his concern, did nothing, then acted surprised when Marcus went to the ombudsman's office. Lakeview produced paperwork for a second overnight aide, a woman named Marjorie Sims who existed on the schedule and nowhere else Marcus ever saw, and the investigator called the staffing compliant. The next Tuesday Cliff called Marcus hostile, called him insubordinate, and fired him anyway. By the time Marcus turned onto his landlord's street, his jaw hurt from clenching it.

Marcus's apartment was dark when he got home. The basement meant the light was always wrong — too dim in the daytime, too harsh when he turned on the overheads at night. He had a couch from Goodwill, a television on a plastic crate, a kitchen that was a counter and a hot plate and a mini-fridge. His CNA certification hung on the wall in a frame his mother had bought him. She'd been proud of that certificate. She'd told everyone — her friends at church, the woman at the dry cleaner, the guy who ran the bodega — that her son was in healthcare.

The certificate always dragged him back to the Tuesday he came home with his locker stuff in a grocery bag and told her he'd been fired. By then congestive heart failure had her on a whole pharmacy tray: lisinopril in the morning, furosemide twice a day, metoprolol at bedtime, potassium because the furosemide leached it out, warfarin and biweekly INR draws. Four months later she died in her bed in the apartment above the dry cleaner on Hamilton Street, not at a hospital, not with anybody billing by the quarter hour. The television was on because she liked the noise and he couldn't stand the silence. He knew what the change in her breathing meant before it was over and sat there holding her hand anyway.

Lola had called the next day, somehow with his number. Nobody else from Lakeview did. That January she'd passed along Harry's message: Marcus should visit. By then he'd burned through warehouse temp work, two weeks on a landscaping crew before his knees gave out, and a Target night-stocking job that paid eleven-fifty an hour. He'd gone in expecting maybe condolence money, maybe Harry wanting him to make noise at the ombudsman's office again. Instead Harry, a man Marcus had honestly thought was confused, laid out the business proposition like he was explaining somebody's taxes: supply, margins, buyer need, why Marcus was useful. Marcus should have left. Rent was due in five days. He stayed in the chair.

He ate a bowl of cereal standing at the counter because he didn't have a kitchen table and the couch was too low to eat from without spilling. The milk was still cold from the store. Then he sat on the couch and took out his prepaid phone and called Lola.

She picked up on the second ring. He could hear the television in her room — the news, or a game show, one of those sounds that was just Lakeview's ambient hum.

"Everything went good," he said. "Rosa's set. Derek asked about sumatriptan for his wife. Janine wants next Thursday instead of two weeks. I told her I'd ask."

"Good, good." She was quiet for a moment, and he heard the slight rasp of her hand against the phone case. The tremor made it hard for her to hold the phone steady some days. "Agnes wants the count anyway. She said if Janine is moving up, she wants to know what is actually left, not what you think is left."

"Metformin's fine. Forty went to Rosa and Derek. It's oxy that's the question. I told Janine I'd ask, not promise."

"Tell Agnes Derek asked about sumatriptan too. For his wife."

Something bumped the mouthpiece on Lola's end. Then Agnes said, close and flat, "No."

Marcus sat up. "No because you don't have it, or no because you don't like the ask?"

"Both. I do not hand out triptans because a husband tells a story in a parking lot. If she wants me to look at it, he brings the current bottle with the label. Original label."

"She's missing work."

"That changes her need. It does not change pharmacology." Paper shifted near the receiver, the dry slide of Agnes already counting while she talked. "And Janine does not move up unless I can count the oxycodone twice and still spare it."

"She said if she gets surprised by the pain she falls."

"I heard you." Agnes did not sound angry. That was worse. "We are not a pharmacy, Marcus. We have a cookie tin and a count sheet."

Lola came back on, breath a little shorter from the handoff. "Mijo, don't start. If the pills are there, they're there. If they're not, then what, you tell Janine yes and hand her air?"

"I'll tell her. She'll do the math." Another pause. "Mijo, how's your mom?"

His throat closed. It did this every time, a physical thing, like someone had their thumb on his windpipe. Lola asked about his mother every week. She'd been asking for twenty months. He'd been lying for twenty months.

"She's good," he said. "She's got her — her heart stuff, you know, but she's doing okay. She's watching her shows."

"Tell her I said hi. Tell her she raised a good boy."

"I'll tell her."

On the cushion beside him sat the folded cash and the grocery receipt. He could hear the softer voice Lola would use if he told her the truth: *Ay, mijo.* Then she'd start asking if he was sleeping, if he was eating, saving him half a dinner roll in a napkin for Thursday. He pressed his thumb into the phone hinge and said nothing.

"You eat dinner?" Lola asked.

"Yeah. I had — yeah, I ate."

"Cereal isn't dinner, Marcus."

He laughed, a small, involuntary sound that surprised him. "How'd you know?"

"Because I know you. Go make something real. There's rice, you can make rice. Everybody can make rice."

"I'll make rice."

"With something. Rice with something."

"Rice with something. I promise."

He could hear her smile through the phone. "Okay. Goodnight, mijo. Be safe."

"Goodnight, Lola."

He sat on the couch after she hung up. Then he got up anyway and went to the kitchenette because standing up felt close enough to obedience to count for something. The cabinet over the sink held a ten-pound bag of rice, one can of black beans, garlic powder, a packet of sazon Lola had bullied him into buying because plain rice, she'd said, was hospital food. He touched the bag and left it there. The sink held one bowl, one fork, one pan with dried egg stuck to the bottom. The apartment was quiet except for the boiler in the utility closet on the other side of the wall, which ticked and rumbled at irregular intervals like a mechanical heartbeat with an arrhythmia. Upstairs, his landlord's television murmured. The ceiling was low enough that he could hear the floorboards creak when the landlord's wife walked to the kitchen.

He did not make rice. He went back to the couch with his prepaid phone in his hand and the CNA certificate on the wall staring back at him. Upstairs, his landlord's wife crossed the kitchen again. Marcus bent forward until his elbows hit his knees and stayed there under the low ceiling while the boiler ticked and Lola's voice kept saying *Rice with something.*


# Chapter 7

Carmen got to the school twelve minutes early because she'd overestimated the lights on Route 27. She parked behind a silver Lexus with a stick-figure family decal on the rear window — two parents, three kids, a dog — and cut the engine to save gas.

Mid-September, but the heat hadn't broken all the way. Sweat had dried tacky at the base of her neck on the drive over. She cracked the window. Reggie's elementary school was a single-story brick rectangle behind chain-link, its entrance banner reading WELCOME BACK VIKINGS in bubble letters some PTA parent had hand-painted with more enthusiasm than skill. The crossing guard stood at the curb in an orange vest, talking to another parent about something that required hand gestures.

Her mother usually did pickup. But Gloria had a rheumatology follow-up in Edison — her hands again, the swelling that flared and eased on its own calendar — and wouldn't be back until five. Carmen had the day off. Her first in eleven days, if she counted the half-shift she'd swapped with Denise on Tuesday, which she counted, because she'd driven there and back and used the gas.

She checked her phone. No messages from Lakeview. No messages from anyone. She'd been doing this for five days now — picking up the phone, checking the screen, waiting for something she couldn't name — ever since the night she'd told Harry she wouldn't give him a week and then given him one anyway. Two days left.

She put the phone facedown on the passenger seat and watched a line of kids file out the side door. Whatever was happening at Lakeview wasn't happening right now. Right now she was a mother in a pickup line, and the only math that mattered was the grocery list folded in her bag and the $74 she'd budgeted for it.

Reggie came out at 2:47, carrying his backpack on one shoulder because both straps were apparently uncool even in second grade. He climbed into the back seat and buckled himself in without being asked. He'd started doing that sometime in September, and each time it happened she felt the quiet pride of a milestone she'd missed noticing when it first appeared.

"Mom. Did you know sea turtles can hold their breath for five hours?"

"I didn't know that." She pulled out of the line, checking the mirror. "Is that for your project?"

"Yeah. Five hours. That's like from school to bedtime."

"Almost."

"Can we get poster board? Ms. Aldridge says we need poster board for the presentation part."

"What color?"

He considered this with the seriousness it deserved. "Blue. Because the ocean."

"We'll see what they have."

She drove to the ShopRite on Route 18. Not the closer one on Easton Avenue, which had better produce but charged thirty cents more per pound on chicken, and she went through three pounds a week. The parking lot was half-full — mid-afternoon on a weekday, the good hour before the after-work rush hit. She got a cart with a stuck front wheel because she didn't feel like walking it back for another one, and Reggie walked beside her with his hand on the cart's edge, steering them toward the cereal aisle by habit.

"We're going around," she said. "Produce first."

"Can we get Cinnamon Toast Crunch?"

"We'll see."

They wouldn't. It was $4.79 a box and the store-brand version was $2.49 and tasted close enough that Reggie had stopped noticing after the first bowl. But she didn't say no yet because saying no required a reason, and sometimes he forgot to ask again by the time they reached that aisle.

She worked the list. Bananas, sixty-nine cents a pound — she took four. A bag of Gala apples, $3.99 for the three-pound bag. The Honeycrisps were $6.49 and she didn't look at them. Carrots, a head of broccoli, a bag of yellow onions. She picked up the cherry tomatoes at $3.49 a pint, held them for two seconds, put them back, and took a can of diced tomatoes for eighty-nine cents instead.

Reggie held the apples while she compared unit prices on chicken thighs. Bone-in skin-on was $1.89 a pound. Boneless skinless was $3.29. She did the math on yield — bone-in lost maybe twenty percent to waste, but the bones went into stock, which gave her soup base for two dinners, which dropped the per-meal cost below the boneless even before she factored in the labor of stripping the meat. She put two family packs of bone-in thighs in the cart. $7.12 total.

Rice, $3.49 for the five-pound bag — still half a bag at home, skip it this week. Dry black beans, $1.29. Eggs, $2.99 for the eighteen-count. She could never bring herself to buy the dozen at $2.49; the per-egg math was wrong and the per-egg math was the only math. Bread, $1.89, store brand. Peanut butter, $2.69 — she was loyal to this brand because Reggie liked it and some things she refused to substitute.

In the cereal aisle Reggie stood in front of the Cinnamon Toast Crunch and didn't say a word. He just stayed there with one hand on the box and waited. Carmen knew the move from Gloria.

"Next time," she said.

He picked up the store brand without argument. That was worse than complaining. It meant he'd learned the difference between what you wanted and what the budget allowed, and he was seven, and she hated that he already knew.

The poster board was in the school supply section, wedged between folders and glue sticks. Blue, $1.29. She put it in the cart and he held it flat across the top of the bags like he was transporting something fragile and important.

At checkout the total came to $68.43. Under budget by $5.57, and she'd come back Thursday for milk and whatever fruit was on manager's special. She paid with her debit card and watched the number leave her checking account in real time on her phone's banking app. $411.18 remaining. Rent was due in sixteen days.

She loaded the bags into the Civic's trunk while Reggie sat in the back with the poster board across his lap, tracing waves in the air with his finger.

---

At home — the apartment was on the second floor of a two-family house, on a street where the sidewalks buckled over tree roots — she unpacked groceries while Reggie spread his project across the kitchen table. He had a library book about sea turtles with a cracked spine, a printed worksheet from Ms. Aldridge with guiding questions, and six markers that were all running low because markers were always running low.

"Mom, loggerheads can weigh three hundred and fifty pounds."

"That's like three of me."

He looked at her, calculating. "Maybe two of you."

"I'm choosing to take that as a compliment."

She seasoned the chicken thighs with adobo and garlic powder and put the pan in the oven at 375. Not the broiler — the broiler ran uneven and she couldn't afford a burned batch. Rice on the stove. She washed the broccoli and cut it into pieces Reggie would eat, small enough to seem manageable, not so small she'd be standing at the counter with a knife for twenty minutes.

While the chicken cooked she sat with him and helped draw a sea turtle on the poster board. She wasn't good at drawing. The turtle came out looking like a worried football with flippers. Reggie studied it with generous gravity, said "That's OK, Mom," and carefully drew his own version next to hers. His was better in every way — rounder shell, actual flippers, an eye with expression in it. He labeled it LOGGERHEAD SEA TURTLE in careful block capitals. The second G was backward.

"They eat jellyfish," he said. "And they don't even get stung."

"How?"

"Their throat has, like —" He made a shape with his hands, fingers pointed inward. "Pointy things. Spines. Inside their whole throat. So the jellyfish can't sting when they swallow."

She watched his face while he explained this — the concentration, the pleasure of knowing something she didn't — and felt the thing she always felt when Reggie showed her who he was becoming. Not pride exactly. Something closer to fear. He was growing into someone who would need things she wasn't sure she could give him.

They ate at the kitchen table with the poster board moved carefully to the counter. The chicken was fine. The rice was fine. Reggie ate the broccoli without complaint, which meant he was too distracted to notice it, because he was telling her about leatherback sea turtles. They could dive deeper than submarines. "Well, some submarines." And plastic bags in the ocean looked exactly like jellyfish, and the turtles ate them by mistake and died, and there was a photo in his library book of a dead turtle cut open and its stomach was full of plastic.

"That's really sad," she said.

"Yeah." He ate a piece of chicken, chewing thoughtfully. "Ms. Aldridge says we can write to our senator about it."

"You should."

"Will they write back?"

"Probably someone in their office will. Like an assistant."

He considered this. "That's not the same."

"No," she said. "It's not."

She cleared the table. He took a bath — he was old enough now to do most of it himself, which was a relief she felt guilty about the way she felt guilty about every convenience that arrived from him needing her less. She checked behind his ears, the back of his neck. He smelled like the strawberry shampoo in the bottle shaped like a fish, which cost $1.80 more than the store brand and was the one expense she didn't negotiate because it was the only shampoo he didn't fight her on.

Homework: a reading comprehension worksheet and eight math problems involving subtraction with regrouping. She sat on his bed while he worked at his desk, his pencil pressed too hard against the paper the way it always had been, since pre-K. The lead broke on problem six. She sharpened it and handed it back. The school's occupational therapist had said he'd self-correct. The occupational therapist outside the school, the one who could actually work with him regularly, cost $150 an hour and wasn't covered by her insurance. So the pencil grip would self-correct, or it wouldn't, and either way she couldn't afford to find out.

He finished at eight-fifteen. She read to him — three chapters of a book about a dog who solved mysteries, borrowed from the library because books were free if you returned them on time. She was religious about returning them on time.

He fell asleep at twenty to nine. She stood in his doorway for a moment. His breathing had that particular open-mouthed child-sleep sound — deep and unguarded, with a small whistle on the exhale that the pediatrician said was just his adenoids being seven-year-old adenoids, nothing to worry about. His poster board leaned against the desk. The worried-football turtle and the better turtle, side by side. LOGGERHEAD SEA TURTLE. The backward G.

She pulled his door most of the way shut and went to the kitchen.

---

The dishes were done and the chicken was portioned into containers — two lunches, one dinner reheated over rice on Thursday — when she called Yolanda. Nine-twenty. Late enough that Reggie wouldn't wake, early enough that Yolanda would still be doing what she did every night: watching old ER episodes on her laptop with the sound low while her cat sat on the keyboard and she pretended this bothered her more than it did.

Yolanda picked up on the third ring. "Hold on." The sound of the cat being lifted and set down on something. "OK. What's up?"

"Nothing. Just calling."

"You sound off."

"I'm tired."

"You're always tired. This is a different kind."

Yolanda had been an ICU nurse at University Hospital in Newark for nine years. She read voices the way she read monitors — baseline deviations registered before the person talking knew they were deviating. She was also the kind of person who bought name-brand everything and then complained about money, which Carmen found both maddening and faintly admirable, a willingness to choose pleasure over math that she herself had never managed.

"I'm fine. How's work?"

"Garbage. We're short three nurses because two called out and one quit, and HR sent a temp who didn't know where the crash cart was. Nine years and I'm still showing people where the crash cart is."

"Rough."

Carmen folded the dish towel into thirds, then thirds again, until it was a white strip under her hand. "You ever see them put somebody on leave over a narc discrepancy when it wasn't diversion?"

Yolanda didn't answer right away. "At Lakeview?"

"It's not — no. I'm asking."

"Then yes." Yolanda exhaled. "That's why the whole floor spent half the shift pissed today. HR finally made Dana Whitfield clean out her locker."

Carmen didn't know a Dana Whitfield. "Who's that?"

"Three South. Med-surg. She'd been here — four years? No, five, because she started after — anyway. Solid nurse. Not flashy, but she knew her stuff. Back in January, pharmacy sent up a hydromorphone count that didn't match what she actually got."

Carmen's hand was resting on the counter. She straightened the folded towel and set it on the edge of the sink.

"How far off?"

"Two tablets. And she documented it, called her supervisor, the whole—"

"And they still pulled her."

"That night." Yolanda's voice had gone flat, the way it did when she was past anger and into the duller terrain on the other side. "Board put an interim suspension on her license while they investigate."

"Before they proved anything."

"No charges. No proof she took anything. Just two tablets on paper and she can't touch a med cart. It's been eight months."

"What's she doing?"

"Moved in with her sister in Bloomfield. She's got a kid — seventh grade, or eighth, I forget. She's doing DoorDash and fighting HR over the PTO they still owe her."

Carmen looked at the containers of chicken on the counter. Two lunches. Thursday dinner. Three-day cycle.

"Even if they clear her—"
