"I know. She's the nurse who got walked out over narcotics. That's what sticks."

"Not whether she did it."

"Not whether she did it." Yolanda exhaled. "Eight months of gap and every interview from now on, she's explaining that. You know how that goes."

Carmen knew how that went.

"Anyway." The cat returned — Carmen heard the small thump and Yolanda's automatic "Get off, Nelson." She'd named the cat after the hospital's parking garage because that's where she'd found him, two years old with a torn ear and a personality disorder. "How's Reggie?"

"Good. He's doing a project on sea turtles."

"Cute. He's such a weird little scientist."

"He's not weird."

"He's a little weird. In a great way. He told me dolphins sleep with one eye open and then stared at me to see if I'd try it. That's weird, Carmen."

A laugh hit the back of her teeth and stopped there. What came out was a flat "Yeah."

Yolanda paused. Carmen had heard that pause before: Yolanda going quiet when she knew somebody was lying.

"You sure you're all right?"

"I'm fine. Long stretch. I haven't had a day off in—" Eleven days, but saying the number would make it sound like she was asking for something. "I'm just tired."

"You keep saying that."

"Because I'm fine, Yolanda."

"Come over Saturday. I'll make that rice thing. We'll drink wine and talk shit about men we don't even have."

"Maybe. Let me see how the schedule looks."

They talked for another ten minutes — Yolanda's upstairs neighbor who played reggaeton at 3 AM, a patient on the unit who'd coded and come back swinging at the respiratory therapist, Nelson's vet bill for a tooth extraction that cost $387 and which Yolanda described with the specific outrage of a person who'd spent that money on a cat's mouth without hesitating and then needed someone to be appalled on her behalf. Carmen listened and laughed at the right places and gave the right responses and didn't say any of the things she couldn't say.

After they hung up she sat at the kitchen table.

The apartment was quiet. The refrigerator hummed the way it did every forty minutes or so, the compressor climbing into a higher whine before settling back down. Through the shared wall she could hear the downstairs tenant's TV — a muffled laugh track, then a man's voice, then the laugh track again.

She opened the calculator on her phone.

$47,000 a year. Gross. After federal, state, Social Security, and Medicare, her take-home was $2,998 a month. She knew this number the way she knew Reggie's birthday and her blood type. It arrived on the first and the fifteenth, half and half, and it was gone by the time the next one came.

Student loans: $680. She'd consolidated three years ago and the monthly payment had actually gone up because the interest rate adjusted and she hadn't understood the fine print well enough to argue it. $38,000 remaining. She'd be forty-four when they were paid off if nothing went wrong, if she never missed a month, if the rate didn't adjust again.

Rent: $1,200. The landlord had raised it $75 in June. Carmen had said thank you because the alternative was a $1,400 apartment farther from Reggie's school, and the difference in gas money would have eaten the savings.

Car payment: $400. A 2019 Civic with 94,000 miles. The transmission had made a sound last month — a low grinding on the upshift between second and third — that she was choosing to interpret as normal.

Car insurance: $200.

$2,998 minus $2,480 left $518 a month. For everything else. Her phone, $68, because work required the apps and the scheduling portals. Gas — Lakeview and back, Reggie's school, her mother's house, the ShopRite that was farther but cheaper — $160 in a careful month. Food for two: $74 a week if she bought bone-in thighs and store-brand cereal and never once looked at the Honeycrisps.

The $518 covered those things and then it was done. What was left — Reggie's school supplies, the field trips with their $12 permission slips she couldn't send back unsigned, the shoes he outgrew every four months, the copays, the dentist twice a year at $180 because her insurance covered one cleaning and she wasn't going to skip his second — came from doubles. Fourteen in the last two months. Five hundred, sometimes eight hundred extra a check, before taxes. Her mother watched Reggie those nights for free, which was not free, because Gloria's time had value and the fact that her mother never mentioned this was a cost Carmen carried differently but carried all the same.

The doubles were the margin. The doubles were the entire gap between making it and not making it.

If she lost her license, the $47,000 disappeared. The doubles disappeared. She had nine years of nursing experience and an LPN credential, and if the board suspended her — not revoked, not convicted, just suspended pending investigation the way they'd suspended Dana Whitfield for two hydromorphone tablets — the nine years became a gap she'd have to explain at every interview for the rest of her working life. She could do retail. She could drive for DoorDash. She could move in with her mother, put Reggie in Gloria's second bedroom, and try to rebuild from the floor of a career that had taken her five years of night school and $38,000 in debt to build.

If she stayed quiet, tomorrow's controlled-substance count started from tonight's lie. She'd unlock the cabinet, count with another nurse, and sign beneath numbers she knew were wrong. Whoever relieved her would sign after that.

She closed the calculator. The screen went dark.

She opened Lakeview's incident-report portal, the one they used for falls, med errors, skin tears, anything administration might someday swear nobody had told them about.

The form wanted a date of occurrence, a medication name, a witness, confirmation that the supervisor had been notified, and the name of the dispensing nurse. Her thumbs stayed still above the keyboard. The pills were logged under her license number. They always were. Leaving it blank meant she'd be back in that drawer tomorrow, signing the same lie again.

She turned off the kitchen light and walked down the hall.

Reggie had rolled onto his side. One arm hung off the mattress, his hand open, fingers loosely curled the way they went when he was fully gone. The nightlight — a blue plastic turtle she'd found at a garage sale for fifty cents, which he'd carried around the house for two days before allowing it to be plugged in — cast the room in the color of shallow water. His breathing, the small whistle.

His worksheet sat on the desk under the blue light, one corner curled up. In the box labeled THREATS he'd printed PLASTIC BAGS, BOATS, PEOPLE, pressing so hard the pencil had dented the paper. Her phone screen lit her palm with the blank report.

Carmen pulled the door most of the way closed and stood in the hall with the report open in her hand until the screen went dark.


# Chapter 8

Agnes Chen's roommate breathed in nine-second cycles. There was a wet click on the inhale, then a pause, then the drain on the exhale. The interval varied from seven to eleven seconds. Agnes had logged the numbers in her head for two months before accepting that the variation was random. It was not pathological. It was a pharmacist's reflex: measure, correlate, intervene. There was nothing to intervene on. Her roommate, Doris Okonkwo, eighty-three, was five years into Alzheimer's and had not produced a recognizable word since Christmas two years ago. The breathing meant only that Doris's body was still running its autonomic functions while the person who had lived inside it was gone.

It was Sunday. Helen was coming.

Agnes lay facing the wall with her eyes open. She had been awake since five-fifteen. She was always awake by five-fifteen. Her body kept pharmacist's hours; she had opened the Walgreens in Edison, New Jersey, at six AM five days a week for twenty-seven of her forty-two years behind the counter, and the internal clock had not adjusted for retirement, stroke, or institutionalization. The suprachiasmatic nucleus, she knew, was largely immune to changes in circumstance. You could strip away everything else and the body would still wake at five-fifteen and expect you to count pills.

The Sunday morning aide was Shanice, early twenties, efficient, talked while she worked. She came in at seven-forty and changed Doris's incontinence brief with competent hands, narrating as she moved: how's your morning, Mrs. Okonkwo, let's get you cleaned up, there we go. Agnes gave Shanice credit for this. Most aides talked to the wall or to their phones. Shanice talked to Doris. It was a small kindness. It cost nothing. It was still rare.

To Agnes, without slowing down: "Morning, Mrs. Chen. Helen's coming today, right? You want me to do your hair?" She spoke at normal volume and speed, which meant she had either not read Agnes's chart recently or had read it and didn't adjust. Either way, Agnes preferred it to the alternative.

Agnes made no response. She had refined her performance over three years into something the staff could categorize and dismiss: silence, stillness, eyes that tracked nothing. The clinical notes read "nonverbal, oriented x0, no meaningful response to verbal or visual stimuli." She had seen the notes because a nurse's aide sometimes left the chart open on the bedside table during linen changes, and Agnes could read upside down. She had spent decades checking prescription labels across a counter from the patient's side. The diagnosis was wrong. The classification was wrong. But it was consistent, and consistency was what kept the system from looking again.

Shanice left. Agnes sat up. The dizziness that sometimes accompanied position changes, a residual from the stroke, did not come today. She put on her glasses from the nightstand and walked to the bathroom. It took four steps. Her left foot scuffed the linoleum faintly, a drag she could mostly control when she concentrated. She used the toilet, washed her hands, and checked the hair above her ears in the mirror. Another week and she would trim it herself with the nail scissors in the bedside drawer. The salon visits the facility offered required forty-five minutes of performative helplessness and somebody tipping her chin as if her neck no longer worked. She would not sit through that. She pushed the glasses higher on her nose. She had been wearing some version of them since 1993; the frames had been replaced twice, and the prescription had held steady for six years. She tugged the waistband of her pajama pants back into place. She had held at a hundred and eight pounds for fifteen years. Her body, like her internal clock, did not accommodate circumstance.

She ate the breakfast tray in her room: powdered scrambled eggs, toast, a carton of chocolate Ensure mandated by the dietary order. She ate slowly. Her face stayed mechanical and blank. Doris's tray went untouched until Shanice came back to spoon-feed her, holding a paper towel under Doris's chin to catch what slid out.

Helen arrived at ten-fifteen.

Agnes heard her in the hallway first. Helen's voice carried, louder than necessary, narrating her arrival as she narrated everything: "Come on, come on, Grandma's room is right here. Lily, careful of the — Tyler, do not touch that." The grandchildren came with her. Lily was thirteen now and had learned to walk through Lakeview with her eyes fixed on her phone, which was how she avoided the residents in their wheelchairs, the walkers parked along the walls, the warm sourness underneath the disinfectant that no one who worked here could smell anymore but that visitors flinched from. Tyler was ten and still young enough to stare.

Helen came through the door carrying a canvas tote bag and wearing her Lakeview face: bright, effortful, already tired. She was fifty-two, small like Agnes, with Agnes's jawline and her late father's height. She had on a quilted vest over a turtleneck and white sneakers that had never been worn for anything more strenuous than a grocery run. She kissed Agnes on the forehead. Agnes held still and permitted this. Resisting would break character.

"Hi, Mom. I brought those almond cookies from H Mart, the ones you like." Helen spoke slowly, clearly, with each word separated by a fraction more space than it needed. The volume was fifteen percent above conversational baseline. Agnes had measured that too. Helen used the same setting with Tyler when he wore headphones. "Lily, come say hi to Grandma. Tyler, I said come say hi."

Lily approached and said, "Hi, Grandma," with the compressed discomfort of a thirteen-year-old who did not know what to do with a grandmother who did not respond. She held her phone against her thigh with both hands. Tyler came to the foot of the bed and said, "Hi, Grandma," borrowing his mother's intonation, because he was ten and borrowed his emotional cues from the nearest adult.

Agnes let her eyes rest on each grandchild for a slow count of three, long enough to seem like involuntary recognition, and then returned her gaze to the wall. This was the performance that cost her the most. The children's discomfort did not bother her. It was healthy. Children should be uneasy around illness and age. Lily's hands bothered her. They were long-fingered and narrow-nailed, with the slight turn of the thumb Agnes had noticed when Lily was seven and checked again at every visit since. They were pharmacist's hands. They would be good for precise work.

Helen filled the silence. She was practiced at this. She set the cookies on the nightstand, unpacked a new cardigan she'd found on sale (lavender, soft, the kind of purchase that made Helen feel she was doing something without requiring her to examine what that something was), and narrated: Lily had made the school volleyball team, her serve was strong but her passing needed work; Tyler's teacher said he talked too much in class but his reading was advanced; the landscaping people had finally finished the backyard; Bill was traveling again, always traveling, she hardly saw him. Helen delivered her own life in the same bright, careful voice she used for all of it, the children's news and her own loneliness flattened to the same register, and Agnes listened to all of it and said nothing.

Helen visited every other Sunday. She brought cookies or a cardigan or a photograph of the children. She talked for forty minutes. She touched Agnes's hand before she left. And she went home believing she was a good daughter doing a hard thing, because the alternative was a thought that would require her to dismantle three years of decisions. Helen built forward. By noon there would be cookies on the nightstand, a lavender cardigan over the chair, and Agnes still charted as oriented x0.

The stroke happened on a Tuesday in March, three years earlier. Agnes was in her kitchen in Edison, the house she had owned for thirty-one years and paid off in 2014, when the left side of her body stopped cooperating. Her left hand released the coffee cup. It hit the counter, not the floor. It was laminate, not tile. She remembered the sound. She made it to the phone. She called 911. She tried to say her address. Noise came out.

The stroke was ischemic, left middle cerebral artery. The aphasia, the word loss, was the most dramatic symptom. She could think in complete sentences. She could not produce them. The words were on the correct shelves in her mind, properly labeled, but the dispensing window had jammed shut. She could write, slowly, with her right hand. On the hospital whiteboard she wrote: "I am a pharmacist. I am cognitively intact. My speech will recover." She wrote it three times in three days. The neurologist, Dr. Fein, confirmed the prognosis: Broca's aphasia with high probability of significant recovery within six to twelve months.

The day Agnes learned Helen had moved from panic to administration, a speech therapist was holding up laminated picture cards: apple, comb, ladder. Agnes could point. The words would not cross the gap. Helen stood at the window with Agnes's purse open on the sill, sorting keys from receipts.

The therapist said, too brightly, "Since your daughter found a good assisted living place near her, we should practice functional phrases for that environment. Bathroom. Water. Pain."

Agnes held out her right hand. The therapist passed her the marker. Agnes wrote on the whiteboard in block capitals: I DID NOT AGREE TO THIS.

Helen turned from the window. "Mom, you can't go home alone right now."

Agnes wrote: RIGHT NOW IS NOT PERMANENT. CALL DR. FEIN.

Helen's face tightened into the same bright strain she wore on Sundays. "I'm handling this. The house goes on the market Friday."

Agnes stared at her, then pressed the marker hard enough to squeal. TAKE IT OFF.

The speech therapist said she should come back later. Helen said yes, please, without asking Agnes, and the therapist left. Agnes sat with the sentence drying on the board while Helen explained square footage, waiting lists, storage, all the logistical nouns people used when they had decided your life was an administrative problem.

Helen sold the house for three hundred and twelve thousand dollars. She moved Agnes's possessions into a storage unit on Route 1, the one next to the tire shop, $189 a month. She toured three facilities and chose Lakeview Gardens because it was near her own house and because the administrator, Cliff Moorehead, had used the phrase "person-centered care" during the tour. Agnes arrived in a wheelchair she did not need, and during intake an admissions aide spoke over her head to Helen while the summary clipped to her file described her as "recent CVA with significant cognitive-linguistic deficits, requires 24-hour supervised care."

Her speech began to recover within two months. By month four, she could produce short sentences with effort and concentration. By month six, she was fluent again, with occasional word-finding hesitations under pressure that most people would not notice.

She spent those six months fighting. She wrote three letters to the Long-Term Care Ombudsman, each more detailed than the last, listing her NJ license number, her cognitive status, and her demand for independent neuropsychological assessment. The first letter received a form acknowledgment. The second prompted a phone call from a caseworker who said she would look into it, which produced nothing. The third was never answered.

She called Helen eleven times in one month. Helen cried on three of those calls and said, "Mom, the doctors said you need this level of care." Agnes said, "Which doctors?" Helen said, "The doctors at the hospital." Agnes said, "Call Dr. Fein. Ask him if I need to be institutionalized." Helen said she would call. She did not call. Agnes knew she had not called because at the next visit Helen said, "Dr. Fein agrees you're in the best place for your recovery," with that bright practiced tone. Helen had told herself the story enough times to believe it. That made it harder to fight.

Agnes demanded a cognitive assessment in writing, verbally, and through the ombudsman's office. The facility administered a Mini-Mental State Examination. Agnes scored 30 out of 30. The result went into her chart. Nothing changed. Her care plan was not updated. Her placement was not reviewed.

By September of that first year, the calculation was complete. Placement, once achieved, was self-reinforcing. The documentation supported the placement. The placement justified the documentation. Agnes was institutionalized because she had been institutionalized. The original cause, a temporary speech deficit from a recoverable stroke, had dissolved. The institutional fact remained.

She began to perform.

The decision was clinical. If the system classified her as impaired, she would be impaired. Fighting it produced no result, and Agnes had spent most of her adult life directing her energy toward useful work. Silence, wall-staring, and nonresponse became resource allocation.

Harry Zielinski approached her fourteen months ago. She had been at Lakeview for nearly two years by then. She had noticed him early. His confusion was too responsive to audience, too calibrated. Real confusion did not adjust its severity based on who was in the room. Harry's did. She watched him for weeks before he noticed her watching, and when he did, he came to her room one night when Devon, the overnight aide, was asleep at the station and stood in the doorway. He said, in a clear, controlled voice: "The tramadol they give Earl is fifty milligrams. Should it be?"

Agnes had not spoken aloud in four months. Her voice came out rough and dry. But the words were exact: "Depends on his renal function. At eighty-one, if his GFR is below sixty, fifty is the correct ceiling. If they prescribed a hundred, they didn't check his labs."

Harry looked at her for a long time. Then he said, "We should talk."

She joined because she could not stand watching medications mishandled. That was true. It was incomplete. She had spent forty-two years behind the same counter at the Walgreens on Oak Tree Road in Edison, opening the gate at six AM, closing it at six PM, standing on a black anti-fatigue mat she replaced every two years because the foam compressed and her lower back knew the difference. She had filled over a million prescriptions, caught fourteen dangerous interactions, refused a contraindicated benzodiazepine-opioid combination for a physician's wife, and survived the Board complaint that followed because she was right. She had trained eleven pharmacy interns. She knew NDC codes as reflexively as a home address.

Then the stroke took her speech, and Helen took everything else, and Agnes had nowhere to put what she knew. The ring gave her a counter to stand behind again. The work was illegal. The work was also pharmaceutical, which meant it still answered to her standards.

She kept four composition notebooks. They were the black-and-white marbled kind, $3.99 each from the Lakeview gift shop, shelved between the stuffed bears and the get-well cards with lilies on them. She had Harry buy them for her, and he did, without asking what they were for, because Harry understood the value of documentation even if he could not have said precisely what Agnes was documenting.

The first notebook was inventory. It listed current stock by drug name, NDC code, dosage strength, quantity on hand, and expiration date. She updated it after every ring meeting. She tracked losses: pills miscounted, pills damaged, pills that Burt had swallowed by accident when his confusion sent him reaching for the wrong cup. She projected supply based on prescription refill schedules and flagged medications that would run low before the next cycle.

The second notebook was the buyer registry. She did not keep names; names were Marcus's responsibility. She kept medical profiles: the buyer's condition, their other medications if known, contraindications and interaction risks, and what they purchased from the ring. Agnes cross-referenced every new sale against the existing profile before anything left the building.

The third notebook was transactions. It listed dates, quantities dispensed, and amounts received. This was Harry's territory, the accounting, but Agnes kept her own parallel record because she did not trust anyone's numbers except her own. Harry's figures matched hers within acceptable rounding variance. This was the only professional compliment she had given him.

The fourth notebook was refusals and corrections. It recorded dangerous combinations, buyers screened out, and Marcus's improvisations stopped before they became someone's respiratory depression or GI bleed. It was the least profitable notebook and the one that mattered most.

She kept the notebooks under her mattress, shoved up against the wall where the fitted sheet sagged loose. Aides changed beds fast. They lifted corners, snapped elastic, moved on. Nobody in the building stripped a mattress clean unless there was vomit or bedbugs. The four notebooks were the ring's operating record: inventory control, buyer screening, financial record, corrective action. They were also the most damning evidence a prosecutor could want. Every transaction was dated. Every quantity was logged in her handwriting, her precise hand, with NDC codes that only a trained professional would think to include. If someone found them, they would read as confession.

She kept them because operating without documentation, relying on memory and approximation, was more frightening to her than the legal risk. An undocumented pharmaceutical operation was an unsafe operation. She would sooner be caught than be careless.

The fentanyl argument was the only time she raised her voice with Harry. It happened in his room at one-twelve in the morning, the television on mute for cover, while Burt slept down the hall with a 50-microgram transdermal patch on his shoulder. Marcus stood by the sink and said, "I got a buyer asking about patches. Real money."

Harry said, "How much?"

Marcus named a number.

Agnes said, "No."

Marcus looked at her. "You didn't even hear the rest of it."

"I heard 'patches.' That is sufficient."

Harry said, "If the buyer knows what he's doing, and if we cut —"

Agnes interrupted him. "Do not finish that sentence. A fentanyl patch is not a scored tablet. It is for an opioid-tolerant patient. An opioid-naive adult could stop breathing before he understood he was in trouble."

Marcus said, "People buy worse shit than that every day."

"Then let them buy it somewhere else."

Harry started again, quieter. "The margin is —"

"No." Agnes was on her feet by then. Earl looked up from the chair by the window. "If you sell that, keep my records off it. The outcome is predictable."

Harry held her eyes for a long time. Then he said, "All right."

Marcus muttered once and stopped when Harry looked at him. Burt's patches stayed on Burt's skin where they belonged.

Helen left at eleven. She kissed Agnes's forehead, told the children to say goodbye, and walked out with the empty tote bag. Her shoulders loosened before she reached the hall. Agnes recognized relief. Lily said, "Bye, Grandma," already turning toward the door. Tyler waved.

The cookies sat on Agnes's nightstand. They were almond cookies from the H Mart on Route 27. Lily had eaten two and Tyler had eaten four, peeling the wax paper wrappers off with sticky fingers. Agnes would eat the rest over the coming week, slowly, because they were good cookies and because Helen, despite everything, still remembered which ones she liked. This was the fact Agnes could not reconcile. Rage had a classification, a shelf location, a clear label. The cookies did not.

---

Agnes waited three days.

She had been watching Carmen Reyes since the confrontation with Harry, though not directly, because direct attention from a woman classified as nonverbal would draw notice. So Agnes watched peripherally, as she had monitored a pharmacy while counting pills. The skill transferred.

Carmen was performing her own uncertainty. She came to work, did her job, administered medications with the same competent warmth she always showed, and then hesitated. She lingered a beat too long at certain doorways. She spent an extra second checking vitals she had already checked. She let her glance at Agnes linger before remembering to look away. Carmen was deciding something. Agnes knew the look. It was the face customers wore when they were working up to admitting they could not afford the prescription.

On Wednesday evening, Carmen came to Room 108A for the seven o'clock medication pass. Agnes's standing orders were atorvastatin ten milligrams and aspirin eighty-one. The aspirin was appropriate. The statin had been added without a lipid panel.

Carmen knocked on the open door. "Evening, Mrs. Chen. Got your meds." She came in with the medication cup, the water, the clipboard. She was professional, warm, slightly too careful. She glanced at Doris, who was asleep. She set the cup on Agnes's tray table.

Agnes was in the chair by the window. The window faced the parking lot and, beyond it, three sugar maples. In the early dark they were only shapes. She turned her head and looked at Carmen.

There was no performance in it. Her gaze did not drift. She looked at Carmen directly, with the full weight of professional attention. It was the look Agnes had once used on prescription labels and Board investigators.

Carmen stopped moving.

Agnes took off her glasses, cleaned them on the hem of her shirt — an old habit from behind the counter, a way to buy three seconds before speaking — and put them back on. She said: "Close the door."

Her voice was dry from twelve hours of daytime silence. At night, during ring meetings, she spoke freely, but her daytime vocal cords had been resting and the sound came out slightly hoarse. She cleared her throat once. She did not repeat the instruction.

Carmen closed the door. She did it slowly, her hand on the handle, her body held in the cautious suspension of a nurse encountering a sudden change in a patient's presentation. When she turned back, Agnes could see the recalibration happening in real time: the clinical training, the assessment instincts, running this new information against three years of chart notes that said this woman could not speak.

"Sit," Agnes said.

Carmen sat on the edge of Doris's bed. Doris did not stir. Carmen put the clipboard in her lap and looked at Agnes with controlled alarm, the face of a clinician not yet sure whether a dramatic change in status was a crisis or a discovery.

"You can talk," Carmen said.

"I have always been able to talk."

Carmen's hand moved to the bedrail. "Since when — is this a neurological change? I need to assess whether —"

"It is not a change." Agnes waited until Carmen's hand stopped reaching. "I had a stroke. Broca's aphasia. The speech recovered in six months. That was three years ago."

"Three years." Carmen said it flat. "You've been — how did nobody — you scored what on your cognitive screen?"

"Thirty out of thirty. Mini-Mental. First year. It is in my chart."

"Your chart says oriented times zero."

"Yes."

Carmen looked at the medication cup on the tray table. She was doing what nurses did when the clinical picture shifted: reassessing, recategorizing, trying to fit new data into an existing framework. Agnes watched her fail to fit it.

"Your daughter," Carmen said.

"My daughter."

A silence. Carmen's hand went to her badge, adjusted it — the automatic motion Agnes had seen her make during medication counts that did not add up. Then Carmen said, "I should be notifying the attending. A verbal status change — I'm supposed to document and call —"

"You will not be notifying anyone."

Carmen stopped.

"The statin on my med list," Agnes said. "No lipid panel was ordered. He prescribed by age and the facility formulary. The aspirin is appropriate. I take the aspirin."

Carmen studied her. Agnes could see the professional recognition happening, one clinician registering another. "You've been — you're the one who does the inventory. Harry said someone tracked the expirations and the —"

"Yes."

"What does that mean? What are you tracking?"

"Expirations. Drug interactions. Buyer contraindications." Agnes held the pace. "Three months ago I flagged a hydroxychloroquine interaction with an aluminum-based antacid. I relayed it through Marcus."

"You relayed it through —" Carmen's voice went flat. "You're screening people."

Agnes pointed toward the bed. "Four notebooks. Inventory, transaction dates, buyer profiles, refusals."

Carmen looked at the bed, then back at Agnes. "You keep written records. Of all of it. In the building."

"An unmonitored pharmaceutical operation is an unsafe one."

Carmen said nothing for several seconds. Then: "Why are you telling me this now?"

"Because your deliberation is visible. You spend six extra seconds at this door every shift. You linger in the hallway after my vitals."

"That's my job. I check on every resident on this —"

"You do not check Mrs. Okonkwo's pulse twice. You do not stand outside 114A after the med pass. You stand outside this door."

Carmen's hands tightened on the clipboard in her lap. When she spoke, the warmth was gone from her voice. "You don't know what I'm risking right now." She stopped, then started again. "I could lose my license. My kid depends on this job."

"You could have called the police when you caught the counts. You did not."

"That's not the same as —"

"You gave Harry a week. You accommodated the counts."

"I didn't accommodate anything." Carmen's voice thinned. "I told him to stop."

"And then you did not report."

Carmen said nothing.

"Come to the meeting," Agnes said. "Look at what is in front of you. Then decide."

Carmen did not move.

After a while Carmen picked up the medication cup. She looked at the two pills, one larger, one smaller, both white. She looked at Agnes.

"Can you tell which is which?" Carmen asked. "Without the packaging."

Agnes glanced at the cup. "The round one, seven millimeters, scored on one side, is the atorvastatin, ten milligrams, generic. The smaller tablet, unscored, slight bevel on the edge, is the aspirin, eighty-one milligrams. The atorvastatin has a manufacturer's imprint you cannot see from this angle."

Carmen separated the aspirin from the statin and held it out. Agnes took it and washed it down with the water. The statin remained on the tray.

"I'll chart that you took both," Carmen said.

"I know."

Carmen stood. She picked up the clipboard and the cup with the remaining pill and moved toward the door. She stopped with her hand on the handle.

"When's the next meeting?"

"Thursday. One in the morning. Harry will expect you."

Carmen opened the door and stepped into the hallway, and her posture reassembled itself: shoulders settling into the practiced ease of a nurse moving between rooms, face smoothing into professional composure. Five seconds later Agnes heard the rubber squeak of her soles heading for the next med cup.

She turned back to the window. The parking lot lights made orange pools on the asphalt. The sugar maples were dark shapes against a darker sky. Behind her, Doris's breathing cycled: the click, the pause, the drain. This one lasted nine seconds. Agnes sat in the chair and waited to see whether Carmen Reyes would document, attend, or do both.


# Chapter 9

On Thursday morning, the flowers arrived at seven-fifteen, which was how Carmen knew the state was coming.

In thirteen months at Lakeview Gardens, she had never seen a living plant in the lobby. The silk ficus by the reception desk didn't count. It had a coating of dust thick enough to write in, and someone had once hung a pine-tree air freshener from one of its branches like an ornament on the world's saddest Christmas tree. But these were real flowers. Grocery-store carnations in a plastic vase, pink and white, their stems already starting to bend from being jammed together too tightly. They sat on the front desk next to the sign-in clipboard like an apology nobody had asked for.

Cliff Moorehead materialized from his office at seven-twenty, which by itself would have counted as a warning. Carmen had worked here for over a year and could count on one hand the number of times she'd seen the administrator before nine. He was wearing a polo shirt she hadn't seen before, navy and still creased from the package, and khakis with an ironed pleat down each leg. He kept smoothing one hand over the new shirt and resetting his mouth into a customer-service smile that slid off as soon as nobody gave it back.

"All right, everyone." He clapped his hands once in the hallway outside the nurse's station, the sound flat and managerial, like he'd practiced it on the drive in. "Surveyors are here at ten. I need all documentation current by nine. All documentation. MDS assessments, care plans, medication records, incident reports. If it's not up to date, it doesn't exist."

Tammy, passing with a stack of linens balanced against her hip, didn't break stride. "So half our patients don't exist."

"Tammy."

"I'm just saying. You want me to chart or you want me to change Mrs. Patterson, because she's been sitting in it since six and I only got two hands."

Cliff's mouth worked for a moment, opening and closing around words that didn't arrive. "Chart first. Then yes. Prioritize."

"I'm prioritizing Mrs. Patterson's sacrum, Cliff. She's got a Stage Two on her tailbone and she needs turning every two hours and she gets it every three because you won't hire another aide for this wing."

"We're not discussing staffing right now."

"We're never discussing staffing. That's the point." Tammy turned the corner with her linens. Cliff stood in the hallway for another five seconds, then retreated to his office. A moment later, Carmen heard the printer start up, the heavy, grinding sound it made when someone was printing more than ten pages at once.

By eight-thirty, the transformation was visible. Cliff, or someone he'd deputized, had taped a motivational poster to the wall outside the dining room. It featured a sunrise over mountains with the words QUALITY CARE STARTS WITH YOU in a font that suggested clip art and a 3 AM deadline. The edges were still curling from the printer heat. It had been positioned directly over a scuff mark that had been on the wall since before Carmen started, and it covered the scuff but not the nail hole above it, so the effect was less "inspirational workspace" and more "someone is trying very hard."

The carnations had already begun to wilt.

Carmen moved through her morning routines at her steadiest speed: eight o'clock medications, vitals, the blood sugar check on Mr. Andersen in Wing B, the dressing change on Mrs. Patterson's pressure ulcer. No wasted motion, no second looks at the MAR, every task already placed where it needed to go. She'd worked a state survey at her previous facility in Edison, a smaller place that got dinged for medication storage temperatures and a bathroom handrail loose enough to spin in its bracket. Surveyors came annually, theoretically unannounced, though facilities always seemed to know within hours of the survey team's departure from their regional office. Cliff had sent a staff email at four-twelve in the morning. Carmen's phone had buzzed on her nightstand while Reggie slept.

She was not worried about the surveyors finding the ring's operation. The ring left no physical evidence in accessible places. Agnes had insisted on that from the beginning, with the unbending precision of a pharmacist who'd spent forty-two years understanding exactly what regulatory scrutiny looked like. No pills in drawers, no cash in envelopes, no notebooks in rooms during the day. Everything moved through Marcus and out the building within hours of a meeting. The prepaid phone was powered down and hidden inside a heating vent cover in Harry's room, behind a screw Agnes had replaced with one that turned by hand.

What worried her was the medication count log.

She had documented every discrepancy herself. She'd written her initials and her license number next to each one, because that was the procedure, and Carmen followed procedure. Six documented variances in the past three months. All on her shifts, all under her name, all explained in the notes column with plausible fictions the charge nurse had taught her to write before Carmen understood she was covering for something larger than charting errors: documentation error, shift-change miscommunication, pharmacy delivery discrepancy. Each explanation was something that actually happened at understaffed facilities with hybrid paper-electronic systems and overnight nurses who'd been hired two weeks ago. None of them were true.

A competent surveyor would see the pattern. Not necessarily what the pattern meant, but that a pattern existed, and that it centered on one nurse.

She'd known this was coming since the moment she stopped counting honestly. She'd just managed not to picture it as a day with an hour attached. And now the date was here, and the carnations were dying in the lobby, and Cliff's motivational poster was peeling off the wall.

Dr. Prasad arrived at nine for her biweekly rounds, moving through Wing A with the efficient speed of a physician who saw forty patients a day across three facilities and couldn't afford to linger. She didn't seem to know about the survey. If she knew, she didn't change her rhythm. She reviewed charts, wrote orders, asked Carmen about Mrs. Patterson's wound progression and Mr. Andersen's morning glucose, and was gone by nine-forty-five. Carmen envied her. Prasad was a doctor. She came, she prescribed, she left. She didn't sit in the building for eight hours knowing that her license number was on a stack of documents that told a story she'd invented.

---

The surveyor's name was Linda Horvath. She was about fifty, with gray-brown hair cut short enough to be practical and long enough that one section kept slipping forward; every few minutes she shoved it behind her ear with two impatient fingers and kept moving. Her shoes told Carmen everything: black, rubber-soled, scuffed pale at the toes, ugly and clearly comfortable. Hallway shoes. State shoes. She carried a binder thick enough to stop a door, a clipboard with a pen clipped to the top, and a stainless steel coffee thermos she refilled twice from the pot Cliff had set up in the conference room. Real brewed coffee, not the instant packets they usually had. Another tell that the state was in the building.

She brought a second surveyor, a younger woman named Keisha who handled the dietary and environmental review while Horvath took clinical. Carmen saw Keisha photographing the dining room steam table with her phone, then crouching to check the temperature gauge on the reach-in cooler, writing something in her own binder. Cliff hovered near both of them with the energy of a man trying to appear relaxed while standing on a nail.

"Can I get you anything?" he asked Horvath for the third time in twenty minutes. "Water? I set up coffee in the conference room."

"I'm fine, Mr. Moorehead."

"Cliff. Please."

Horvath didn't use his first name. She opened her binder and began with resident interviews at eleven.

Carmen was doing her late-morning med pass when Horvath entered Room 114A. Harry was in his wheelchair by the window, wearing the blue cardigan he wore every day, staring at the parking lot with his mouth slightly open and his eyes fixed past the employee cars, as if the room had failed to reach him. He'd been in this posture when Carmen had come through with his morning medications at eight, pills set in the cup, water on the tray table, Harry taking them without looking at her, swallowing on reflex, the way he accepted a blanket adjustment or a blood-pressure cuff. She'd watched him do it and had the strange doubled experience of seeing an act she knew was an act performed so well it looked like the thing it was imitating.

Now Horvath sat in the vinyl visitor's chair, the one that exhaled a small puff of disinfectant-scented air when weight hit it, and opened her binder.

"Mr. Zielinski? I'm Linda, from the state health department. I just have a few questions for you today."

A pause. Then Harry's voice, querulous and thin, the voice of a man calling across a distance only he could see: "Eleanor?"

"No, sir. I'm Linda. From the health department. Can you tell me what today's date is?"

Another pause, longer. Harry seemed to be considering the question with genuine effort, his mouth working slightly as though the answer was a physical thing he was trying to locate in a cluttered room. "It's... March? Wait. Is it Tuesday?"

"Can you tell me what year it is?"

"Seventy-nine." He said it with sudden confidence, sitting up slightly in the wheelchair, and his voice shifted from lost to certain in a way that was startling. "Seventy-nine, and I'll tell you something, the Yanks are done. Reggie Jackson can't carry them without Munson. Thurman went down in that plane and that was the whole season."

Horvath's pen moved across her clipboard. "Do you know the name of the president?"

"Carter." Harry leaned forward. The words started crowding each other, fresh outrage all over them, like he'd just snapped the paper open at the breakfast table and found somebody willing to hear him out. "Carter, and he's losing Iran in front of everybody. Those people in Tehran are still stuck in that embassy and he looks like he doesn't know where to put his hands. You know what I paid for gas last week? A dollar and two cents at the Exxon on Route 18, and I still sat there half an hour. Eleanor said I should've gone Tuesday. She's right about Tuesdays. Where's Eleanor?"

"I'm not sure who Eleanor is, Mr. Zielinski."

"She was just here. She comes at, what time is it? She brings the thing. There's a thing she brings." He gestured vaguely at the nightstand. "With the..."

"That's all right. Can you tell me the name of this place? Where you are right now?"

Harry looked around Room 114A as though seeing it for the first time. His gaze traveled across the adjustable bed, the nightstand with its box of tissues and plastic water pitcher, the window with its view of the employee parking lot. He seemed baffled by all of it, not frightened, not distressed, just genuinely unable to reconcile his surroundings with whatever internal geography he was navigating.

"Is this the... no." He rubbed his face. His hand trembled slightly, a tremor Carmen had never seen from him before, which meant it was part of the performance, added today for the surveyor's benefit. "I don't. Where's Eleanor? She'd know."

Carmen stood at the sub-station near the Wing A entrance, charting vitals she'd already charted, and listened. Nine minutes. Horvath spent nine minutes with Harry Zielinski, and Carmen timed it because she couldn't help herself, the way you watch a tightrope walker and count the seconds not because you want them to fall but because you need to know how long a person can sustain the impossible.

Nine minutes, and Horvath never stopped writing. Harry gave her Munson's plane crash, gas lines at an Exxon on Route 18, the embassy in Tehran, and Eleanor six separate times, each return to the name bent a little differently from the one before. Carmen drew one unit too much insulin for Mr. Andersen, caught it, and pushed the excess back into the vial without taking her eyes off Harry's door.

When Horvath left Room 114A, Carmen was at the medication cart, drawing up an insulin dose for Mr. Andersen. She glanced toward Harry's doorway. In the small window above the door handle, she could see his reflection in the glass. He was watching Horvath walk away, and his face was completely still. The man in 1979 was gone. The one running the act was back. He held the stillness for two seconds, three, then let his face slacken again, and the transition was so smooth it was like watching water close over a stone.

---

Agnes was next. Room 108A.

Her roommate, Doris, eighty-three and deep into Alzheimer's, was asleep in the bed by the window, her mouth open, her breathing cycling through a wet click, a pause, and a long drain that carried into the hallway. Agnes sat in the chair by her own bed, hands folded in her lap, eyes fixed on a point approximately fourteen inches above the floor. She did not look up when Horvath entered.

"Mrs. Chen? I'm Linda, from the state health department."

Nothing.

"Mrs. Chen, can you hear me?"

Agnes's eyes moved, not to Horvath but to a different point on the floor, six inches to the right of the first point. Her expression didn't change. She might have been watching a crack in the linoleum. She might have been watching nothing at all.

"Can you tell me your name?"

Agnes opened her mouth. She made a sound: not quite a word, not quite silence. Something between a sigh and a syllable, like a radio tuned to the space between stations. Then she closed her mouth and resumed staring.

Horvath tried three more questions, spacing them out with the patience of a professional who'd been trained to give silence room. Agnes responded to none of them. At one point she raised her right hand, the one with the slight weakness from her stroke that she normally overrode with sheer pharmaceutical precision, and touched her own face with the slow, exploratory gesture of someone encountering their own features for the first time. She ran her fingers along her jaw, her cheek, the bridge of her nose. Then her hand returned to her lap.

Four minutes. Horvath wrote a note, closed her binder, and left.

Carmen was passing in the hallway with a blood pressure cuff, heading toward 110A, and she caught a two-second glimpse through the closing door. Agnes was watching Horvath's back with the focused, evaluative expression of a pharmacist watching a technician count pills. Not hostile, not amused, just assessing. Her eyes were completely lucid, her posture alert. The room smelled like Doris's incontinence pads. Then the door swung shut and the glimpse was gone.

---

Lola was in the dayroom when Horvath found her.

She'd positioned herself in the chair nearest the window, wearing a pink sweater with a small stain on the collar, coffee, Carmen thought, and probably deliberate. Her gold crucifix caught the late-morning light through the window. Her hands were wrapped around a rosary, the beads threaded between her fingers, and the tremor in her right hand made the beads click against each other in a soft, arrhythmic pattern, like a clock that had lost its mechanism but kept trying.

"Mrs. Fernandez? I'm Linda, from the state health department."

"Oh!" Lola looked up with such genuine-seeming delight that even Carmen, who knew the act, felt a moment of warmth. "Mija, sit down, sit. You want coffee? The coffee here isn't, well, it's not coffee, it's more like, you know my husband, Tomás, he used to make café con leche, real café, from scratch, his mother's recipe, she was from, where was she from?" Lola looked at the ceiling. "Matanzas? No, that's not right. Somewhere near there. A town outside Matanzas. There was a bakery. She always talked about this bakery."

"Mrs. Fernandez, I just need to ask you a few questions."

"And the bread, Dios mío, she said the bread was so good you could cry, and Tomás always said she exaggerated about Cuba, you know how sons are about their mothers, but I think she didn't, I think the bread was exactly that good, because when we went to Miami for his cousin's wedding, when was that? Was that before the children or after? No, it was after, because Elena was walking already. She had those little shoes."

Horvath tried again. "Mrs. Fernandez?"

"Pink shoes, with a buckle, and Tomás said they were too expensive but I said what is too expensive for your daughter's feet? Forty dollars. For shoes she'd outgrow in six months and he acted like I'd bought a Cadillac. Men." Lola shook her head with fond exasperation, then turned to Horvath with sudden attention. "Are you married, mija?"

"Yes."

"He treats you good?"

"Mrs. Fernandez, I need to ask about your medications."

"You look tired. They work you too hard, I can see it. You eat today? You're thin. Not too thin, you look nice, but thin. Tomás's mother, she always said..."

Something shifted in Horvath's face. Not suspicion. Just the small reset of a professional recognizing a conversation she wasn't getting back. She tried once more. "Do you know what medications you take?"

"Medications?" Lola's face went soft with confusion, not Harry's elaborate temporal displacement but something warmer, as if she'd simply wandered off her own sentence and expected to find it again in a minute. "The pills? Oh, the pills. They give me, the girl gives me, what's her name, la morena, the pretty one..."

"Do you know what the pills are for?"

"For?" Lola held up her tremoring right hand, the rosary beads clicking. "This, I think. For the shaking. And for my, for sleeping. And there's a white one, a big white one that I don't like because it's, Tomás, you know, he never took pills. Not one. Not even aspirin. He said his mother taught him that the body knows how to heal itself, which, you know." She leaned closer to Horvath, conspiratorial. "He was wrong about that. Obviously. But you couldn't tell him."

Twenty-two minutes.

Carmen knew because she was in the dayroom charting vitals for the Wing B residents and couldn't leave without making it obvious she was watching. By the end, Horvath's handwriting had shrunk to the size of pill-count notations. Lola had given her a bakery near Matanzas, Elena's pink shoes, a cousin's wedding in Miami that kept starting over, and Tomás's opinions about pills. She had not given her one answer Horvath could use.

Horvath's handwriting got smaller and faster. She checked her watch twice. The second time, she closed her binder with a finality that was courteous but absolute.

"Thank you, Mrs. Fernandez. You've been very helpful."

"You're leaving? But I didn't finish telling you about the bakery. In Matanzas. Or near Matanzas. I don't remember."

"Maybe next time."

"You come back, mija. And eat something. Promise me."

Horvath walked out of the dayroom fast, binder tucked tight against her side. She tucked her hair behind her ears. Her pen clicked against the binder three times in rapid succession.

Carmen looked at Lola. Lola was looking down at the rosary in her hands, turning the beads with her tremoring fingers, her mouth slack now that there was no one to charm. Then she saw Carmen watching and lifted her face into the smile again.

---

Earl and Burt were interviewed together in Room 116A because Horvath was behind schedule.

Earl was in his bed, propped up on two pillows, a three-month-old issue of Time open on his lap to an article about western water rights he'd already read. When Horvath entered, he gave her one flat look, then turned a page he wasn't reading. He coughed, the deep, wet rattle that Carmen had been hearing more of lately, a cough that came from somewhere below the chest and took its time leaving, and settled back against the pillow.

"Mr. Washington, can you tell me today's date?"

Earl looked at her. He looked at the magazine. He looked at her again. Then he closed his eyes.

Horvath waited. She was good at waiting. Carmen could see that. She let the silence sit without rescuing it, pen loose in one hand, eyes on Earl's face.

Earl's breathing deepened. His head tilted slightly left. Within thirty seconds, he appeared to be asleep. He wasn't. Carmen could see from the hallway the way his fingers still held the magazine's edges, gripping rather than releasing, but Horvath couldn't see that from the visitor's angle, and the performance was more than good enough for a surveyor with sixteen facilities left this quarter.

Burt, in the bed by the window, was a different kind of quiet.

His hands lay on top of the thermal blanket, long-fingered and mostly still. Every few seconds the first three fingers of his right hand pressed down in a small remembered pattern, as if the valves of a trumpet were still under them. Then they went still again.

"Mr. Kovacs?" Horvath leaned forward, speaking clearly. "Can you tell me your name?"

Burt looked at her. His eyes moved across her face the way they moved across most faces now, searching, not quite finding, the effort of recognition visible but the result uncertain. He was alert in the way a person could be alert and lost at the same time.

"Erzsi?" he said softly. Then, in Hungarian, a string of syllables that came out tender and questioning, all of it meant for a woman not in the room.

"I'm Linda. Can you tell me where you are?"

"The music is..." Burt trailed off. He seemed to listen to something that existed only for him. His head tilted, his fingers twitched on the blanket, and for a moment his face held the concentration of a man hearing a passage he wanted to remember. Then the concentration dissolved into something like distress. "Where's the thing? The book. My book."

Horvath wrote a notation. She asked two more questions. Burt answered one with a fragment about Erzsébet that trailed into Hungarian and ignored the other entirely, staring at his own hands as if they'd been attached to his wrists by someone else and he was still adjusting to the arrangement.

Six minutes. The fastest interview of the day.

Horvath closed the binder on Earl's nap and turned back to Burt, who was still working those same three fingers into the blanket in careful little presses. She waited for something clearer from him. It didn't come.

Carmen went into 116A after Horvath left, ostensibly to check vitals. Earl opened one eye.

"She gone?"

"She's gone."

Earl grunted and reopened his magazine. Burt was still looking at his hands.

When Carmen took Burt's wrist for a pulse, he said "Erzsi?" again, softer this time, and let her turn his hand over as if it belonged to somebody else.

---

Horvath found Carmen at the sub-station at two-fifteen, after lunch.

Carmen had been expecting this. Not with dread, exactly, but with the hard, narrowed focus she used when a task required precision under pressure and she couldn't afford to hesitate. She'd rehearsed nothing. Rehearsing would have made her sound rehearsed, and Horvath had been doing this too long to miss the difference between a nurse who was remembering and a nurse who was reciting.

"Ms. Reyes, I'd like to go over some of your medication management documentation."

"Okay." Carmen pulled the second chair out from behind the sub-station desk. "What do you need?"

Horvath sat and opened her binder to a tabbed section. Carmen could see the photocopies: her count sheets, her handwriting, her initials, her license number stamped at the top of each page.

"I'm seeing some variances in your controlled substance counts." Horvath ran her finger down the column without losing her place once, pausing each time Carmen's initials appeared. "Six documented discrepancies over the past three months. Oxycodone, alprazolam, tramadol. All occurring on your shifts."

Carmen's hands were on the desk, fingers laced. She'd placed them there before Horvath had started talking, deliberately, because if she held a pen she'd click it, and if her fingers found the badge clip she'd square it against her scrub top, and her hands would do the things they did when she was lying and she didn't trust them not to.

"August twenty-second." Horvath tapped one photocopied line with her pen. "Oxycodone, five milligrams. Paper count nineteen, electronic count twenty-two. Your note says overnight documentation error. Walk me through the three-tablet gap."

Carmen felt the back of her neck go damp. "That one was the agency night nurse. eMAR froze again around six, so she counted off the paper narc book and never back-entered before shift change. I caught it at seven-fifteen on handoff. Three tablets gone from the card, electronic still showing twenty-two."

Horvath flipped a page. "September third. Alprazolam, one milligram. Pharmacy delivery discrepancy, corrected invoice to follow. I don't have the invoice."

"It would've gone to admin, not the narc book." Carmen kept her hands laced harder. "Pharmacy sent a short card. Seventy-eight in a ninety-count blister pack. I called from the med room, they confirmed the misfill, and the corrected invoice got faxed up front after lunch. Cliff should have it with the vendor paperwork."

Horvath looked up. "And the two unresolved variances? July eleventh and September seventeenth."

"Tramadol on July eleventh. Oxycodone on September seventeenth." Carmen heard herself getting too crisp and made herself breathe. "I re-counted both with the charge nurse and still came up short. So I documented them as unresolved and emailed Mr. Moorehead, because that was the cleanest thing I could do with it."

"And all six still happened on your shifts."

"Because I count them on my shifts." Carmen kept her eyes on Horvath's binder, not Horvath's face. "If the number's bad at handoff, it lands on me whether I caused it or not."

"The protocol says to notify your administrator."

"I did. On the two unresolveds. In writing." This part was true. Carmen had sent Cliff two emails, dated and timestamped. Cliff had not responded to either one, because Cliff did not respond to things that might require him to do something.

Horvath looked at her. No accusation. Then she dropped back to the photocopies, ran the six entries again from top to bottom, tapped the line about the missing invoice, and glanced toward Cliff's office as if measuring whether the walk and the argument were worth it. Her watch caught the fluorescent light when she turned her wrist. She checked the time, rubbed under one eye with the heel of her hand, wrote a cramped note in the margin, and flipped to a fresh page.

"I'm going to note the documentation gaps as a finding," Horvath said. "You'll need to implement a reconciliation process for the paper-electronic crossover. Both formats should be updated simultaneously at the point of count."

"Absolutely. I've actually been talking to our charge nurse about that."

She hadn't. She wouldn't.

Horvath made a final note, closed her binder, and stood. "Your medication administration records are thorough, Ms. Reyes. I can see you take the documentation seriously."

"Thank you."

"The discrepancies are a concern, but the documentation trail supports your explanations. I'll include recommendations in the report."

Carmen nodded. Smiled, not too much, just the professional acknowledgment of a review that had gone well. Horvath gathered her thermos and binder, tucked her hair behind both ears simultaneously, and headed toward the conference room where Cliff was waiting in a chair he'd positioned to face the door, shoulders bunched and already braced.

Carmen sat at the sub-station for another minute after Horvath left. She unlaced her fingers and found that they'd been pressing against each other hard enough to leave white marks on her knuckles. She flexed them until the color came back.

---

The survey concluded at four with a stack of findings that would keep Cliff busy for two weeks: the medication documentation gaps that Carmen had invented a reconciliation process for, a dietary storage temperature variance Keisha had photographed, two overdue care plan updates, a hand sanitizer dispenser that was empty, and the motivational poster, which Keisha had noted was covering a fire safety placard in violation of corridor clearance standards. Minor deficiencies. Mild enough to let a facility keep operating without penalty while generating enough paperwork to prove the state health department was performing its function.

Cliff ordered pizza. Three boxes from a place on Route 1: pepperoni, plain, and something with roasted vegetables that no one touched. He set them on the break room table with a two-liter of warm Pepsi and a sleeve of paper cups and told the staff they'd done a great job. He used those exact words. "Great job, everybody." He said it twice. He didn't mention Mrs. Patterson's pressure ulcer, or the staffing ratio that made it inevitable, or the fact that the facility's survey score was a number that measured documentation completeness rather than whether anyone in the building was actually being cared for.

Tammy took two slices of pepperoni and ate them standing by the break room window, watching the surveyors' state sedan back out of its parking space.

"Flowers are dead," she said, chewing.

"They're not dead," Cliff said. "They're wilting."

"They're dead, Cliff. Rest in peace."

Carmen didn't eat. She poured a cup of the warm Pepsi, drank half, and tipped the rest into the break room sink. She finished her shift. She charted the afternoon medications, updated Mrs. Patterson's wound assessment. The ulcer was the same, neither better nor worse, which was what happened when you turned someone every three hours instead of two. Then she restocked the medication cart for the evening nurse. Normal work. Work she could do with her hands and the front of her mind while the rest of her sat somewhere else entirely, in a place she couldn't yet look at directly.

At six-forty, she clocked out. She walked to her car. She sat in the driver's seat and put the key in the ignition.

She did not turn it.

The parking lot was half-empty, day shift gone, evening shift trickling in, the gap visible in the spaces between cars. Late September light came in low and amber through the windshield, flattening everything into its own photograph. A plastic bag skidded across the asphalt and caught on a shopping cart someone had left near the dumpster. The courtyard trees were starting to turn, just barely, the first suggestions of yellow at the edges of leaves that had been green all summer.

Carmen sat with her hands on the steering wheel, her key in the ignition, her foot on nothing, and looked at the building she'd spent the day protecting.

Beige stucco. Small windows with institutional blinds, half of them crooked. A sign that read LAKEVIEW GARDENS REHABILITATION AND NURSING CENTER in letters that had faded from green to a color that was no longer any color in particular. No lake. No garden. Three scrubby trees in a paved courtyard and a view of Route 1 if you stood on your toes.

Inside that building, Harry Zielinski was either staring at the parking lot or staring at 1979. Agnes Chen was in her room with Doris breathing in the bed by the window, the click, the pause, the drain. Lola Fernandez had her rosary. Earl Washington had his magazine. Burt Kovacs was still studying his hands.

Carmen Reyes, LPN, license number 26NR03891, had lied to the state to protect a controlled-substance diversion operation running out of Lakeview Gardens.

What stayed in her body was the voice she'd used for it. It was the same calm voice she used for med pass, the same pauses, the same dry annoyance at other people's paperwork. Her mother was calling about Reggie. Carmen let the phone ring out. She sat there fifteen minutes while the amber light went gray and the vinyl under her palms cooled. When the screen lit again, she put the key the rest of the way in, started the car, backed out of her space, and drove to her mother's house to pick up her son.


# Chapter 10

The TV room's fluorescent tube buzzed from a loose ballast when Carmen pushed the door open at one o'clock. Harry sat in his wheelchair at the card table — the one the activities aide used for bingo on Tuesdays — already waiting. Agnes sat across from him. Lola was at her post by the hallway door, one hand resting on the gold crucifix at her throat, her other hand still in the tremor that worsened at night. Earl sat in the vinyl recliner by the far window with his eyes closed, though Carmen could tell from the set of his shoulders that he was listening.

Three days after the survey — three days after she sat in her car unable to drive, feeling the weight of what she'd told the state surveyor settle into a place she couldn't reach to remove it — she'd found Harry alone in the TV room during evening med pass, leaned against the doorframe while Lola pretended to watch a game show behind him, and said, "Show me all of it."

Harry had said one o'clock. Nothing else.

Four composition books sat under the white buzz, black-and-white marbling gone soft at the corners, Agnes's handwriting packed so tight and even it looked typeset. Agnes did not look up when Carmen pulled a vinyl chair to the table and sat. She kept sorting pill bottles into short rows, labels turned the same way, caps aligned. There were yellow stickers on some caps, red dots on others, a system Carmen did not know yet. The room smelled like dust cooked under fluorescent heat, stale coffee, and the sweet medicinal film that always clung to Agnes's hands no matter how much sanitizer she used.

"You said all of it," Harry said.

He pushed the top notebook toward her.

"Good," Carmen said. "Then don't give me the pitch."

She opened to the marked page. The spread looked less like a confession than a set of books. Agnes had ruled the paper into columns with a pen so sharp it had scarred the cardboard underneath: MONTH, GROSS, MARCUS, JACK, DENISE W., CLINIC, BURT, AGNES, RESERVE. At the bottom she had boxed the summary twice in red: 18 MONTHS. AVG GROSS 4,000. TOTAL 48,000 +/-. On the right-hand page she had copied out the most recent full month in cleaner block letters:

4,000
1,200
500
400
350
500
400
650

Nothing about it looked frantic. Nothing about it looked improvised. It looked like somebody's side business from a garage, except the raw material had come through med carts and return bins and under her license.

"Eighteen months," Carmen said.

"Since early last year," Harry said.

She ran her finger down the newest month. "Thirty percent to Marcus."

"Twelve hundred," Harry said. "Distribution, gas, exposure."

"Exposure is not a cost center."

Agnes capped a bottle without looking up. "It is if you want the medication to leave the building."

"Twelve hundred is still high."

"Then drive it yourself," Harry said.

Carmen looked up. "I'm here, aren't I."

Harry did not answer that. He sat very still with his hands folded on the table. The stillness was one of the things that made him frightening. He never looked like he was working to control himself. He looked already controlled.

She tapped the next line. "Jack gets five hundred. Your grandson."

"Yes."

"For what."

"Landscaping business in South Brunswick. Fifteen-thousand-dollar hole."

"And he knows where the money comes from?"

Harry waited. The fluorescent buzz filled the space where a softer man would have rushed to explain himself. Finally he said, "He thinks it came from a certificate of deposit I cashed before Lakeview."

"So you lie to him."

"I send him five hundred."

"That's not what I asked."

"No," Harry said. "It isn't."

Earl coughed into his fist, deep and wet. Carmen felt it in her own chest hearing it. Harry kept his eyes on her and said nothing else. The silence did what a speech would have done worse. It left the lie sitting on the table with the notebook.

She went to the next name. "Denise Washington."

Earl opened his eyes. "My daughter."

"Four hundred," Carmen said.

"VA supplement," Earl said. "On paper."

He coughed again, took a second to get his breath, then finished. "Shoes for the girls, school fees, field trips. Heat when PSE&G gets cute."

He closed his eyes again. Conversation over.

Carmen's finger moved to Lola's line. "Three-fifty to a church clinic in Little Havana."

Lola was still looking at the hallway. "Iglesia de la Caridad," she said. "They keep regular meds for people who don't have regular anything. Blood-pressure pills, diabetes meds, inhalers when they can get them. A woman comes in wheezing and what is she supposed to do, mija, pray harder?"

"No return address," Harry said.

"Of course no return address."

Lola's tremoring hand tightened around the crucifix once and settled.

"Burt gets five hundred," Carmen said.

"Compression stockings that actually fit," Harry said. "Hearing-aid batteries. Brand-name carbidopa-levodopa when the facility pharmacy substitutes the generic again and he spends two days nauseated and shaking worse."

"And Agnes gets four."

Agnes said, "Yes."

"For what."

"For being correct."

Carmen looked up from the page. Agnes was still sorting, not smiling, not offering the line as a joke. Under the fluorescent buzz it landed as plain fact. Carmen thought of the white envelope under Agnes's mattress, the children Helen brought every other Sunday, the way Helen used that too-loud careful voice like volume itself could cross whatever she thought had been damaged.

"There's no Harry line," Carmen said.

"I already live on the expense side of the ledger," Harry said. "Move on."

Carmen did not move on. She flipped back one page, then another, then forward. The pattern held. Some months gross dipped into the high three-thousands, some months ran over four, but the spine of it stayed the same: Marcus at thirty percent, the same recipients, the same reserve. It was not random or desperate. It was monthly.

"This is what pisses me off," she said. "Not the notebook. The discipline. You made it look normal."

"It's monthly arithmetic," Harry said.

"It's narcotics diversion."

"And arithmetic."

Agnes slid the second notebook toward Carmen with two fingers. The page already lay open. RESERVE / 650 was written at the top in narrow block letters, and underneath Agnes had listed the uses in hard capitals: MONEY ORDERS, GAS, REPLACE EXPIRED STOCK, BUYER BRIDGE, LOSS.

Carmen tapped the last word. "Loss from what."

Agnes answered this time. "Broken seals, wrong strengths, Marcus stupidity, buyers who lie, replacements when a discontinuation changes back before the pharmacy catches up."

Carmen looked from the word to Agnes. "Say that again."

"An order gets reduced or discontinued. We move too fast. A physician changes course. Omnicare takes a day or two to deliver. If the resident needs the medication back before then, reserve buys retail."

"So somebody in here has gone without."

"Briefly," Agnes said. "Twice."

"Twice too many."

"Correct."

The answer irritated Carmen more than an excuse would have. "You're saying that like it's acceptable."

"I am saying it like it happened."

Harry said, "That is what the reserve is for."

"To cover the times you hurt the wrong person."

"To keep the wrong person from carrying all of it," Harry said.

Carmen stared at LOSS again. The word had more moral weight in Agnes's hand than it would have in anyone else's, not because Agnes was soft but because she was not. A softer person would have called it slippage or error or something else that lied by tone. Agnes had written loss and left it ugly.

"What happened," Carmen asked.

Agnes finally looked up. "One resident went without a bedtime trazodone for a night because a discontinuation turned out to be a dose reduction and not a stop. She did not sleep. She screamed at the television until three in the morning. Another case was Burt. The substitute arrived, not the brand. He vomited twice and refused breakfast. Reserve covered the replacement. I changed the screening rules."

"And you kept going."

"Yes."

"Why."

Harry answered before Agnes did. "Because outside this building people were already going without longer than overnight."

Carmen turned to him. "That doesn't make this clean."

"No," Harry said. "It doesn't."

Nobody in the room rushed to make it cleaner than it was. Lola kept watch at the door. Earl's breathing stayed audible in the recliner, wet on the inhale. Agnes straightened a row of amber bottles until the labels lined up like soldiers. The only thing Carmen could hear besides the ballast was the faraway ice machine kicking on down the hall.

She pulled the newest composition book closer. "Buyer list."

Agnes let go of it. "Read."

Carmen opened to diagnosis headers instead of names: LUPUS-SLE, TYPE 2 DM, CHRONIC PAIN - MUSCULOSKELETAL, HYPERTENSION, HYPERLIPIDEMIA. Under each header Agnes had logged dosage, quantity, frequency, refill cadence, with margin notes in red. There were no names anywhere, no numbers she could call, just conditions, requests, and little pieces of medical history gathered secondhand from parking-lot conversations and pill bottles photographed under bad light.

"No names," Carmen said.

"Need is relevant," Agnes said. "Names are not."

Three entries were marked with red asterisks. This time Carmen did not wait to be directed. She turned to the first and bent over it until she could smell the paper.

The first entry was headed CHRONIC PAIN - MUSCULOSKELETAL above an OXYCODONE REQUEST, with current meds in the margin: clonazepam, trazodone, nightly alcohol use.

"Respiratory suppression risk," Carmen said. "If Marcus hands her oxy on top of clonazepam and she's drinking, he can put her down."

"Yes," Agnes said.

"Did he know that? Did he ask?"

"Not enough."

Harry said, "He asked enough to get forty dollars."

Agnes tapped the margin with one blunt fingernail. "I said no."

"And if you hadn't."

"Then she might not wake up."

There was no performance in Agnes's voice, no emphasis laid on the line to make Carmen absorb it. She said it the same way she would have said a refill was due Friday.

Carmen flipped to the second red mark. The note was shorter and meaner: POST-OP PAIN above an OXYCODONE REQUEST, then Agnes's blunt summary that the buyer had first claimed a husband after shoulder surgery and, on the second contact, an aunt. Same phone. Same quantity request. Ninety preferred. DO NOT SELL.

"She changed relatives."

"Mid-sentence," Agnes said.

"Marcus brought her twice?"

"He thought desperation and lying were the same thing," Harry said.

"They aren't," Carmen said.

"No," Harry said.

"What happened after you cut her off."

Agnes shrugged once. "She found somebody else. That is the usual result."

"And that doesn't bother you."

"Everything bothers me," Agnes said. "That is not the same as saying yes."

Carmen heard the small scrape of Lola's orthopedic shoe pivoting on linoleum. Lola had not moved from the door, but she had angled her body back toward the table now, listening harder.

"You screen from gossip," Carmen said. "From what Marcus remembers and what buyers tell him and what pill bottle he can get a picture of through a windshield. That's not medicine. That's triage done in a parking lot by amateurs."

"Yes," Agnes said. "And still safer than Marcus alone."

"That's a terrible standard."

"It is the one available."

Carmen hated how often that sentence could be true inside Lakeview.

She turned to the third red mark. This one was almost boring in its consistency: LUPUS-SLE, hydroxychloroquine 200 mg, ninety count, same dose, same request interval. Agnes had added: old bottle verified, joint swelling observed, no sedatives, no escalation.

She read it twice, then looked at Harry. "This is the buyer you're sending me to."

"Yes."

"Convenient."

Harry said nothing.

Agnes did. "She is the cleanest case."

"Of course she is."

"Yes."

Carmen waited for Harry to deny that the whole thing had been staged around the safest moral sample on the page. He did not. The silence did not absolve him. It confirmed the calculation.

"So Rosa is your best argument," Carmen said.

"Rosa is one argument," Harry said.

"The easiest one."

"Yes," Agnes said.

"Then why should I see her first."

Harry folded his hands tighter. "Because you asked why anybody buys from us. She can answer that better than I can."

"And the rest of them."

"The rest of them are why Agnes keeps writing NO in red."

Carmen looked down at the pages behind Rosa's. There were more red marks further in. Not as many as there should have been if every buyer lied, not few enough to make the room feel noble. Enough to make the operation look like what it was: not a charity, not a cartel, not even a consistent ethic. A system run by old people with partial information and too much confidence in their own standards.

"What about the ones you say no to," Carmen asked. "They just disappear."

"They go somewhere," Harry said.

"Somewhere worse," Lola said softly from the door. "Usually worse."

That quiet sentence did more damage than a speech. Carmen could picture the alternatives too easily: fake pills pressed in somebody's basement, fentanyl in a baggie, steroids bought from a friend of a friend, Facebook groups full of maybe-medication and maybe-scam. She could also picture the people inside this building whose medications became columns and percentages and reserve losses.
