Both pictures sat there at the same time, refusing to cancel each other out.

Agnes turned another page. "My information is stale by the time Marcus brings it."

Carmen looked up.

"You see order changes first," Agnes said. "Dose reductions, discontinuations, pharmacy substitutions. If I had current lists, I would catch more of this before it reached the lot."

"You're asking me to help."

"I am asking you for current information."

"For a black-market med reconciliation."

"Name it whatever lets you sleep."

"I sleep fine," Carmen said, which was such obvious bullshit Lola made a small sound through her nose.

Harry said, "You don't have to pull anything."

"I know I don't."

"Then don't. Tell Agnes when something changes."

"And then what. You adjust your menu."

Agnes said, "I stop Marcus from handing oxycodone to a woman on clonazepam with a bottle of sauvignon blanc in her refrigerator. If you prefer not to help with that, say so clearly."

Carmen looked at the first red note again: clonazepam, trazodone, nightly alcohol use. The list had no center to it, just old specialists, urgent-care stopgaps, borrowed rides, and whatever the buyer could remember from a doctor who'd retired six years ago. Agnes was right about the risk. Agnes was also asking Carmen to join the record-keeping side of a crime.

"I tell you about changes," Carmen said. "That's it. Dose reductions, discontinuations, substitutions if they matter clinically. I am not pulling pills off carts. I'm not altering charts. I'm not getting cute."

"That is a start," Agnes said.

Before Harry could close the notebook, Carmen reached for the older one and turned it open herself. FACILITY MONTHLY MEDICATION COST PER RESIDENT was written on the left-hand page. UNINSURED RETAIL on the right. The institutional side of the equation was ugly enough to make the numbers feel fictional: monthly medication costs that ran past two thousand dollars for one resident without anybody at corporate blinking because billing abstractions and managed-care discounts kept the pain off-site. On the retail side the same drugs looked like bus fare turned into extortion.

Hydroxychloroquine, 200 mg, ninety count: 389.
Rosuvastatin, 20 mg, thirty count: 212.
Metformin, 500 mg, sixty count: 87.
Oxycodone, 5 mg, thirty count: 241.

Carmen did not need Agnes to narrate the math. She could follow the route herself: physician orders, facility pharmacy, bulk purchasing, med carts, return bins, discontinued cards, delayed pickups, pills piling inside the institution because it cost less to let them accumulate than to verify anybody still needed them. Then the other side of the same road: people outside paying retail with no coverage, cutting tablets, skipping doses, pretending a prescription from last year was still current because a doctor's visit cost money they did not have.

"Surplus on the left," Carmen said.

"Deficit on the right," Agnes said.

"Surplus in the building," Harry said.

"Not in the residents," Carmen said.

Harry gave her a short look that might have been agreement if he had been a man who wasted agreement out loud.

She closed the notebook. The cardboard cover made a dry smack on the table. "You already set up the meeting."

"Thursday," Harry said. "Your shift starts at three. Be in the lot at two-thirty. Silver Civic, far end."

"You picked the clean case."

"Yes," Agnes said.

"And you're still going."

"Yes," Harry said.

"Why."

This time Harry took a second before answering. "Because even the clean case isn't clean. You should see that too."

Carmen handed the composition book back to Agnes. Agnes took it without fumbling, quick and certain, and stacked it under the others. Lola returned her full attention to the hallway. Earl coughed once more, harder this time, and pressed the heel of his hand to his sternum until the spasm passed. Carmen sat another second in the white buzz of the room with the numbers still moving behind her eyes: forty-eight thousand total, four thousand a month, twelve hundred to Marcus, five hundred to Jack, four hundred to Denise, three-fifty to the clinic, five hundred to Burt, four hundred to Agnes, six-fifty to reserve. There was enough ugliness in it to ruin everybody and enough order to make ruin look administrative.

Then she got up and left before any of them could mistake staying seated for agreement.

---

Rosa drove an early 2000s Civic — silver paint going milky at the fenders, a St. Christopher medal on the rearview mirror, a striped beach towel folded over a tear in the passenger seat. She was parked at the far end of Lakeview's lot, away from the entrance and the employee spaces, in the spot where visitors parked when they did not want to be seen arriving or did not want anyone to remember them leaving. Carmen knew the spot. Families used it when they came to admit a parent they would not visit again.

Thursday. Two twenty-five. Carmen had arrived early, crossing the lot in her scrubs with her badge clipped and her bag on her shoulder. The afternoon was warm for late September, holding onto summer even with the courtyard trees starting to turn. A supply van was backed up to the loading dock, beeping every time the driver touched reverse. The air smelled like hot asphalt, diesel, and whatever somebody in dietary had burned at lunch.

Rosa got out of the car when she saw Carmen coming. She was fifty-two and moved like someone whose body charged her for every step. Her dark hair was cut close, practical. She wore a fleece vest over a long-sleeved shirt despite the warmth. Carmen clocked the cold sensitivity before Rosa said a word.

"You're the nurse," Rosa said.

"Yeah."

"Harry said you wanted to talk."

"I wanted to understand the costs."

Rosa gave a small laugh with no humor in it. "Everybody wants to understand the costs once it isn't theirs."

She leaned back into the car, grabbed a CVS bag off the passenger seat, and shut the door with her hip. The bag was fat with papers: receipts, pharmacy printouts, old bottles with labels still stuck to them, the paper trail of being uninsured in America.

"I do this every few months," Rosa said. "I go in and ask again. Same prescription, same question. They say the number out loud, I thank them, I leave, I go sit in my car and want to throw up."

She took out a folded receipt, then another, then a pharmacy estimate printout stapled to a coupon that had still left the price impossible.

"Hydroxychloroquine, two hundred milligrams, ninety count. It was three eighty-nine at CVS this week, three sixty-two at the Walgreens on Route 1 if I used their little loyalty card, and four twelve at Rite Aid before that one closed. Costco was cheaper one month but still over three hundred, and I don't have a membership, so who gives a shit."

"And from Marcus."

"Forty."

The number sat between them in the heat.

"Cash," Rosa said. "Exact if I can manage it. I count the tablets before I drive away."

"You trust him that much."

"Trust." Rosa snorted. "I trust he wants forty dollars and I need the pills. That's what I trust. I don't tell him where I live. He doesn't ask me how my week was. Works out."

She pulled an amber bottle from the bag and handed it to Carmen. The label was almost rubbed white from living in a purse. Hydroxychloroquine sulfate, 200 mg. Directions and refill history old enough to be humiliating.

"Sometimes there's a typed sheet in the bag," Rosa said. "Sometimes it's handwritten. Spacing, expiration dates, don't take it on an empty stomach unless you like nausea, all that. Whoever writes it isn't fucking around."

"Agnes," Carmen said before she meant to.

Rosa lifted one shoulder. "Probably. The old pharmacist lady. All right. She can stay mean if the instructions stay right."

They stood beside the Civic while cars went by on Route 27 and the supply van kept beeping behind them. Wind pushed a paper cup across the lot and trapped it against the curb by Rosa's front tire.

"I was at a dental office in Woodbridge," Rosa said. "Receptionist there, twelve years. I answered phones, chased insurance authorizations, called people to remind them about cleanings they were never going to keep anyway. The dentist retired. The new owner brought his own staff. I got two weeks and a card with a fruit basket on it."

She folded the receipts again along lines she had already memorized.

"No COBRA because it was a small group plan. I checked. Marketplace was five-forty a month for the premium and a three-thousand-dollar deductible before anything useful happened. At the temp agency I was making sixteen an hour. You can do that math faster than I can."

Carmen could. Rent, gas, food, a car that needed a new starter, a utility bill, a premium, a deductible, and a disease that did not care what your hourly wage had been at the dentist's office. Carmen knew that corridor even before she knew the policy language for it. Too much money for Medicaid. Not enough for labs, eye exams, and the pills that kept your hands from swelling and locking up.

"You have a rheumatologist now?" Carmen asked.

Rosa laughed again, still without humor. "I have memories of a rheumatologist."

"Labs."

"Not since last year."

"Eye exams."

"Two years." Rosa looked at her. "I know what the drug can do. I also know what lupus does when I stop taking it. Those are the two options on the menu."

That landed harder on Carmen than the receipts had. Even the clean case was not clean. Rosa was not getting monitored the way she should have been. She was not buying a safe legal substitute. She was buying a partial version of medical care from a parking lot and calling it survival because survival was the honest word.

"What happens when you don't take it," Carmen asked.

Rosa's face changed a little, not softening, just losing patience with the fact that she had to make the case all the way down to symptoms.

"Flares," she said. "Joints first — knees, hands, ankles if I really get lucky. Then fatigue so bad you sit on the edge of the bed and bargain with the room. Mouth ulcers. Fever that isn't a real fever. Everything hurts and none of it looks dramatic enough for anybody to care."

She held up her right hand. The knuckles were swollen, skin stretched shiny over the joints. The inflammation had a wet look to it, as if the hand had been overfilled.

"Last time I ran out before Marcus, I started cutting tablets in half with a pill splitter. Then quarters. Then every other day because I wanted the bottle to keep lying to me a little longer. Three weeks like that and I couldn't open a jar. Couldn't button my pants. Couldn't turn my apartment key without using both hands."

She rested the bad hand on the roof of the Civic and flexed it once, winced, stopped.

"I had just started a warehouse data-entry job. Sitting down work. Easy, right? Except your fingers still have to bend. I called in one day, then another, then a week. They let me go after day four and the temp agency stopped calling back. That's what happens."

She said it flat, like she had told it before to people with clipboards and warm eyes and no power to fix any part of it.

"Can I see your left hand," Carmen asked.

Rosa held it out. Carmen took it. The metacarpophalangeal joints were warm and boggy with active synovitis. She turned the hand gently and felt the swelling over the ring-finger knuckle. Rosa did not flinch, but Carmen could feel the effort of not flinching in the tendons.

For a moment it was only the hand in hers: heat in the joint, fluid in the tissue, the thick tender swell over the ring finger, the stale lotion smell on Rosa's skin, the tiny tremor of somebody holding still because being examined by a stranger in a parking lot was preferable to not being examined at all.

"This one's worse," Carmen said.

"I know."

"You should have imaging."

"With what money."

Carmen let go. Rosa put her hand back in her vest pocket and kept it there.

"Harry told you I was the clean case," Rosa said.

Carmen said nothing.

"He did. I can tell."

"Agnes did."

"Same difference."

Rosa leaned against the car again. Her body looked tired in the specific way Carmen recognized from patients who spent all day negotiating with pain instead of resting from it.

"Look, I'm not buying oxy in a Walmart lot," Rosa said. "I'm not nodding off in my car. I'm trying to stay on the med I was prescribed so my hands keep working long enough to get another job with benefits before my body gets even dumber ideas."

"That still doesn't make this safe."

"No," Rosa said. "It doesn't."

"You could be getting bad storage, expired stock, wrong manufacturer, no monitoring, no nothing."

"Yes."

"You know that."

"I'm standing in a nursing-home parking lot buying prescription drugs from somebody's nephew. Of course I know that."

Carmen didn't have an answer for that.

"Then why keep doing it."

"Because forty isn't three-eighty-nine." Rosa looked at her own knuckles. "I told you about the marketplace. I told you about the temp job. I stop taking the med, my hands go, I lose the work, and then I'm really —" She shook her head. "You felt it."

She bent to pick up one of the papers that had slipped out of the CVS bag. It was not a receipt. It was a printed estimate for a comprehensive eye exam and retinal screening from a clinic in Edison, folded into quarters and rubbed soft at the creases from being opened again and again.

"I keep everything," Rosa said. "Not because it helps. Because if I don't keep it, I start thinking maybe I imagined the numbers."

She shoved the paper back in the bag and looked at the building over Carmen's shoulder.

"I'm not stupid about Marcus either. I count before I leave. I don't ask him for extra. Once he offered to see if he could get me pain pills too and I said no because that's not what I need and I don't need a new disaster. Another time he told me he could maybe do prednisone and then came back and said no because the old lady pharmacist didn't like the blood-pressure history. Good. I don't need people improvising with my kidneys."

That detail snagged Carmen. "He offered first."

"Yeah. He offers things. He's a guy with inventory."

"And Agnes says no."

"Seems like it."

"You keep coming back anyway."

Rosa folded her arms tighter. "I come back for the hydroxychloroquine. I don't need to be in love with the operation."

The beeping from the loading dock stopped. For a second the lot went quiet except for traffic on Route 27 and the metallic tick of Rosa's cooling engine.

"If I report it," Carmen said, and heard how formal she sounded saying it, "you know what that could turn into."

"Yeah." Rosa did not blink. "Cops, board, whatever else rich people call when they want a mess cleaned up by force."

"And you're still here."

"I am."

"Why."

Rosa's mouth tightened. "Then I go back to stretching ninety days into one-forty. Cutting tablets. Trying to make the bottle last." She shifted her weight and winced when her knee caught. "Report it if you — I can't stop you. Just don't tell yourself you helped me."

They looked at each other in the heat.

"I don't know what I'm doing yet," Carmen said.

"OK." Rosa opened the driver's door and got in. She put the CVS bag on the passenger seat. "My refill's in November. Just so you know."

She pulled the door shut. The engine turned over on the second try — hesitation, catch, a belt complaining once before it settled. Carmen stepped back as Rosa pulled out of the space, brake lights glowing at the exit before the Civic took a left and merged into the afternoon traffic on Route 27 and was gone.

Carmen went in through the front doors with four minutes left before shift. The silk flowers Cliff had put out for the survey were still on the reception desk, already collecting dust along the fake petals. Her badge hit her scrub pocket when she clipped it on. At the sub-station Devon was awake now, staring at a glucometer like it had insulted him. Somewhere down the hall a television audience laughed too hard at something not funny.

In the medication room the day nurse was waiting with the controlled-substance log open beside a fresh pharmacy tote. Burt Kovacs's refill sheet was clipped to the top; generic carbidopa-levodopa had been substituted again. In the return bin, a discontinued trazodone card still held twenty-six sealed tablets, expiration next spring. Beside it sat two blister packs of rosuvastatin with nine tablets left in one and eleven in the other, both marked for destruction because the dose had changed and the family did not want the overfill.

"Cliff wants recounts on anything that even looks funny," the day nurse said without preamble. "Survey's got him twitching. If it's off, say it's off before I sign out."

Carmen set her bag down and washed her hands. The water in the med-room sink never got fully hot. She dried them on the brown paper towel that left lint in the webs of her fingers.

"I know how counts work," she said.

"I'm saying it because he said it six times at huddle and twice in my face."

The day nurse slid the log toward her. Carmen looked at the ruled lines, the initials, the dosages, the official story of what had moved and what had not. Controlleds were the only medications in the room that got treated like they were real. Everything else could disappear into delay, substitution, family confusion, pharmacy backlog, physician indifference, or a return bin with no hurry attached. But the narc book mattered. The narc book had signatures. The narc book had her license number waiting one bad audit away.

She opened the drawer and started counting with the day nurse watching. The plastic card pressed against her fingertips, tablet wells caught under her thumb, and sharp medicinal dust hung in the air. She said the numbers out loud and then said them again. Her hands moved automatically, but her mind kept splitting the room into paired columns the way Agnes's notebook had: official, actual; institutional, retail; clean case, not clean; harm inside, harm outside.

The day nurse pointed with her pen. "Burt's sheet came in. Generic again. He'll puke all over me tonight and then tell me I'm poisoning him."

"He's not wrong about the nausea."

"I know he's not wrong. I'm just tired."

Carmen initialed the line for receipt of the pharmacy tote. The pen clicked against the clipboard when she lifted it. In the return bin the trazodone card sat there like a small dumb sermon. Twenty-six tablets no one in administration would fight over because they were not controlled and no reimbursement report would ever care whether they were destroyed today or next week or never. On Agnes's pages that accumulation had become reserve, bridge, loss. On Rosa's side of the wall it had become the distance between forty dollars and three hundred eighty-nine.

"You good?" the day nurse asked.

Carmen counted again.

There was the official number. There was the second number behind it, the one that would turn the room hard and bright if she spoke it aloud. If she said it, Cliff would be in here within ten minutes with his tie crooked and his survey voice on, wanting signatures and incident forms and a chain of custody that would end, sooner or later, under 26NR03891. Maybe that was what should happen. Maybe that was the clean version. But clean was not the same as harmless and she had spent three days learning how little those things overlapped.

She thought of Harry telling Jack the money came from a certificate of deposit. She thought of Agnes writing LOSS without flinching. She thought of the woman on clonazepam and trazodone and nightly alcohol use who might stop breathing if Marcus worked alone. She thought of Rosa's hand in hers, hot and swollen, and the eye-exam estimate folded in the CVS bag like a bill from another planet.

"What'd you get?" the day nurse said.

Carmen wrote the official number where the form required it, kept the second count in her head, and went out to start med pass.


# Chapter 11

At 3:07 AM, while Carmen was back in the medication room recounting the shift's controlled substance log, the call bell from Room 116A went off. She was trying to make the numbers come out the way Agnes had taught her to make them come out: carefully, with specific purpose, each discrepancy accounted for in the parallel ledger she now kept in her head.

She was in the hallway before the second chime. Lakeview at night was its own creature: half-lit corridors, the ventilation cycling down to save on electricity, every sound traveling farther than it should. She heard her sneakers on the linoleum, the distant hum of the ice machine, and Room 110's soft continuous moan, which had become as ambient to her as highway noise on her drive home.

At Room 116A, she pushed the door open.

Earl was standing between the two beds in his undershirt and pajama pants, barefoot on the cold linoleum, and he had one of the facility's thin white towels pressed against the side of Burt's head. His other hand was braced on the nightstand for balance. His breathing was heavy, wet, and the effort of getting out of bed and crossing three feet of floor had cost him something she could hear in the rattle of each inhale.

Burt was on the ground. He'd gone down between the bed and the nightstand, the gap barely eighteen inches, and he was wedged there on his side, one arm pinned under him, the other reaching toward nothing. His legs were tangled in the sheet that had pulled off the bed with him. The bedside rail was down. It shouldn't have been down. She'd put it up at the start of her shift. She was almost sure she'd put it up. But the rails at Lakeview were old and the latches were loose and Burt moved in his sleep sometimes, reaching for things that weren't there anymore.

The blood was what she saw first and what she'd see when she closed her eyes for the next three nights. Scalp lacerations bled like nothing else, especially skin this thin, this old, the capillaries close to the surface and the tissue paper-fragile. Burt's white hair was dark and matted on the right side. Blood had pooled under his head on the linoleum, a small lake of it catching the light from the hallway, and there were smears on the nightstand's sharp laminate edge where he'd hit. The blood was thin and watery-looking, the way old blood was when the body's production line had started running slower than its losses.

"He fell," Earl said.

Carmen was already on the floor next to Burt. "Mr. Kovacs. Burt. Can you hear me?" She kept her voice level, the nurse voice, the one that cost her effort to hold steady. Her hands did the work: penlight from her scrub pocket, left pupil sluggish, right pupil responsive but slow. She took the towel from Earl's hand and pressed it to the laceration, feeling the split in the skin through the terry cloth, two inches long above his right ear, deep enough that she could feel the hard ledge of skull underneath.

"How long ago?" she asked Earl.

"A few minutes. I heard him get up. Then—" He stopped. The cough came, deep from his chest, the kind that folded him at the waist. He sat down on his own bed heavily and hacked into the crook of his elbow, and for a moment the only sounds in the room were Earl's coughing and Burt's breathing, which was shallow but regular, which was something.

"It's okay. You did right pressing the bell."

Burt's eyes were open but unfocused. His lips were moving. Carmen leaned closer. Hungarian. He was speaking Hungarian, the language that surfaced when the dementia peeled back the English like old wallpaper. *Erzsébet*, she heard, and then something else she couldn't parse, a long run of syllables that sounded like a question or a prayer. It was his wife's name, his wife's language, the one she'd used the dictionary to teach him before she died, before her dictionary became something else entirely.

"Stay still, Mr. Kovacs. Don't try to move." She held pressure on the wound with one hand and pulled her phone from her scrub pocket with the other to dial 911. She gave the information in the clipped shorthand of someone who'd called ambulances from this building before: 88-year-old male, ground-level fall, scalp laceration approximately two inches right temporal region, altered mental status at baseline with acute change, history of vascular dementia, current medications including fentanyl patch 25 mcg transdermal and oxycodone 5 mg PRN, possible concussion. She wanted c-spine precaution, and she wanted it fast.

She hung up. The room was quiet except for Burt's murmuring and Earl's breathing and the tick of the wall clock that ran two minutes slow because nobody had fixed it in the fourteen months Carmen had worked here. The ambulance should take seven minutes, maybe ten; at three in the morning in central New Jersey, the roads would be empty.

She pulled a second towel from the stack on Earl's shelf and layered it over the first, which was soaked through. The blood was on her hands now, in the creases of her knuckles, under the nail she'd broken last week opening a medication blister pack. The overhead light was off. Earl and Burt slept with it off. She was working by the light from the hallway and the green glow of the pulse oximeter on Burt's bedside table. She clipped the oximeter to his finger. It read ninety-four percent, low but not dangerous. His hand in hers was cool and slack, and his fingers, those long musician's fingers, the ones that had held a trumpet in a photograph she'd seen a hundred times, didn't grip back.

And then she saw the shelf.

It was behind her, mounted to the wall above the radiator on Burt's side of the room. Three items: a framed photograph of Burt with a woman who must have been Erzsébet, both younger, him holding a trumpet, her laughing at something beyond the camera's edge; a ceramic mug with the Berklee College of Music logo that held two pens and a broken watch; and the dictionary, a Hungarian-English hardcover with a faded red cloth binding and a spine cracked and reglued with tape that had yellowed to the color of old teeth. It was the book Erzsébet had given him so he could teach her his language while she taught him to think in hers. It was also the book that, since Agnes had hollowed out the center with a box cutter and pharmaceutical patience, held sixty-three oxycodone tablets and forty alprazolam, wrapped in small ziplock bags that Agnes had labeled in her pharmacist's print.

Carmen looked at the dictionary on the shelf and her hands kept pressing the towel to Burt's head while her mind split into two tracks that ran simultaneously, tracks that she would realize later had always been running simultaneously, ever since the night she walked Harry back to his room and didn't call the police. One track stayed with patient care: spinal precaution, pressure on the wound, monitor pupils and oxygen, wait for EMS. The other went to what the EMTs would see when they came. They might not search the shelf. They were here for the patient, not his belongings. But the hospital would inventory his personal items if they came with him. Or they'd leave the belongings and send a facilities report, and Cliff would dispatch someone to clean the blood off the linoleum, and that person might straighten the room. If anyone picked up that dictionary and felt how wrong the weight was, how a book that should weigh a pound weighed three, if anyone opened it and saw the hollowed-out cavity packed with labeled bags of Schedule II opioids, then the pills stopped being numbers in a drawer and became evidence.

She moved without deciding to move. That was the thing she would think about later, in her car, in her apartment, in the shower with the water too hot; there was no moment of decision, no internal argument, no weighing of consequences. She stood up, keeping one hand on the towel against Burt's scalp, and reached back with the other hand and took the dictionary off the shelf. It was heavier than a book that size should be. She could feel the pills shift inside, the soft rattle of plastic against hollowed-out pages, the weight of a hundred and three tablets that represented roughly eighteen hundred dollars in street value and a federal possession-with-intent charge that would end her career, her freedom, and the life she'd built for Reggie.

"Earl."

He looked at her. He looked at the dictionary in her hand. His face didn't change, not because he was hiding his reaction but because his face had already settled, minutes ago, into the expression of a man who understood what was at stake and had decided to let someone else handle it. He'd been a union organizer. He knew when to act and when to let the person with standing take the floor.

"Keep pressure here," she said. She guided his hand to the towel, repositioning it over the laceration. "Press hard. Don't let go until I come back."

Earl's large hand replaced hers. He pressed. His nails were trimmed short and clean. Tammy did that for him every Tuesday, with the same clippers she used for three other residents. There was blood between his fingers now, his roommate's blood, and his hand didn't shake.

Carmen stood with the dictionary against her hip and walked out of Room 116A. She went left down the hall, past the darkened rooms where people slept or didn't, past the sub-station where her charting waited half-finished on the desk, past the bathroom where she'd once seen Harry walking at 2:40 AM carrying something in a towel, and into the supply closet at the far end of Wing A.

The closet smelled like industrial bleach and the synthetic lavender of a plug-in air freshener that someone had wedged behind the toilet paper rolls. A mop sat in a bucket. Cases of latex gloves were stacked to her shoulder. The shelf above her head sat behind a case of adult incontinence pads, large overnight ones, the brand Lakeview bought in bulk because it was cheapest and leaked by 4 AM. Carmen put the dictionary on the shelf, spine facing the wall, and closed the closet door.

She stood in the hallway. Her right hand was tacky with Burt's blood, already cooling and stiffening in the creases of her palm. The fluorescent above her head flickered with its perpetual tick-tick-tick, the dying ballast that maintenance had been promising to replace since August. Down the hall, she could hear Earl coughing.

She walked back to 116A.

The EMTs arrived eight minutes later. Two men, young and efficient, moved through the room with the quiet competence of people who did this three times a shift and would do it again before dawn. They stabilized Burt's cervical spine, assessed his Glasgow score at 11, dressed the laceration with hemostatic gauze and a compression bandage, started an IV line in a vein so thin and fragile the younger EMT needed two attempts. Burt kept speaking Hungarian. One of them asked Carmen what he was saying. She said she didn't know. She knew one word, the name he kept repeating, soft and broken, the same syllables over and over, but that wasn't theirs.

They loaded him onto the stretcher and took him out through the side entrance. Carmen followed to the ambulance bay and watched the doors close. The October air was cold and damp, the smell of wet asphalt and dead leaves and diesel exhaust hanging after the ambulance pulled away, lights on, no siren. No siren meant they didn't think he was dying. The red wash from the taillights slid off the cinder-block wall and left the loading bay dim again. When she pushed back through the side door, Room 110 was still making its soft continuous moan and the wall clock at the sub-station was still two minutes slow.

She went back inside. She washed her hands in the medication room sink. The water ran pink, then diluted pink, then clear, and the institutional soap left her skin tight and dry. She pulled up Burt's chart on the computer and began the incident report. Date: October 1, 2026. Time: 0307. Location: Room 116A, between bed and nightstand. Description of event: *Resident found on floor with scalp laceration, approximately 2-inch, right temporal region. Bedside rail found in down position. Roommate (E. Washington) reports hearing fall, pressed call bell. Nurse responded, applied pressure to wound, assessed LOC and vitals, called 911. EMS arrived 0315, patient transported to Saint Peter's University Hospital for evaluation. Family notification per protocol.*

Her handwriting was steady. She documented everything she was supposed to document.

She didn't mention the dictionary.

---

Burt came back two days later, Saturday afternoon, in a medical transport van, belted into a wheelchair he didn't try to get out of. Carmen was starting her shift when the van pulled up to the side entrance. She signed the transfer paperwork from Saint Peter's: concussion with post-concussive syndrome, six sutures to the scalp laceration, CT scan showing no acute subdural hematoma but noting diffuse cortical atrophy consistent with moderate-to-severe vascular dementia, recommended follow-up with neurology in two weeks. Then she walked alongside the wheelchair as the transport aide rolled him to 116A.

He took up less room. The hospital discharge summary listed 142 pounds, down from the 151 in his Lakeview chart from three weeks ago, but the number explained only part of it. Something in him had folded inward, and the loss wasn't just weight; it was volume and presence. Before the fall, confusion had not emptied him out. Stories still came out of him about the Blue Note, about playing trumpet in clubs on Fifty-Second Street in a decade he might or might not have actually worked. The stories looped and stalled and fused with other stories, but they had heat. They came from a place in him that still had opinions about the world. He'd hum standards, mostly. She'd recognized "Round Midnight" once, and fragments of "In a Sentimental Mood," melodies that surfaced and sank like things seen through water.

He wasn't humming. He sat in the wheelchair with his hands in his lap, those long fingers knotted and liver-spotted, trembling with a fine tremor that was new or that she hadn't noticed before. The sutures on his scalp were covered by a gauze pad taped to his thin white hair. His eyes were open and aimed at the hallway but they weren't tracking anything. They were receiving light the way a window receives light, without processing it, without response.

She got him into bed. He didn't resist. Before the fall he had resisted: small, wordless protests, pulling his arm back when she reached for the blood pressure cuff, turning his face from the thermometer, the stubborn physical vocabulary of a person who still had preferences about what was done to his body. Now she lifted his arm and it stayed where she put it. She wrapped the cuff. It read 128 over 76, and his pulse was 68. She checked the suture site: dry, no infection, the wound healing the slow grudging way old skin healed, as if the body resented the effort. She changed his fentanyl patch, peeling the old one from his shoulder and pressing the new one to his upper arm, and he didn't flinch, didn't try to bat her hand away the way he used to.

Earl watched from his bed. He had a Sports Illustrated open on his lap, the same issue from three weeks ago, the one that hid the paperback he was actually reading, but he wasn't reading either one. He was watching Carmen check Burt, and his face had the expression she'd seen in photographs of men at union halls: someone counting costs and finding the total higher than the estimate.

"He's settled," she told Earl. "I'll check on him through the shift."

Earl nodded. He looked at Burt, then looked away. Then looked back. What got her was the looking-back, the way he couldn't leave it alone, the way his eyes kept returning to his roommate the way your tongue keeps finding a broken tooth.

"You moved the book," he said.

"Yeah."

He nodded once. That was all.

Carmen finished the intake assessment. Under cognitive status she wrote: *Decline noted from baseline. Reduced verbal output. Decreased resistance to care activities. Diminished environmental awareness and engagement. Pattern consistent with post-concussive acceleration of existing dementia. Will monitor and update physician at next rounds.*

She read it back once: *Reduced verbal output. Decreased resistance to care activities. Diminished environmental awareness and engagement.* On paper it was clean. In the room it meant Burt had let her peel the old fentanyl patch off his shoulder, stick a new one to his arm, and settle him into bed without once pulling away. He lay there afterward looking past her at nothing and not humming.

She left the room. The shelf above the radiator on Burt's side was missing one item. The photograph was there: Burt and Erzsébet, the trumpet, the laugh. The Berklee mug was there with its two pens and broken watch. The space where the dictionary had sat was a rectangle of slightly lighter paint where the wall hadn't faded.

---

The ring met at one in the morning the following Thursday, five days after Burt came back. Carmen had started attending meetings after the survey, not every one, but enough that her arrival no longer produced the careful silence it had the first two times. She'd nearly gone home after shift. Then she'd opened the narc drawer for count and seen Burt Kovacs's fresh oxy card still sitting in the slot under his name. The dictionary was still in Wing A supply behind the overnight briefs. Until somebody said out loud what happened next to Burt's pills, she wasn't leaving.

The TV room was the same as always: vinyl chairs in a loose half-circle, the wall-mounted TV dark, the card table with one short leg propped on a folded napkin. Harry preferred the brass reading lamp that Lola had taken from the nurses' break room months ago. It threw warm, limited light that made the room smaller, almost domestic, like a kitchen, like somewhere people actually lived. Outside the windows, the parking lot was empty under its orange lights and the October wind pushed leaves across the asphalt in fitful scrapes.

Harry was in his chair, the one nearest the lamp that no one else used. His faded blue cardigan was buttoned one off from the top, and either he hadn't noticed or it didn't matter enough to fix. He looked old tonight in a way that had nothing to do with the performed frailty he assembled for the day staff and the surveyors: the slack posture, the unfocused eyes, the careful theater of diminishment. The skin under his eyes was bruised with sleeplessness, and his hands rested on the chair's armrests without moving, which meant he was working something out that he hadn't solved.

Agnes sat across from him, back straight, wire-rimmed glasses reflecting the lamp. She had nothing in front of her: no notebook, no pillbox organizer. The operation had pulled inward since the fall, contracting the way a hand closes around an injury, and the meetings had shrunk with it. There was less inventory to review, less logistics to manage, and more silence to fill.

Lola was by the door, her usual post, though her lookout role had relaxed since Carmen had started sending Devon to take his breaks at the other wing's station. She held a coffee cup in both hands, the tremor in her left making the surface of the coffee shiver against the ceramic. She was watching Harry's face the way she watched everything, through the performance of not watching, her eyes settling elsewhere while her attention stayed fixed.

Earl had come from 116A, where Burt was asleep. He took the chair he always took, the one farthest from the center, closest to the door. He'd brought nothing. He never brought anything to meetings anymore. Before, he'd carried the cash envelopes, or the count from his side of the logistics, but that had stopped after the fall. The operation had contracted and Earl had contracted with it, pulling his participation inward the way he pulled everything inward.

"We need to talk about Burt," Harry said.

Nobody answered right away. Lola drank her coffee. Agnes adjusted her glasses with one finger at the bridge. Earl's silence was the same silence he always produced, but Carmen noticed it was aimed differently tonight, toward the empty space to Harry's left where Burt's wheelchair had been parked at every meeting since the ring began, where Earl used to push him in and set the brake and check that the blanket was tucked around his legs.

"He's still prescribed," Harry said. "Oxycodone five, ninety count, refilled last week. Agnes already flagged it for the rotation."

"I flagged it because that is my job," Agnes said. "That does not mean I flagged it for use."

Harry looked at her. "His prescriptions are the highest-value inventory we move. Without the oxy, we're short about eighteen hundred a month."

"He does not know what month it is."

"He didn't know what month it was six months ago either."

"Six months ago he could tell you he wanted to be part of this. He said yes. He understood what yes meant."

Carmen sat in the chair between Lola and Earl, the seat that had become hers by proximity and repetition. She was still in her scrubs from the shift she'd clocked out of two hours ago. Staying late had become its own small deception: the dedicated nurse, the one who cared enough to linger. She was tired of the irony without being willing to give it up. She listened.

Harry leaned forward. The chair creaked. "We all agreed when we started this. Every one of us. Burt included. He said—"

"He said yes to a man he trusted, in a room he recognized, on a night when he knew his own name." Agnes's voice hadn't risen, but it had compressed, with more facts per sentence and less space between them, the pharmacist under pressure accelerating instead of getting louder. Her hands were moving on the armrests of her chair, her fingers sorting nothing, organizing invisible inventory the way they'd organized pills for forty-two years. "He cannot say yes now. He cannot say no. He cannot produce anything that constitutes informed consent, and you know that, Harry."

"He's not being harmed by it," Harry said. "His pain management is the fentanyl patch. The oral oxy sits in his drawer untouched. We take pills he doesn't use and wouldn't know were gone."

"That is not the standard and you know it."

"What standard, Agnes? We're running a pill operation out of a nursing home. There's no ethics board."

"The standard," Agnes said, and her hands stopped moving, "is that you do not distribute a patient's medications without that patient's knowledge or agreement. I do not care that there is no board. I was the board for forty-two years. A patient with diminished capacity does not get spoken for. Not by his family. Not by his doctor. Not by us."

Lola set her coffee on the card table. The ceramic made a small sound against the wood grain. "He's the same Burt," she said. "He's just — he's tired, that's all, mijo. He was always a little tired."

"He is not the same," Agnes said. "You want him to be. He is not."

Lola's hand went to the crucifix at her throat, her thumb finding the small gold cross and rubbing it the way she did when something landed that she couldn't redirect with warmth or a story. She didn't answer. Her left hand trembled around the coffee cup.

Harry turned to Earl. "You live with him. You see him every day. What do you think?"

Earl looked at Harry. The room waited. Earl's silences were never empty. They had the specific weight of a man who'd spent thirty years in negotiation rooms and on picket lines, learning that the moment you spoke mattered as much as what you said. Carmen could hear the clock in the hallway, the lamp buzzing, and her own breathing.

Earl cleared his throat. Underneath it was the wet sound, the rattle she'd been hearing more of and that he kept turning away to hide.

"He fell," Earl said. "He was on the floor bleeding, and he was calling her name, and he didn't know where he was."

"That's not—" Harry started.

"He was calling her name and he didn't know where he was." Earl said it again in the same flat voice. The repetition itself was the emphasis. Earl, who said things once and expected them to land, was saying it twice because the room needed to hear it twice.

They sat with that. The reading lamp hummed. Outside, the wind picked up and a branch or a piece of loose gutter scraped across the window glass with a sound like fingernails on a chalkboard.

Carmen looked at her hands. The blood was gone, but she could still feel the drag of it drying in the creases of her knuckles. The dictionary was still in Wing A supply, behind the overnight briefs, with sixty-three oxycodone and forty alprazolam sitting inside it like a second emergency she had left on a shelf.

"He'd want us to keep going."

The word hit after she said it: *us*. Harry's eyes lifted. Agnes's did too.

"That is not an argument," Agnes said. "That is a guess about what a man who cannot speak for himself would want. I spent three years being spoken for. My daughter decided what I needed. The facility agreed. Nobody asked me. I will not do that to someone else."

"So what do we do?" Carmen said. "His prescriptions. The rotation."

"We pull his inventory from the active list," Agnes said. "His medications are dispensed to him and administered as prescribed. Every tablet. We do not divert them."

Carmen said, "What about the tablets already in the dictionary?"

Harry turned to her. "Earl told me you pulled it before EMS got there. I told Agnes. Lola's hearing it now. Where is it?"

"Wing A supply closet," Carmen said. "Top shelf, behind the overnight briefs."

Lola shut her eyes for a second. "Ay Dios."

Agnes stood. "Good. Then it leaves that closet tonight and those pills leave circulation before dawn."

"That's eighteen hundred dollars," Harry said.

"Then lose eighteen hundred dollars. I will not put loose tablets back in Burt's drawer, and I will not sell them."

She held her hand out to Carmen. "Show me."

Carmen got up. Harry got up more slowly. Earl stayed where he was, looking at the floor a moment longer, then pushed himself to his feet.

"I'm going back to him," he said.

Lola stood too. "I'm coming."

They left first, Earl toward 116A, Lola a step behind him with her coffee cup still in her hand.

The supply closet light buzzed on. Carmen reached behind the case of briefs and brought the dictionary down. Agnes took it from her, opened the cover, and looked at the bagged pills inside without touching them.

"Sixty-three oxycodone," she said. "Forty alprazolam."

Harry stood in the doorway. He did not argue again. He took the folded active list from his cardigan pocket, found Burt's room number, and drew a line through it with the pen he used for everything.

Agnes shut the dictionary and tucked it under her arm. "I will keep this in my room. I will destroy the tablets before breakfast med pass."

Harry folded the list and put it back in his pocket. Agnes walked the dictionary down the hall toward 108A. In a few hours the breakfast trays would start rattling out of the kitchen, and Burt's pills would be gone.


# Chapter 12

The word she was looking for was aluminum.

Not an important word. She'd been trying to describe the foil over the breakfast tray to her roommate Mrs. Kim, who couldn't hear her and wouldn't have cared, and the word had gone missing somewhere between her brain and her mouth. She'd stood there with her hand out, miming the act of peeling back the foil, and what came out was "the — the shiny. The paper that's not paper."

Mrs. Kim hadn't looked up from her oatmeal.

Lola sat on her bed after breakfast and tried the word again. Aluminum. There it was, back in place, as if it had only stepped out for a minute. Her heart jumped under her nightgown. She couldn't tell if the word had slipped because she'd been performing confusion for fourteen months and the act had worn a track through her, or because she was seventy-nine and had Parkinson's and sometimes seventy-nine-year-old women with Parkinson's lost words. Both belonged in the room. There was no test for sorting them. No blood draw, no brain scan, no doctor saying this one is the disease and this one, Dolores, is what you taught yourself to do.

She held her left hand up in front of her face. The tremor was there, as it always was — a fine, persistent shaking, worse in the mornings before her carbidopa-levodopa kicked in, worse when she was tired, worse when she was afraid. Right now it was moderate. A ripple, not a quake. She watched it and felt nothing useful. You couldn't argue with a tremor. You couldn't ask it to explain itself. It was just there. The medication helped and didn't help, and some mornings she could button her blouse on the first try and some mornings the buttons won and she asked Tammy for help with the same flat acceptance she'd learned to apply to everything her body did without her permission.

Her roommate, Mrs. Kim, was being wheeled to physical therapy by an aide Lola didn't recognize. A new one. Mrs. Kim had gone far enough into vascular dementia that most mornings she looked past whoever was in front of her, and Lola had stopped expecting recognition. The aides came and went so fast that Lola had stopped learning their names, which felt like its own small cruelty but was also just practical. You couldn't mourn everyone who left.

She got up and walked to the window. Outside, October had taken hold. It always seemed to happen with her back turned. The three trees in the courtyard had started to turn — though "turn" was generous. They were mostly just giving up, dropping leaves in patchy clumps onto the pavement below. Beyond them, the parking lot, half-full at this hour. Beyond that, Route 27. If she pressed her face to the glass she could see the Dunkin' Donuts sign, which she did sometimes, not because she wanted a donut but because the sign was bright and unmistakable and it reminded her that there was a world out there that did things she no longer did, like drive and buy coffee and stand in a line and choose.

She was supposed to be confused right now. That was her job, her contribution: the lookout, the smokescreen, the warm old woman who distracted nurses and visitors with stories while the real work happened behind her. In the hallways, during meals, during activities, when the aides came in, when Dr. Prasad made her biweekly rounds — Lola Fernandez was chatty, affectionate, mildly disoriented, calling everyone mijo or mija, telling stories that went in circles, asking what day it was three times in a conversation. The warmth was real. The stories were real, most of them. The confusion was manufactured, and it had been easy at first. Fun, even. She'd been a teacher for thirty-one years, fourth grade, and every day in a fourth-grade classroom was a performance of patience and enthusiasm that sometimes became real by third period. Playing confused was simpler than that. You just let the words tangle, let the sentences loop, let your eyes go soft when someone asked you a direct question. You became a version of yourself that people already expected a seventy-nine-year-old woman in a nursing home to be.

But fourteen months of playing confused had done something she hadn't anticipated. The confusion had weight. It seeped into her like sofrito smell working into the kitchen curtains in Union City, a little each day until the cloth held it whether you wanted it to or not. She forgot words now. She wasn't performing it. She wasn't choosing to stumble for whoever was watching. She reached for a word and found the shelf bare. Aluminum. Thermometer, last week — she'd been trying to tell Carmen her room was cold and she'd said "the thing, the thing that measures" and Carmen had looked at her with that careful nurse's attention and Lola had smiled through it, had made it part of the act, but alone afterward she'd sat on this bed and said thermometer six times fast with her hands balled in her lap. Carnation, the week before that, and that one had frightened her, because Tomás used to bring her carnations, cheap ones from the bodega on Bergenline Avenue, and if she was losing the word for the flower her husband brought her then she was losing a piece of him, and she had so few pieces left.

She thought about Burt. She didn't want to but she did. Burt had fallen two weeks ago and come back from the hospital worse, quieter, the confusion in his eyes no longer intermittent but settled, and watching him — watching a man whose dementia had been real all along, real where hers wasn't, or hadn't been, or might be becoming — had put a sound in her ears she couldn't shake. A kind of hum. Not a sound anyone else could hear. Just the low, steady vibration of a future she was walking toward whether she wanted to or not, a future where the word aluminum didn't come back, where thermometer stayed gone, where the act and the woman performing it merged into one thing and nobody, including her, could tell the difference.

Tomás would have said something about this. Something practical, something slightly impatient, because Tomás had not been a gentle man about worry. He'd been gentle about other things — he could cup a baby bird in his hands with a softness that made you forget those same hands poured concrete for twenty years — but worry made him restless, made him want to fix. "Lola, stop," he would have said. "Either it's happening or it's not, and sitting there scaring yourself doesn't change which one it is."

He was right. He was always right about that kind of thing, and wrong about everything else.

She sat on the bed and let herself think about him. Not a prayer exactly. A visit. Most mornings began like this. Tomás, she said in her head. Amor, mira. The conversation was one-sided and it always had been, because even alive Tomás hadn't been a big talker, and dead he was even less so.

He'd died of pancreatic cancer ten years ago, and he had not gone peacefully. People said passed away, went gently, is in a better place. Lola let them say it. What she had was Tomás in a hospice bed in their apartment in Union City, a bed the insurance company had delivered with more efficiency than they'd ever shown for his actual treatment, and three months of dying that he'd spent furious. Not sad. Not resigned. Not at-peace-with-God. Furious. He'd cursed the nurses who came to change his IV. He'd cursed the hospice chaplain, a kind young woman named Sister Beth who'd tried to talk to him about acceptance, and Tomás had told her to take her acceptance and get out of his house. He meant it. He was dying at sixty-eight and he had poured concrete and raised two children and paid his taxes and fixed the sink when it leaked and gone to Mass when Lola asked him to and now his pancreas was eating itself alive and no one could tell him why.

The last two weeks, the morphine drip ran almost constantly and he was in and out, and the in was worse than the out because the in meant he knew where he was and what was happening to him. He'd lost forty pounds. His skin had the color and texture of wet newspaper. He vomited bile into a plastic basin because his stomach couldn't hold anything, not even water, not even the ice chips Lola put on his lips with a spoon. The morphine constipated him so badly that she gave him suppositories, which she did without complaint, without performance, because she'd also wiped his back when he sweated through his shirts at night and held his penis when he couldn't aim at the urinal anymore, and she'd done all of it because that was what forty-three years of marriage actually meant. Not the vows. The vows didn't know to include any of this.

He'd died at 4:17 on a Tuesday morning in January. She'd been sitting next to the bed, not sleeping, her hand on his arm. His breathing had changed — the rattling, the gaps that got longer — and then it stopped, and the room went quiet in a way rooms with living people in them never were, and she'd sat there for a long time before she picked up the phone. Those minutes were the last minutes she'd ever be in a room with Tomás. Once she called the hospice number, the machinery of death would start — the funeral home, the insurance paperwork, the obituary, the calls to Roberto and Miriam, the casseroles from neighbors who meant well and didn't know what else to do — and she wasn't ready for that. She wasn't ready for the shift from wife to widow, the last role change she'd ever have to make, or so she'd thought at the time.

She'd been wrong about that. She'd made one more: from widow to patient. And one more after that: from patient to lookout, to performer, to a woman who watched through a mask of confusion while pills moved through cookie tins and envelopes of cash went into the pockets of a man everyone believed was her nephew.

Tomás, she thought. Amor. What would you say about all this.

She knew what he'd say. He'd say: "Are you eating? Is anyone hurting you? Then do what you need to do and stop worrying." That was Tomás. He operated from a simple moral architecture: are you fed, are you safe, is someone being cruel. Everything else was negotiable.

Her son Roberto called every Sunday from Chicago. The calls lasted about twelve minutes. She'd timed one once, not to catch him, just out of the bleak curiosity that came from having too much time and not enough to spend it on. Twelve minutes covered his wife Elena, the kids, work, the weather in Chicago, and "how are you doing, Mamá?" She always said fine. She performed mild confusion on these calls now, because Roberto talked to the staff, and if Lola was lucid and articulate on the phone but drifting in the building, someone would notice. So she let her sentences circle. She asked about the grandchildren and then asked again five minutes later. Roberto's voice when she did this — patient, a little too loud, careful — sounded like the voice Helen Chen used when she visited Agnes, and that comparison made Lola's stomach turn.

Her daughter Miriam sent cards from Orlando. Birthday, Christmas, Mother's Day, and random Hallmark cards with flowers on the front and messages like "Thinking of you today!" The exclamation point always felt wrong to Lola, something pushed when it should be quiet. If you were thinking of someone, you could come. But Miriam had two kids and a husband who traveled for work and a mortgage she'd mentioned twice, each time giving a little laugh right after the monthly number, and Orlando was far, and Lola didn't blame her. She didn't blame either of them. They were living the lives America demanded of children — spread out, working, building families in whatever city had jobs — and the alternative was guilt, which helped no one and which Lola had seen do terrible things to other families in this building. The daughter in Hackensack who visited every day and cried every time, until the resident — a man named Phil who had Lewy body dementia and didn't know anyone's name — started getting agitated whenever she showed up. The son who came once a month from Pennsylvania and spent the entire visit staring at his phone. Guilt made people stupid. Lola preferred the cards.

She touched the crucifix at her throat. The gold was warm from her skin. She didn't pray — she hadn't prayed since Tomás died, because she had some things to say to God and none of them were requests — but the crucifix was her mother's, and touching it was a way of touching her mother, dead longer than Tomás and still easiest to reach through the hands. Her thumb found the little cross the same way she'd watched her mother's thumb find it through entire Masses, back when Lola was small enough to fit under her arm.

Ay, Dolores. What are you doing.

She said it to herself in her mother's voice. Not with judgment. With tired wonder.

---

Tammy found her in the courtyard at two o'clock, which was how most of their afternoons together started — not by plan but by routine, by two people who lived in the same small world eventually falling into rhythm without deciding to. Tammy's break was 1:45 to 2:15, and Lola was usually out here by one-thirty because the room was too quiet after Mrs. Kim was wheeled back from therapy and fell asleep, and the courtyard, even with its scraggly trees and its view of the parking lot, was outside. Outside still counted for something.

"Move over," Tammy said, and sat down on the bench without waiting for Lola to shift. She was wearing her teal scrubs, the ones with the bleach stain on the left thigh that she'd given up trying to hide, and her hair was in a knot that looked like it had been assembled in the dark, which it probably had been — Tammy's studio apartment, the one she'd moved into after the divorce, had one overhead light and a window that faced a wall. Lola knew this because Tammy had described it three times, each time in the same flat way, as if bad lighting were just another item on a list of things that had gone wrong and she didn't have the energy to rank them anymore.

"Coffee?" Tammy held up two Styrofoam cups from the staff kitchen. The coffee at Lakeview was terrible — thin, vaguely brown, produced by a machine nobody had cleaned since the previous administrator — but Tammy always brought two cups, and she always got it right. Two sugars, milk not cream. It was the kind of thing that shouldn't matter and that mattered more than almost anything.

"Gracias, mija." Lola took the cup with both hands, partly because of the tremor and partly because the warmth felt good. The October air had an edge to it now, a sharpness that meant summer had stopped pretending. Two of the courtyard trees had gone mostly bare, the third holding on to its leaves in scattered orange patches, stubborn or just slow. Leaves littered the pavement around the benches, dry and curling.

They sat. The courtyard was empty except for them and a squirrel working the base of the middle tree, nosing through the fallen leaves for something edible. Lola watched it with a recognition she didn't examine.

"So," Tammy said.

"So."

Tammy drank her coffee and made the face she always made, the one that said this is undrinkable and she was going to finish every drop. "Greg's got a girlfriend."

Lola didn't look at her. She knew from fourteen months of performed inattention, from a lifetime of reading rooms, that Tammy needed her to not make this a moment. She lifted her coffee and blew on it.

"Yeah. Brandy. Her name's Brandy, like the — yeah." Tammy's voice had gone carefully flat, which meant she was working hard to keep it there. "She's twenty-eight. Works at a Verizon store in Bridgewater."

"Ay."

"It's fine. It's been eight months. It's whatever."

It was not fine and not whatever and they both knew it. Tammy worried the rim of her Styrofoam cup with her thumbnail. Her words came out one by one, too careful, too even. The divorce had reached Lola in pieces over these afternoons. Greg had left in March. Before that there'd been the gym woman whose name Tammy never said. Papers were final in July. Tammy had moved into the studio in Perth Amboy with her cat, who'd immediately peed on the mattress. She'd replaced the mattress, not the cat. That was the detail Tammy let out when she was deciding between laughing and doing something worse.

"Twenty-eight," Tammy said again, looking at the squirrel instead of Lola. "I'm forty-three and he picks a girl who wasn't born when I started working here."

Lola wanted to say something real. The want was physical, pressing at her sternum like a cough she couldn't release. She wanted to drop the mask and say: Mija, I watched my husband die in our apartment. He cursed the chaplain and I wiped bile off his chin and I gave him suppositories because nobody else was going to and we didn't talk about dignity because dignity was a word for people who hadn't had to do those things yet. And twenty-eight-year-old Brandy from Verizon is not going to do that for Greg when the time comes, and the time comes for everybody. She wanted to say: your ex-husband is an ordinary man doing an ordinary stupid thing and it'll hurt you until one day it won't and I can't tell you when that day is because grief doesn't keep a schedule, not even the grief for a man who's still alive and just chose someone else.

But she was Lola Fernandez, room 112A. Warm, confused, mildly disoriented. And Tammy was the person who brushed her hair on the mornings the tremor made the brush jump and skitter in her grip. Tammy was the one who knew about the two sugars and the milk. If Lola spoke too clearly, too specifically, Tammy would notice. Tammy was tired, not stupid. She noticed things. And noticing would lead to questions, and questions would lead to the ring, and the ring would lead to Harry and Agnes and Earl and Carmen and Marcus and Burt, and Lola would be the one who'd unraveled everything because she couldn't stand being lied to about coffee by a person she was lying to about everything else.

So she said: "Men." She said it with a wave of her hand, a puff of air, pure performance — dismissing the whole gender as a weather pattern. "Tomás — my husband — he forgot our anniversary once. Thirty years, mija. Thirty years married and he forgot. Brought flowers the next day like that fixed it." She let the story ramble, let it loop in the loose circles her cover required, and the story was true — Tomás had forgotten their thirtieth, and the flowers had been carnations from the bodega, and she'd forgiven him before he walked through the door because forgiveness wasn't something she'd ever had to decide about with Tomás, it was just the climate they lived in — but the truth of the story was separate from its job here, which was to be harmless, to fill space, to sound like a confused old woman trying to soothe a younger woman's pain.

"Greg never forgot an anniversary," Tammy said. "He just forgot to give a shit about the other three hundred and sixty-four days."

"Sí."

"Sorry. Language."

"Mija, I've heard worse." She had. She'd heard Tomás curse God and a chaplain and the pharmaceutical company that made the morphine that wasn't strong enough and the insurance adjuster who'd denied the home health aide request two weeks before he died.

"Yeah." Tammy stretched her legs out. Her shoes were the white nursing clogs that all the CNAs wore, scuffed gray on the sides and collapsed at the heels from the miles she logged every shift — the steps between rooms, the laps around the wing, the back-and-forth to the supply closet for gloves and wipes and the incontinence briefs they went through forty of per day. Lola looked at those shoes and saw fifteen years of work that nobody wanted to discuss over dinner. "She posted pictures on Facebook. Her and Greg at some restaurant with candles. On the table. Candles." Tammy's voice thinned on the word. "We ate off paper plates because I was too tired to wash dishes after a twelve-hour."

"I'm sorry, mija."

"Don't be. I'm just — it's just stupid. The whole thing's stupid."

Lola felt the guilt land under her ribs, and the coffee went sour in her mouth. Tammy brought her coffee and brushed her hair and told her about Greg and the apartment and the bad lighting and the cat, and Lola kept giving her room 112A instead of herself.

The coffee shook in her left hand. She put the cup down on the bench between them.

"You know what the worst part is?" Tammy said.

"Tell me."

"I miss him." She said it as if missing him were something she'd been caught doing. Not the volume. The posture — her shoulders came forward, her chin dropped. "Not the — I don't miss the lying. Or the gym woman. Or the part where he let me pick up doubles every week while he watched videos on his phone. I miss having someone there. In the apartment. I miss noise. It's so quiet, Lola. My place is so quiet. It's like —" She stopped. Picked at the rim of her Styrofoam cup. "You know when you turn the TV off and the silence afterward is louder than the silence before you turned it on? That's what it's like. Every night."

Lola knew that silence. She'd lived inside it for ten years, first in the apartment in Union City where it had been Tomás-shaped — a quiet that filled the exact spaces his sounds had occupied: the scratch of his pen on the crossword, the wheeze in his breathing that she'd told him to get checked and he hadn't, the thud of his boots by the door — and then in this room, where the quiet was different, institutional, a quiet made up of the sounds just outside it: Mrs. Kim's breathing, the ventilation hum, the distant call bells.

"I know," Lola said.

And then the words came out before she could stop them. "You're a good person, mija."

Tammy looked at her. Her thumbnail stopped at the rim of the cup. For a beat she looked straight at Lola, not at the squirrel or the parking lot.

"That's sixteen-fifty an hour and a studio with a window facing a wall," Tammy said.

Lola let her eyes slide away to the garbage can, where the squirrel had made it to the lid and was hanging there with its tail twitching. "Ay. That's true."

"Yeah."

"I gotta get back," Tammy said. She stood and her back cracked, two sharp reports like knuckles popping, the sound of a forty-three-year-old body that did the physical work of a much younger person for compensation that assumed she was replaceable. She picked up both coffee cups — hers and Lola's — because that was Tammy, then stood there another second with the cups in her hand, waiting.

"Tammy."

"Yeah?"

Lola had the first two clean words in her mouth: I'm not. She pinched the Styrofoam seam until it bent under her thumbnail. If she said them, Tammy would hear the whole rest of it.

"Tell Brandy I said hi," she said instead.

Tammy snorted. Not a real laugh. The short, hard sound a person made when something was funny enough to crack the surface but not enough to break through. "Yeah, I'll get right on that."

She walked back inside. The courtyard door closed behind her with the heavy pneumatic exhale that all institutional doors made, a sound engineered for a building designed to contain rather than welcome. Lola sat with the bench to herself and the two wet rings where the coffee cups had been and set Tammy's life in order against her knee, counting with her thumb on the fingers of her shaking left hand. Greg. Brandy. Verizon. Twenty-eight. Bridgewater. Studio. Window facing a wall.

She did it again, slower. Greg. Brandy. Verizon. Twenty-eight. Bridgewater. Studio. Window facing a wall.

On the third pass, Bridgewater disappeared.

Not the feeling of it. Just the word. The little Jersey town where Brandy sold phone plans and posed beside Greg in candlelight on Facebook, and Lola could not catch its name.

She sat with that blank spot for a beat, then another. Then she got up, knees crackling, and went to the courtyard door.

Inside, Tammy was pushing the linen cart past the nurse's station. Lola let her shoulders sag, softened her eyes, and put the old familiar drift back in her voice. "Mija?" she called. "Did Mrs. Kim eat? I can't — I don't remember if she ate."

Tammy looked over. "She ate, Lola. She's back in bed."

"She ate," Lola said, nodding as if that settled it.

Then she went down the hall at her careful shuffle while Tammy turned back to the cart, the act back in place and Bridgewater still gone.


# Chapter 13

Marcus was sitting in the vinyl chair closest to the door when Carmen came down the hall at quarter past midnight. Marcus didn't sit in meetings — Marcus paced, shifted his weight, leaned against walls and pushed off again like a man who couldn't afford to stop moving. Tonight he had both hands on his knees and his feet planted flat.

The TV room was cold. October had arrived while facilities management debated switching the heating system from its summer settings, and the result was a building that held the day's chill instead of releasing it. The overhead fluorescents were off. They used the television's standby light and a brass lamp Agnes had brought from her room months ago, its shade throwing amber across the card table and leaving the rest of the room in blue-gray shadow. The single-pane windows were black, and when Carmen passed close to one she could feel the cold radiating off it like the opposite of warmth, a presence rather than an absence.

Harry was at the table in his wheelchair, though he'd pushed back from it by a few inches — not close enough to work, not far enough to be out of the circle. A calculation in furniture. Agnes sat beside him with only a coffee cup, no notebooks, no pill organizer. Earl was against the far wall, arms crossed, legs extended in his institutional slippers. Next to him, Burt's empty wheelchair was folded and leaned against the baseboard. Nobody mentioned it. Nobody had mentioned it for two weeks, not since Burt had come back from the hospital with his eyes fogged over and his hands forgetting what they were holding. The empty wheelchair was just a shape in the room now, like the TV nobody watched or the fake plant by the window that had been there since before any of them arrived.

Lola was at her position by the hallway door, but she kept turning back toward the group with quick glances that made her tremor visible even in the low light. Her left hand opened and closed at the base of her throat.

Marcus had come in during visiting hours — signed the log as Lola's nephew at four-thirty, stayed through dinner, and melted into the building the way he always did. Devon, the overnight aide, had been asleep at the nurses' station since eleven, earbuds in, a true-crime podcast murmuring through tiny speakers. Carmen had heard it when she passed on her med check.

"So," Harry said.

Marcus looked at the floor, then at Carmen, then at Harry. He was checking the room's temperature before he opened his mouth. Carmen had seen him do this with buyers in the parking lot descriptions Harry gave her — read the space, calibrate, then talk. Tonight the calibration took longer.

"I got approached," Marcus said. "By a buyer. Not like our regular people."

Agnes's fingers tightened around her cup.

"A reseller," Marcus said. "Club scene, maybe college towns. I didn't get his whole biography. He's got his own customers who want oxy. Steady demand. He wants tens mostly. Says he'll take fives too if that's what I have, but he priced it simple: eighteen for a ten, nine for a five."

"How much volume," Harry said.

"Two hundred ten-milligram tabs a month to start," Marcus said. "Or the five-milligram equivalent. Same money either way."

Agnes set her coffee cup on the card table. It clicked.

"That's—" Carmen started.

"Thirty-six hundred a month just on oxy," Harry said. "On top of the four grand we're already pulling."

"Seventy-six hundred total if everything else keeps moving." Marcus stood up. There was the pacing — he crossed to the window, turned, crossed back. His sneakers were quiet on the linoleum but Carmen could feel the movement as spatial pressure, the room getting smaller with each pass. "Look, that's double. Harry, you could clear Jack's debt in a few months. Earl, Denise could drop the weekend shifts. Everybody breathes."

"Not everybody," Agnes said.

"Come on, Agnes—"

"I am not finished." She did not raise her voice. The room adjusted anyway. "Clubs. College towns. Those are not patients who lost prescriptions. Those are recreational users."

"Some of them could still have—"

"No." Agnes leaned forward. "Those are people buying oxycodone to get high, then mixing it with alcohol or alprazolam because they think five milligrams sounds small. It is not small. Forty milligrams can kill an opioid-naive person. Eighty if they have been drinking. That is four tens. That is eight fives. That is one Saturday night."

The room held the number. Carmen felt it settle behind her ribs.

Marcus stopped pacing. "We don't know that's what he does. We don't know who he's selling to—"

"We know that you do not know," Agnes said. "That is enough. When I sort pills for Rosa, I know her condition, her weight, her other medications. If I hand this man two hundred tablets and he walks away, I know nothing after that. I have spent my career making sure that does not happen."

"Agnes." Marcus held both hands up. "I hear you. I do. But we're already moving controlled meds out of a nursing home. We crossed a line a long time ago."

"We crossed a line," Carmen said. "Not this one."

Marcus turned to her. His jaw flexed once. "You sure? Because from where I'm standing, the only difference between Janine and this guy is Janine lets you sleep after."

"The difference is Janine has chronic pain and a chart."

"A chart in a building we already steal from."

"We know what it's for."

"So now we're pharmacists?"

Carmen thumbed the edge of her badge through her scrub top. "Agnes is."

Marcus looked at her for a beat too long.

Harry tapped the table twice. The taps were soft and measured. Everybody stopped.

"Two questions," he said. "First, what does this do to our exposure. Second, what does it do to us."

"The exposure's manageable," Marcus said. He sat back down. His leg bounced. "I handle him like anybody else. Separate phone, different location, cash only. He doesn't know about Lakeview, doesn't know where the supply comes from—"

"He knows about you," Agnes said. "That is enough."

Manageable. Carmen had heard administrators use that word about scabies, falls, staffing holes. It always meant the problem was going to land on whoever still had a license.

"He knows a first name and a number."

"He is a drug dealer, Marcus. That matters. He gets arrested, one of his customers gets arrested, somebody wants a deal, names start moving up the chain. You are not adding one customer. You are adding everyone after him."

Carmen pictured a Board packet on cheap white paper with her license number at the top and the controlled-substance log clipped behind it. One arrest. One bargain. One person saying Marcus's name.

"Every buyer we have now could talk."

"Not like this. Rosa gets caught with hydroxychloroquine, that is possession and maybe pity. Your reseller gets caught with two hundred oxycodone, that is trafficking. Trafficking goes upstream. They find you. You bring them here."

Marcus rubbed the back of his neck. His leg kept bouncing. "You're assuming the worst case."

"I am assuming the likely case. Do you know this person's real name?"

"I know what he goes by."

"Do you know whether he has prior convictions? Whether he is currently under investigation? Whether he was sent to you by someone cooperating with law enforcement?"

Marcus did not answer. His leg stopped.

He didn't know. That hit Carmen harder than the money. Up to then she had still been pretending there was some procedure to this, some shape. There was a man in a parking lot and Marcus did not know his real name.

"A man you have never dealt with before approached you and offered premium prices for Schedule II opioids in quantity," Agnes said. "You did not find him. He found you. That should bother you more than it does."

Marcus leaned forward. Both elbows on his knees. When he spoke, the jokes were gone.

"Likely case?" He gave a short laugh with no humor in it. "Okay. My likely case is I get stopped on Route 1 because the brake light's still out. Two months now. I keep meaning to fix it and then rent's due or the tank's empty or some other shit comes first. I've got Schedule II pills in the glove box. I'm the one standing on asphalt with my hands where a cop can see them. Me. Not Harry. Not Agnes. Not..." He glanced at Carmen. "Not any of you. They put cameras in here, it's poor old residents manipulated by some outside operator. They put cameras on me, I'm the dealer going to Rahway."
