The room was quiet except for Earl's breathing, the rasp underneath each exhale, the faint wet whistle Carmen heard every time and kept meaning to say something about.

"You carry more risk," Harry said. His voice stayed neutral. "That's true."

Marcus looked at him, quick, then down at the floor.

"But that's separate from this buyer," Harry said. "The risk is not just yours anymore. It's the count discrepancy on Carmen's log. It's inventory that does not balance. Two hundred extra oxycodone a month is not a documentation error. It is a pattern. Patterns trigger DEA referrals."

"So we adjust the—"

"There is no adjustment that covers two hundred pills a month, Marcus. The arithmetic does not work. I have been running the numbers on what we can move without tripping automated flags for fourteen months. We are close to the ceiling now."

Under the brass lamp Marcus still looked neat — jacket clean, collar straight, nails trimmed. It was a former CNA habit, the kind of neatness that made families trust you with a parent. Carmen believed the part of him that hated carrying the risk. She believed the part that wanted the money too.

"You know what, Carmen?" His voice went tight. "You got here late. Harry had this running, Agnes built the supply side, I built the routes. You walked in after and now you get to decide what kind of crime counts as care."

"That's not—"

"You're pulling thirteen hundred extra a month out of this situation and telling me where the moral line is."

Carmen kept her hands flat at her sides. "I don't take money from this. You know that."

"Right. You just take the part where nobody asks why your controlled-substance log doesn't add up. That's free, right?"

It wasn't free. She kept her mouth shut.

Lola came away from the doorway. She had been standing there the whole time, one hand on the frame, the other pressed hard against her thigh, and now she moved into the circle with the unsteady gait Carmen still could not parse — Parkinson's or performance or some blend of both that Lola herself probably could not untangle anymore.

"I don't like this," Lola said. Her words came quick and crowded. "No, I don't, Marcus, listen. We had our ugly little thing and I could keep it straight. Janine with the bad back. Rosa with the lupus, those poor hands all swelled up. I could put faces on it. Ay, that's what I mean. I could lie to Tammy in the morning and still know who I was lying for. This is some man in a parking lot and then some kid I don't know with his head over a bathroom sink and then God knows who after that. Daytime me is already dropping words on the floor. Nighttime me can't carry strangers too."

Her left hand shook harder. She pressed it against her thigh and the tremor only moved into her shoulder. Marcus watched her trying to hold her own body still with one hand. Agnes's fingers loosened from the coffee cup. Harry kept his eyes on the table.

"Lola," he said. "I'm not trying to make it worse. I'm trying to make—"

"I know. I know, mijo." She wiped her eyes with the heel of her hand. "But that doesn't make it right."

Harry looked at Earl.

Earl had not spoken. He had been in the same position against the wall since the meeting started — arms crossed, legs out, his face giving Carmen nothing. His breathing was the loudest thing about him. When Harry said his name, Earl cleared his throat and the clearing turned into a cough, short and hard and wet. He waited it out, wiped his hand on his pajama pants, and looked at Marcus.

"We kept our own covered when this place wouldn't," Earl said. "I'm not building a stranger's business."

The back of Carmen's neck went cold. Earl almost never spent a full sentence unless he meant it to close a door, and hearing him say our own made something ugly shift under her ribs. She had been telling herself for weeks she was still adjacent to this, not inside it.

Lola's hand dropped to her side. Harry's chin dipped once. Marcus stared at Earl like he had expected backup there and found a wall instead.

"That's it?" Marcus said.

Earl looked away. He did not add anything.

"You're sitting here telling me what this is about? I'm the one who—" Marcus stopped, shook his head, started again. "Fine. Okay."

Harry straightened in his wheelchair. His hands went flat on the table again.

"Here's what's going to happen," Harry said. "You are going to decline the buyer. You are going to tell him the supply is not available. You break the contact. No second meeting. No counteroffer. No keeping his number for later."

"Harry—"

"That is the decision."

"It's the wrong one."

"Maybe. We have survived wrong decisions before. We will not survive a trafficking investigation."

For one second Carmen wanted to let Harry finish it without her. She wanted him to be the one who said no and carried the weight. But her name sat on the logs, and silence had started to feel like a signature.

Carmen had been running the legal calculation the whole time: her name on every dispensation record, her license number on every controlled-substance log, the Board file that would start with her if any of this came back through a reseller's arrest.

"Marcus." She waited until he looked at her. "If you take this deal, I report everything. I go to the Board. I hand over the diversion records, the dates, the quantities. All of it."

The room went still. Even Earl's breathing seemed to quiet, though Carmen knew that was her own attention narrowing.

"You'd go down too," Marcus said.

"I know."

"You'd lose your license." He paused. "You'd lose Reggie."

Her son's name in this room, in this argument, at one-thirty in the morning in a nursing home where she was supposed to be administering care. She felt the badge under her scrub top pressing against her sternum.

"I know what I would lose," she said.

"And you'd still do it."

"I am telling you what I will do."

Harry was watching her. Completely still. Agnes had both hands flat on the table, her coffee forgotten. Lola was crying, silently, one hand on the crucifix and the other pressed against her stomach. Earl was looking at the wall.

Marcus looked around the room. Harry sat fixed behind his calculations. Agnes did not blink. Earl had gone back into silence. Lola was crying because of him. Carmen waited.

"Okay," Marcus said. "Fine. I'll tell him no."

He said it too fast.

Carmen heard it. Agnes heard it too; her eyes narrowed a fraction. Harry took his hands off the table and settled them in his lap.

"I'll call him tomorrow," Marcus said. He was already standing, already straightening his jacket. "I'll shut it down. It's done."

"Good," Harry said.

"Yeah." Marcus adjusted his collar without looking at anyone. "So. We good?"

Harry looked at Lola.

"Lola will walk you out," Harry said.

Lola wiped her face with the back of her hand and went to the hallway door. She leaned out, checked both directions — the lookout muscle, automatic even now — then turned back to Marcus. "Vamos, mijo," she said. He followed her out.

Their footsteps faded down the hall — Lola's shuffle, Marcus's heavier tread — and then there was only the building's night sounds: the ventilation cycling, a call bell pinging in the other wing, the hum of Devon's podcast leaking from the nurses' station.

***

Agnes picked up the coffee cups and stacked them. "He did not agree," she said. "He bought himself until tomorrow."

Harry looked at her. "I know."

"I will not sort oxycodone for that man," Agnes said. "Not one extra tablet. If reseller volume appears, it appears without my hands on it."

Earl pushed himself off the wall with a grunt. "Then watch the count."

He coughed into his fist, hard enough to fold at the waist, and left before anyone answered. His slippers made no sound on the linoleum.

Harry turned to Carmen. "Med room."

Carmen followed him out of the TV room and around the bend to the sub-station. Devon was still asleep with his head tipped back and one earbud hanging loose, the podcast host whispering about a missing woman no one in the building had time to look for. The medication room smelled like alcohol swabs, old plastic, and the chalky dust that lived on blister packs no matter how often she wiped the shelves. She unlocked the cabinet. Harry waited in the doorway with his chair angled sideways so the footrests would not clip the jamb. Agnes stood at the sink, hands flat on the counter, not touching anything.

"Since Burt came off," Agnes said, "there is no cushion. Not for oxycodone. He was our highest-yield patient and I already pulled those cards. If Marcus wants reseller volume, he needs a new source or he starts tearing holes in live orders."

The word patient hit harder than resident would have. Carmen slid out the oxycodone cards and lined them on the stainless counter. Ten milligrams. Five. The labels curled at the edges from months of damp hands and disinfectant.

"Then we make the holes visible," Harry said.

"They're visible to me," Carmen said. "That's the problem."

"More visible," Harry said. "Spot count every time he comes in the building. Not just shift change. If one tablet moves wrong, I want it before breakfast."

Carmen looked over her shoulder. "Tammy already clocks when I'm off pattern."

Harry didn't blink. "Then worry about that after you worry about trafficking."

Agnes pushed her glasses up with one finger. "He was right about one thing. Police see him first. The Board sees you first. The outcome is still ruin."

Carmen peeled back the first blister card. The pills sat under the clear plastic, chalky and intact. She counted aloud because if she did not, the numbers turned slippery in her head. "Eight. Sixteen. Twenty-four."

Harry listened with his hands folded on the blanket over his knees. Agnes watched the labels, not the pills. Carmen moved from card to card, matching sheet to plastic, plastic to drawer, drawer to log. No missing pockets. No punctured foil. No substituted tablets. Everything balanced. That only meant nothing had happened yet.

When she finished, she wrote the totals on a clean scrap of printer paper and then copied them to the controlled-substance sheet. Date. Time. Initials. The official line and the unofficial one sat side by side under her hand.

Harry read the scrap without touching it. "Keep that with you till shift change."

"I'm not starting a second record."

"You already did."

She looked at the paper, at her own handwriting, angry because he was right and more angry because he was making her stay right next to him while it happened.

Agnes took off her glasses and polished them with the hem of her cardigan. "If he comes to me asking for more outside the usual names, I will tell him no once. After that I stop speaking to him about product at all."

Product. Agnes said it the way she'd say stool sample or interaction risk, flat and exact.

Harry nodded. "Fine."

Agnes put her glasses back on. "This is not fine."

She left.

Harry stayed in the doorway while Carmen returned the cards to the cabinet. Metal touched metal. Plastic snapped. She locked the door and tugged it twice.

"He was right about the stop risk," Harry said. "Street exposure is his. Paper exposure is yours."

"I know."

"That doesn't make him right about the deal."

"I know that too."

He watched her for a beat. In the bright medication-room light he looked older than he had in the TV room, the skin under his eyes more purple, the cardigan hanging looser across his shoulders.

"If the count moves," he said, "you wake me first. If I can't stop it, you do what you threatened."

"You mean it?"

"Yes."

"Even if it burns you."

His mouth pulled once, not a smile. "Carmen, we're already in it."

Harry backed his chair out and turned toward his room. The wheel squeaked once before he was around the bend.

At the sub-station, the visitor log was still open where the evening receptionist had left it. Marcus's name sat on the line in neat block letters: WEBB, MARCUS. Relationship: nephew. Resident: Dolores Fernandez, 112A. Time in: 4:32 PM. Time out blank. No supervisor initials beside it. No overnight check mark either. Visitor control at Lakeview was mostly a clipboard and a shrug.

Carmen put her finger over the empty box. One line in blue ink and the lie would move from something everybody cooperated with into something she had certified herself. She could write 1:43 AM. She could write refused to sign out tonight. She could circle the entry and hand the whole page to a state investigator and watch the building start to come apart from there.

Instead she closed the book and slid it back under the clipboard tray. Devon snored once and shifted in the chair, the loose earbud dropping to his shoulder. If state walked in right then, they would get sleeping staff, unsigned visitors, and a locked cabinet that balanced on paper. She hated how ordinary that looked.

Carmen stayed in the medication room. She checked on Room 110 — the woman inside breathing with the mechanical rhythm of her oxygen concentrator. She checked on Burt in 116A, asleep with his mouth open, his hands loose on the blanket. Earl's shape was visible in the other bed, the rise and fall of his chest, the rattle audible from the doorway.

Back in the medication room, she counted again. It was the thing she did when she could not do anything else — opened the cabinet, logged the controlled substances, matched the numbers to the sheet. Everything balanced. The oxycodone was where it should be, the alprazolam, the tramadol. The count was clean.

The count was clean and Marcus had agreed too fast and there was now a second set of numbers in her pocket with no legal standing and too much meaning. Outside the medication room window the October dark sat on the parking lot. She was thirty-one years old and in too deep, and the only thing between her and what came next was the hope that a man who had spent the last hour explaining why he deserved more from this operation had not already decided to find it somewhere else.

She put the clipboard back on its hook and locked the cabinet.

She charted the count. She charted it accurately, the way she'd charted everything since the beginning — every number true, every timestamp real, every entry a faithful record of a system that concealed its losses inside the institution's tolerance for error. The charting was honest. Everything around it was not. Accuracy was turning into one more way to keep it running till morning. She closed the chart, put the pen down, and sat very still in the medication room's alcohol-and-pill-dust quiet, waiting for day shift.


# Chapter 14

In 110, the resident had gotten stool clear through the pad again, the third time that shift, and Tammy knew before she pulled back the sheet that the woman would be raw. The brief had twisted up in the crease of her thigh and let everything run where it wanted. Tammy rolled her onto her side, got one hand under the hip, and cleaned the mess out of the folds of skin while the woman stared at the wall and made the same low radiator sound she always made when anything hurt.

The woman had a red strip starting over the tailbone, not open yet but close enough that night shift would hand it a wound by morning if they left her flat. Tammy dried her carefully, spread the barrier cream thick with two gloved fingers, worked a dry sheet under her hips, snapped a fresh brief into place, and tucked a pillow at the small of her back so the pressure sat somewhere else for a while. She checked both heels before she pulled the blanket over her. The left one was hot. She floated it off the mattress with a folded towel.

By the time she tied the dirty linen into a bundle, the room smelled like shit, baby powder, and the sour plastic heat the old radiators threw when they first kicked on. Tammy carried the bag into the hall and the smell came with her. It always did. People who talked about this work like it was angels and service and all that never mentioned that part. A smell could stay in your scrubs through a whole ride home and still be there when you peeled them off.

She dumped the hamper in soiled utility, washed up to the elbows, and came back with a clean towel because the woman in 110 liked to rub at her chin after Tammy finished. The towel calmed her down sometimes. It was a small thing and it worked often enough that Tammy remembered it.

When she passed Agnes Chen's room, Carmen was still in there with the door pulled nearly shut. Med pass had ended forty minutes earlier. The cart sat locked at the station. Carmen stood by Agnes's chair talking low, not charting, not checking vitals, just talking in a voice Tammy could hear the shape of without catching words.

Agnes sat by the window in that cardigan she wore like it had grown onto her. Her chin stayed tucked. Her mouth stayed slack in the way that made agency people write words like withdrawn and nonverbal and poor historian. Her eyes did something else. They stayed on Carmen's mouth, then slid to Tammy when she crossed the doorway, then cut back to Carmen so fast it looked practiced.

Tammy kept walking. She had learned a long time ago that if you let people see you noticing them, they either shut down or started performing. Better to finish the job in front of you and file the rest where it belonged.

Mr. Delaney was waiting with the last of his pudding cup on the overbed table and both hands painted vanilla from wrist to thumb. He had Parkinson's and liked to insist he could manage the spoon himself even after the shake got ahead of him. Tammy wiped his fingers one at a time and straightened him more upright because if you left him half-slid in bed he would cough the whole cup back up and then night shift would say he aspirated out of nowhere. "You rushing me again?" he said. "I'm keeping your lungs out of trouble," Tammy said. "That's different." He sniffed, which was his way of conceding the point. When she set the water within reach, he caught the cup clean on the second try. That still counted. People at Cliff's level never understood that half this job was counting what still counted.

At ten-fifty-five Priya came on and Carmen gave count at the station. Tammy was tossing Delaney's wrapper when she heard the numbers. The oxycodone count was ninety. The alprazolam count was sixty. The signatures landed where they always landed. Priya looked tired, Carmen looked neat, and the clipboard looked as innocent as paper always did right before it got somebody fired.

Tammy waited until Priya moved off with the narcotic keys clipped to her waistband. Then she said, "What's going on with Chen?" Carmen didn't look up from the lock. "She's declining fluids again. I was documenting intake." "That took you forty minutes?" "I had charting behind it." Carmen clicked the lock once more after it was already shut, then adjusted her badge against her scrub top. "You know how it gets." "Yeah," Tammy said. "I do." Carmen gave her a quick work smile that had no heat in it. "I've got one note left."

She headed back down Wing A without her coat. Tammy watched the blue of her scrub top turn the corner and disappear around the bend that led toward the TV room and the back cluster of rooms. The move might have meant nothing. People left notes late. People caught up after rough shifts. People also lied in healthcare every day with charting open in front of them and a badge clipped over their heart.

Tammy did one more round before she clocked out because the aides on nights were always walking into somebody else's missed problem. In 110 she checked the pillow under the woman's back and made sure the heel was still clear. In 118A she pulled a wet washcloth out from under Mr. Hennessy's shoulder before it chilled the skin there. In 112A she found Lola Fernandez trying to button the top snap of a cardigan with fingers that would not hold still.

"Here," Tammy said, and took the cardigan gently so Lola would not have to pretend the tremor was just bad light. "I'm making a whole production out of it," Lola said. "You'd think it was a wedding dress." "It's a purple sweater from Kohl's." "Exactly. Too much ceremony." Tammy got the snap through and sat Lola back in the chair by the window. The room smelled like baby lotion and the mint hard candies Lola kept in the top drawer and forgot she had. On the dresser sat a folded reminder card for Roberto's Friday call, his number written in thick marker so Lola could not say she had misplaced it.

"Tomorrow's Friday," Lola said, glancing at the card. "He calls after Wheel. If I'm asleep, don't let me miss him." "I won't," Tammy said. Lola touched the crucifix at her throat as if checking that it had not wandered off. "Good. He gets touchy if I let it ring more than twice. He thinks Chicago people have things to do."

Two rooms later Tammy heard Lola ask Carmen what day it was in that soft drifting voice she used when families visited. Tammy stood in the doorway of 114A with a stack of washcloths in her arms and let the sound travel down the hall to her. The Friday call card was on the dresser. The Thursday amnesia was in Lola's throat. It was one more thing in the pile.

Harry Zielinski was in bed with his blanket folded straight across his lap and the television muttering a game show no one was watching. He looked past Tammy toward the hall.

"Eleanor coming?" he asked.

"Not tonight," Tammy said.

His face did the usual crumple then, the lost one, the old-man confusion that families recognized and staff charted and nobody could bill enough. But last Tuesday she had found him with both feet on the floor and his hand steady on the doorknob, clear as anybody, saying, No, I'm fine. Thank you, Tammy. He had used her name and shut his own door like a man who knew exactly what privacy was for. By breakfast he was back to asking Priya where Eleanor had parked.

Real decline had a shape to it. Tammy knew the shape. Mrs. Okafor had lost words by inches over months, as if somebody were sliding them off a shelf one at a time. Mr. Delaney had good hours and bad hours, but the good ones did not arrive only when certain doors were shut and certain people were nearby. Agnes had come in three years ago mad enough to fill legal pads with complaints. Then one week she went still all at once and the chart called it progression. Tammy had seen progression. Progression did not track conversation with its eyes.

When she clocked out at eleven-oh-six, Carmen's spot was still full by the side entrance.

Home was twelve minutes away if Route 27 cooperated. The studio in Dunellen would still be over Grace and the Christian station she kept on low enough to hear through her floor and high enough to call witnessing. The carpet pad would still smell like the cats of whoever had died or moved or gotten evicted there before her. Friday was technically her day off. None of that made her turn the key.

She backed into the far row facing Wing A, the one she'd used for fifteen years when she wanted ten minutes alone with vending-machine coffee or, back when she still smoked, a cigarette she told herself did not count because she had not bought the pack. From there, if the lights were on, you could see the last bank of windows around the bend, including the TV room. Inside the building the sub-station could not see that room at all. Devon barely saw what was in front of him.

It had stopped feeling random. Tammy was careful with big words because big words turned into incident reports and meetings with Cliff and documentation about team communication. Random was one oxy count off. Random was a travel nurse parking somebody in the TV room because he would not quit ringing. Random was not Carmen slipping into the same set of rooms after count and coming out with that fixed, tidy face.

The state survey had come through in September with clipboards and sensible shoes and fake plants near the lobby desk. Before that week, Carmen had been what she looked like: tired, fast, competent, sometimes prickly, mostly done with everybody by the end of a double. After the survey she started lingering in Harry's room and Agnes's room and Lola's room, with the doors mostly shut and explanations that sounded usable until you laid them side by side.

Tammy sat with the engine running and watched the staff entrance. She remembered Agnes on admission, tiny and furious, correcting the paperwork from the wheelchair because someone had listed the wrong blood-pressure medication. She remembered Agnes refusing applesauce because the thickener ratio was off. She remembered telling a social worker, plain as anything, that her daughter had sold her house out from under her. None of that matched the silent woman by the window now except the eyes.

She remembered Harry last Tuesday. She had been filling water pitchers after dinner because kitchen had shorted them again. Harry's door was open. She knocked with her hip, expecting Eleanor, maybe socks, maybe the bathroom. Instead he looked up level and said, No, I'm fine. Thank you, Tammy. Not slurred. Not circling. Then his hand reached across and pulled the door shut with a clean steady motion that said he had decided something and did not need help deciding it.

She remembered Lola this evening with Roberto's Friday call card on the dresser and the right day in her mouth, then Lola two minutes later asking Carmen what day it was. That one bothered Tammy in a separate way because she liked Lola. Everybody liked Lola if they had blood in them. Tammy had told her things in the shower room she would not have bothered telling people from her own family. The idea of Lola performing anything with Carmen in the room made Tammy feel mean, like she'd missed a joke everybody else had gotten.

None of it proved what it felt like it proved. That was the problem. In fifteen years at Lakeview she had seen a man turn blue on a piece of chicken while she tried the Heimlich on the dining-room floor and somebody from admin yelled for a clipboard. She had seen a night nurse sleeping in the med room with vodka on his breath. She had seen hearing-aid batteries go unordered for a month while Cliff hung fall-festival posters in the lobby and talked about community morale. Strange things happened in that building all the time. Most of them were just neglect wearing a clean shirt.

But Carmen behind 108A, 112A, and 114A after count had a pattern to it, and patterns were what Tammy trusted when feelings got expensive.

Cliff would get nothing from her until she had something he could not wave off as tone. Marcus Webb had gone to the ombudsman two years ago about understaffing and got fired for it. The understaffing stayed. Forty-two residents still got handed to one overnight aide with bad knees and a glowing phone and no prayer of making every round on time. Reporting at Lakeview was how you ended up in the office explaining your attitude while the original problem kept right on happening.

At eleven-twelve Priya crossed the station with a chart pressed to her chest. At eleven-twenty Devon was already folded over the sub-station desk, his shoulders rounded and his phone lighting his jaw blue. At eleven-thirty Carmen's car had not moved. Tammy shut off the engine and let the heater tick down into silence so she would hear if a side door opened.

The cold found the car fast. It came through the glass first, then through the steering wheel, then into her bad knuckles where she'd jammed two of them on a Hoyer lift years ago. She pulled her scrub jacket tighter and kept her eyes on the building. The parking lot lights buzzed. Somewhere down Route 27 a siren rose and flattened out again.

After fifteen years in one place Tammy knew facts that were useless anywhere else and useful as hell there. She knew which hall had the fluorescent tube that stuttered before it died and made Mrs. Moretti think lightning was happening indoors. She knew which rooms smelled like GI bleed before the labs came back. She knew which fire door swelled shut in August and which window on Wing A lined up with the far row if you parked two lanes back. She knew the TV room sat where nobody at the sub-station could watch it without leaving the desk.

She also knew what people did when they thought no one had the patience to notice. They took the long way down empty halls. They lowered their voice only after checking who was nearby. They used real explanations too often, wore them smooth, and called that honesty. Tammy had spent enough years cleaning up after everyone else's little deceptions to recognize the housekeeping around a larger one.

At twelve-oh-two a figure moved past the side-entry glass and vanished before she could place the height. At twelve-ten Priya walked into 116A with a med cup and came back out alone. At twelve-sixteen Devon stood, stretched, scratched his ribs under his scrub top, and disappeared toward the men's room. When he came back he sat down with his phone again. The building settled around its own noises. There was no alarm, no shout, and no crash of a fall.

Her mind did what it always did when nothing else could be done. It inventoried Agnes's eyes, Harry's clear sentence, Lola's Friday card, Carmen's car, Carmen's badge adjustment, Carmen's one note left, and Priya's signature under a count that balanced too politely. Each thing alone would have been the kind of half-story Tammy came home with every week. Together they made her stay.

At twelve-thirty the TV room light came on.

Tammy leaned forward until the seat belt bit her hip. The room was too far for faces, and one light by itself did not mean much. A resident might have wandered. Priya could have parked somebody there with cable and warm milk to keep the yelling off the hall. Staff used empty common rooms for phone calls sometimes, especially the travel nurses who did not want to cry where anybody knew their name.

Still, the TV room was the farthest common room from the station and the care plans said residents were supposed to be in bed by then unless something had gone wrong. The light stayed on. A shape crossed the window, low and wide like a wheelchair. Another shape moved in front of it and back again. Then the first shape shifted a little and held.

Tammy checked the dashboard clock, then the building again. She had no proof that anybody in that room was Carmen or Harry or Lola or Agnes. She had even less proof about why they would be there together after midnight with the blinds half-open and the station blind to it. What she had was the feel she got when she found a dark pressure spot on somebody's heel and knew she was looking at the start of a worse thing if no one changed course fast.

She stayed another ten minutes. No one came out the front entrance. No one used the side door she could see. The TV room light kept burning. When it finally went dark, it did not happen with the quick clean snap of somebody leaving. It clicked off and then on again for a second, as if a hand had missed the switch in the dark, and then it went black for good.

Tammy started the car.

Dunellen after one in the morning looked like somebody had set the town out neatly and forgotten to put people in it. The laundromat sign on North Avenue was dark. A pizza box leaned against the curb outside the closed nail place. Grace's porch light was still on below the apartment because Grace believed darkness invited men and raccoons in equal numbers and both were better discouraged early.

Tammy parked in back beside the leaning fence and sat for a minute with the engine off. The quiet here was not real quiet. The freight line a few blocks over kept up a low iron mutter. A dog barked once and got told to shut up through a window. From below came the thin wash of Christian radio through Grace's ceiling, some woman singing about surrender like it was a thing you chose with clean hands.

The outside stairs held the day's cold in the metal rail. Tammy climbed them with her purse pulling at one shoulder and the smell of the nursing home still warm in her scrubs. By the second landing she could already smell the apartment before she opened it, that old cat smell ground into carpet pad and heated back up every time the radiator pipes clicked alive.

The studio gave her the same first hit it always did: cat piss under lemon cleaner, radiator steam, the stale grease that lived in the little kitchen no matter how often she wiped the vent, and the sweet candle Grace kept burning downstairs that came through the floorboards when the place got warm. The room was one rectangle with a half-wall pretending to separate the bed from the couch and a window unit she had already wrapped in plastic for winter. The plastic snapped softly whenever the draft pushed at it.

She locked the door, set the purse on the counter, and hung her jacket on the brass hook she had unscrewed from the hallway of the old house the last afternoon before Greg changed the garage code. He had installed it himself because he hated clutter on chairs. The screws were stripped now, and the hook leaned a little left, but it held.

The couch sagged in the middle where a spring had gone. The Walmart television reflected her back at herself in a black square: hair coming loose from the knot, teal scrub top dark under the arms, face older at one in the morning than it had any right to be at forty-three. She toed off her shoes and felt grit through one sock because this place could manufacture grit out of nowhere.

She went into the bathroom first because if she sat down before washing up she would stay there. The overhead light made everything look the color of phlegm. She stripped, dropped the scrubs in the hamper, and stood under water that took too long to turn hot. When it did, the heat found every split in her hands. She scrubbed until the smell backed off from her skin, though never all the way. It never all the way left. Care work stayed in the seams.

The towel rack rattled every time the downstairs toilet flushed. Grace flushed twice in quick succession while Tammy was drying off, then the radio got louder for a minute. Tammy pulled on old sweatpants and one of the Lakeview T-shirts they gave staff for survey week, the navy one with the logo cracking across the chest. She had three of them. She wore them because free was free.

The fridge held half a gallon of milk, yellow mustard, eggs, a heel of wheat bread, and the plastic grocery bag with two apples going soft inside it. There was leftover macaroni from Tuesday in a margarine tub and a jar of store-brand salsa she had bought because it was cheaper than decent. On the freezer door she kept a magnet clip with the gas receipt from Tuesday, the car-insurance reminder, and the electric bill she was pretending not to see until after next payday.

Her pay stub from the last check sat under the magnet too, folded twice. She took it down and carried it to the little table by the window that served as desk, kitchen, and landing zone for everything she was too tired to deal with standing up. The chair wobbled unless she kept one foot braced against the table leg. Greg had taken the good kitchen set because technically his mother had bought it for them. Tammy had kept the folding card table from a yard sale and two chairs that never matched.

The stub still said $16.50 an hour in print so crisp it felt insulting. A decent month, meaning one where she picked up enough extra shifts and nobody cut hours because census dipped, left her around twenty-two hundred clear. She turned over a junk-mail envelope and wrote the number down.

Then she started subtracting the pieces she knew by muscle memory. Rent was four hundred seventy-five. The car payment was two-forty. Insurance took one-eighteen. The phone cost eighty-two because every time she tried to lower the plan some man in a headset acted like she was asking to live without electricity. Gas varied, but it was never under ninety and usually worse. Groceries were manageable only if she behaved. Ibuprofen was not optional. Coin laundry kept taking quarters. The copay came due when her left shoulder acted up. The dentist bill still sat there because owing them in daylight felt different from owing them on paper at one in the morning.

She did not write in nice straight columns. She wrote the way she charted when the shift had gone bad, hard enough to dent the sheet under it. By the time she reached the bottom of the envelope, the number left over looked like a joke somebody from administration would call budgeting. It was enough for cereal, cat litter if she had a cat, which she did not, and whatever fresh insult the Honda decided to become next.

She opened the drawer for another pen because the first one had gone dry, and the manila folder with the divorce papers caught on the lip and slid halfway out. It always did that. Everything in a studio ended up jammed against everything else. The Post-it was still clipped to the front because she had never taken it off.

Sorry, T, Greg had written in the round block print he used for grocery lists, oil-change dates, and the note on the old freezer that said eat this first. He had left it on the counter the day he took the rest of his shirts and the drill set and whatever nerve he had needed to decide twelve years of marriage could be boiled down to two words and an initial. Tammy stood there with the dead pen in her hand and looked at it until Grace's radio bled up through the floor with a preacher saying the word grace three times in thirty seconds.

Greg had the house now. Greg had the couch that did not sag, the real mattress, the yellow Fiesta dishes from their wedding, and the blond Verizon woman from Woodbridge Tammy had once looked up on Facebook after half a bottle of Yellow Tail and two nights of bad sleep. The woman had a beach picture with Greg's hand around her waist and a smile that said she had never once had to turn a grown man in bed by herself. Tammy had stared at the photo longer than she meant to, then hated herself for handing them another witness.

She put the folder back too hard and the drawer bounced. Something in the silverware tray rattled like a cheap bell. Her face felt hot, then stupid for feeling hot. Divorce was old news. She knew that. Old news still had a way of waiting under things for the hour when there was no one around to watch you get hit by it again.

On the counter sat the half bottle of Yellow Tail from three weekends ago, shoved behind the toaster. She took it out, looked at the inch left in the bottom, and put it back without pouring. She had work-brain still, and work-brain did not do better after wine. It did arithmetic and replayed rooms and let every small weird thing stand under a bright light until it either made sense or kept refusing to.

The apartment had settled into its night sounds by then. The radiator knocked twice in the wall behind the bed. The plastic over the window gave a dry little pop. Grace coughed downstairs, turned her radio down, then back up one notch because down was never enough. A car went by outside too fast for the street and carried bass with it for three seconds, then the whole place was small and still again.

Tammy looked at the envelope covered in numbers and realized she had been writing about Carmen's shift in the same hard pressure without meaning to, about the count and the car still there and the TV room. If she let the thought sit there unmarked, it would blur by morning into one more thing she was almost sure about and could not prove.

She flipped the envelope to the clean side.

At the top she wrote the date. Under it she printed: At 10:55 p.m., Priya signed the count. On the next line she wrote: At 11:30 p.m., Carmen's car was still there. On the next she wrote: At 12:30 a.m., the TV room light was on and there was movement inside. She added Wing A because details mattered later when people started pretending they had never understood the layout.

Then she stood there with the pen point on the paper and added a few more things she had not meant to write. Carmen was in Agnes's room after med pass. Lola asked for Friday with Roberto's card on the dresser. Harry was clear on Tuesday night. She stared at the last line a second, then left it. If she was wrong, she wanted to be wrong in specifics.

The purse lay open on the couch with a pack of gum, two crumpled receipts, hand lotion, and the little zip pouch where she kept her spare work badge and twenty-dollar emergency bill. She folded the envelope twice and slid it into the pouch behind the bill. It felt stupid and dramatic, like evidence in a show her mother would have watched, but she did it anyway.

Her phone sat plugged in by the bed on a charger that had to be balanced just right or it stopped connecting. She set the alarm for 11:15 p.m. Friday, then another for 11:20 because she did not trust the first one or the charger or herself to sleep through the whole evening the one night she actually could. Friday was supposed to be laundry, a grocery run, maybe vacuuming if she could stand the machine noise. Friday was not supposed to end in the Lakeview parking lot.

She brushed her teeth at the kitchen sink because the bathroom mirror made her feel cornered. The toothpaste tasted medicinal and cheap. When she spit, she saw a thread of pink in the foam where she'd brushed too hard at the gum above the back molar that liked to complain. Another thing not worth a dentist bill until it became impossible to ignore.

In bed, the sheet felt cold on the side that stayed uncovered all day and warm in the middle where the mattress held body heat like a bad memory. She lay on her back and looked at the water stain spreading over the ceiling in the shape of Ohio. Greg used to say it looked more like a mitten. Tammy had spent two winters correcting him and then another year missing the sound of being able to correct somebody in the dark.

Sleep did not come because the chapter of the night had not closed. Her brain kept running the same strip of film: Carmen adjusting the badge, Agnes's eyes cutting over, Lola's Friday card, the TV room window lighting up around the bend. She could still see the shape in the glass, low and wide, maybe a wheelchair, maybe not. It was close enough to matter and not close enough to use.

She turned on her side, then back again. The place smelled cleaner after the shower but the old-cat heat lived under it and kept rising whenever the pipes knocked on. Somewhere below, Grace laughed at something the radio host said and then went quiet. Tammy put one hand over her face and breathed into the heel of it until the itch to get up passed.

If she took it to Cliff tomorrow, he would ask whether she had seen an actual policy violation. He would ask whether fatigue might be coloring her interpretation. He would say Carmen was under strain and Lakeview needed team trust. He would mean protect the license on the narc book until somebody with a credential forced his hand. Tammy had watched that movie enough times to know the ending.

If she was wrong and said nothing, she had wasted a night and maybe some dignity. If she was right and said something too early, whoever was doing whatever they were doing would stop long enough to clean it up. Fifteen years in one building had taught her that timing mattered almost as much as truth. Sometimes it mattered more.

She got up once to check that the alarm had saved and once more to make sure the envelope was really in the purse. On the second trip she stood by the window and looked down at Grace's porch light pooling yellow on the patchy grass. The whole building smelled faintly of old cooking oil and damp carpet and somebody's lavender plug-in fighting a war it could not win.

When she lay back down, she left the bedside lamp off and listened to the radiator tapping in the wall. Tomorrow night she would back into the far row again. She would bring a notebook this time, not a junk-mail envelope. She would watch longer if she had to. She was tired of half-knowing things.

Friday was supposed to be her day off. She closed her eyes and kept the alarm set anyway.


# Chapter 15

Earl woke at 5:14. Fifty years on the plant's schedule had fixed that hour in him, and nothing since had reset it. Not retirement. Not Maryann dying. Not this place. His body was a shift worker's body. It didn't know how to sleep past the whistle.

The room was dark except for the bar of hallway light under the door. Burt breathed in the other bed, a shallow wet sound like a straw pulled through the last of something. Since the fall, Burt slept fourteen, fifteen hours a day. When he was awake he spoke in Hungarian, to his dead wife, to people Earl couldn't see. The doctors said the concussion had accelerated what was already happening. The word they used was "trajectory." Earl understood the word. He'd seen enough trajectories. Steel cooling too fast, a man's back giving out at fifty-two, a local folding after the last round of layoffs. Things headed one direction.

He lay still for ten minutes, listening. The overnight nurse, not Carmen but the one named Janet who walked heavy in her sneakers, passed the door at 5:21. A call bell rang down the hall. The heating system ticked in the walls, radiators warming up for the morning, the building's pipes expanding with a sound like knuckles cracking.

Then the cough came.

It started low, below the ribs, a pressure that built before it broke. Earl sat up and pressed the handkerchief to his mouth and let it work through him. Deep, wet, a sound like something loose and heavy turning over in his chest. His eyes watered. His shoulders seized. He coughed until the muscles between his ribs burned, and then he coughed past that, because the cough didn't care about pain. It had its own schedule, its own clock, and it ran until it was done.

Burt didn't stir. Six months ago, Burt would have said something. "Earl, you all right?" Or he would have muttered in English about the noise. Now nothing. The room took the sound in with everything else: the smell of disinfectant and old skin, the hum of Burt's oxygen concentrator, the thinning light of late October coming through a window that faced the parking lot.

Earl got up and went to the bathroom. He closed the door and turned on the light and held the handkerchief under the fluorescent bar.

White. Clean.

He checked the folds with the same care he'd once given a weld seam, looking for the stain that meant the joint was bleeding. Nothing. He folded the handkerchief into quarters and put it in the pocket of the pants he'd laid on the towel bar the night before. Khakis, a flannel shirt, the brown jacket that still fit because he hadn't gained a pound in thirty years. He'd just lost them, slowly. That was how things left you in here.

It was outing day. Last Thursday of the month.

He'd set everything out before lights-out: the clothes, the jacket, and in the jacket's inside pocket, the greeting card. He checked it now, fingers finding the envelope by feel. It was a generic "Thinking of You" card, bought from the rack in the lobby near the front desk, where Lakeview sold greeting cards and puzzle books and small stuffed animals for visiting grandchildren. Inside the card, Earl had written "Love, Dad" and nothing else, because Denise wouldn't see it and the person who would didn't need a message. Taped flat against the inside of the card, covered by a second piece of cardstock Earl had cut from a cereal box, were forty-six alprazolam tablets and twenty oxycodone. Each pill fixed to a strip of medical tape, arranged in rows like a sheet of postage stamps. Agnes had counted them out of the bottles she still allowed into the count, most of it Earl's own. Burt's had been out since the fall. Earl had taped them. The work had taken twenty minutes the night before, seated on the edge of his bed while Burt muttered about Budapest.

He dressed. He shaved with the electric razor the facility required. No blades because of fall risk, as if he hadn't handled a straight razor for fifty years without cutting himself. The razor buzzed against his jaw and he looked at himself in the mirror: an old man, tall but stooped now, the stoop getting worse each month. His face was thinner than it had been in September. He could see that. The cheekbones more prominent, the skin looser along the jaw. Eighty-one. He'd been bigger once. Two hundred and ten pounds on the shop floor, arms that could hold a beam steady while the crane operator repositioned. Now one-sixty, maybe less. He didn't weigh himself. He didn't want the number.

He brushed his teeth, rinsed, spat. He went back into the room and sat in the chair by the window and waited.

---

The van left at ten. Phil, the maintenance man who doubled as driver, had it running in the circle drive with the heat on. Phil was fifty-eight and had a bad knee from a fall off a scaffold in '09. It wasn't a football story or a war story, just a man who climbed a scaffold for fifteen dollars an hour and came down wrong. He walked with a careful, testing limp that worsened in the cold, and he kept AM sports talk on the radio at a volume just above silence, a murmur of scores and opinions, low and constant as pipe noise in the halls.

Earl was the only passenger. He climbed into the second row, holding the grab bar with his left hand. Phil waited, watching in the rearview, but didn't offer help. Phil had driven Earl to the cemetery seven times and had learned early that Earl didn't want a hand up.

"Same place?" Phil said.

"Yeah."

They pulled out of the circle drive and onto Route 27. The trees along the road had turned, but the color was already going. The maples were brown and bare in patches, the oaks holding yellow leaves that rattled in a wind Earl couldn't feel through the van's windows. The sky was white-gray, an end-of-October lid over central New Jersey. He watched the strip malls go by. A nail salon, a check-cashing place, a laundromat with a hand-lettered sign in the window: WASH & FOLD $1.25/LB. He'd used a place like that once, after Maryann went to the hospital the last time and he was doing his own laundry for the first time in forty years. He'd stood at the counter with a garbage bag of clothes and the woman had weighed it and told him the price and he'd paid without speaking because paying someone to wash his clothes, when his wife was dying and his life was changing in ways he couldn't reverse, had closed his throat. Nine dollars and seventy-five cents. He remembered the amount. He remembered everything in amounts.

The pension was $2,340 a month. Medicaid took $2,290 as his patient responsibility, his share of the nursing home bill, computed by a formula he'd read three times and understood perfectly and considered a legalized theft. That left fifty dollars. That was his personal needs allowance. The carnations on his lap had cost $4.49, almost a tenth of what the state said he needed for a month. Fifty dollars bought toothpaste, electric razor heads, stamps, the greeting cards from the lobby rack. It did not buy flowers twice. It did not buy anything for his grandchildren. It did not buy a goddamn thing for Denise.

Denise was forty-four. Home health aide in Trenton, $14.80 an hour, no benefits she could afford to use. Three kids: Keisha, thirteen; Jaylen, ten; Amara, six. Rent was $1,100 for a two-bedroom in a building where the hallway smelled like cooking grease and somebody else's weed. Her Altima had 180,000 miles on it and kept making a belt noise her mechanic said could wait, which meant it couldn't.

The $400 a month Earl sent paid for after-school for Jaylen and Amara. The program didn't bargain. In August Denise had called him from the parking lot, trying to keep her voice level while the aide waited in her coat and the two younger kids sat on the curb with their backpacks between their knees. She was one late payment from losing the slot. Earl had sent the money order the next morning.

He'd told Denise the money was a VA supplemental benefit. Aid and Attendance, he'd called it. A real program, which made the lie better. He'd looked it up at the facility library, on the one computer in the activity room that still connected to the internet, until he could talk about it like a man who'd filed the paperwork himself. She hadn't asked much. Just, "Daddy, are you sure? Four hundred a month?" He'd said, "The government owes me. Korea." It was close enough to an explanation that she let it stand. Four hundred wasn't a fix. It kept the kids out of an empty apartment till Denise got off shift.

Phil turned onto Raritan Road. The cemetery was another fifteen minutes.

"Mind if we stop?" Earl said. He'd said it the same way every month. Phil knew the stop.

"ShopRite?"

"Yeah."

Phil pulled into the lot. Earl got out and went in. The flower bucket was by the entrance, between the shopping carts and a display of pumpkins marked down to $2.99. He picked up a bunch of carnations in cellophane, white and pink, the stems cut short, a sprig of baby's breath that was already browning at the edges. $4.49. He paid with a five at the self-checkout and put the change in his jacket pocket with the fifty-one cents already there. He walked back to the van, holding the flowers low against his hip, and climbed in without help.

Phil pulled out. The sports radio murmured about the Giants' offensive line. Earl held the flowers on his lap and watched the road.

---

The cemetery was off a county road south of New Brunswick, set back from the street behind a stone wall that had been pointed and repointed so many times the mortar was thicker than the stones. The grounds were maintained by a crew that came Tuesdays. Earl knew because Maryann's plot was always trimmer on Wednesdays, the grass clipped close, the edges clean. By Thursday the first day's growth had softened things. He preferred it.

Phil parked the van in the small lot near the maintenance shed. Three other cars: visitors, or maybe the office staff. And Marcus's Corolla, dark blue, parked at the end of the row facing out.

Earl didn't look at it directly. He sat in the second row with the flowers on his lap and waited.

Marcus knew the routine. Within a minute his Corolla pulled forward and stopped alongside the van, driver's window to Earl's window, close enough that the side mirrors nearly touched. Marcus was wearing a gray hoodie, no jacket, which was too little for the weather but Marcus always ran warm.

"Hey," Marcus said through his open window.

Earl cranked the van's window down. It was an old van, no power anything. The cold came in raw, already at work before Halloween was done. He reached into his jacket pocket and pulled out the greeting card envelope and passed it through. Marcus took it and slid it under his thigh without looking at it. They'd done this seven times. The motions were set.

Marcus reached across to his passenger seat and handed a white envelope through the gap between the vehicles. Business-size, sealed. Four hundred dollars in twenties. Earl put it in his inside jacket pocket, the one the greeting card had been in. The pockets traded contents. An empty one filled, a full one emptied.

"How you doing?" Marcus asked.

"Getting by."

"Keisha still getting those grades?"

Earl looked at him. Marcus knew about the grandkids because Marcus asked about them every month, and every month Earl gave him the same amount of information: enough to answer, not enough to invite more. "She's doing all right."

"Good." Marcus drummed his fingers on the wheel. He had something else to say. Earl could see it in the way he held his jaw, slightly open, the next sentence waiting. But Marcus looked at Earl's face and decided against it. Smart. Marcus was smarter than the others gave him credit for.

"I'll see you," Earl said. He cranked the window back up. Marcus put his window up and pulled out of the lot, and Earl watched the Corolla turn onto the county road and disappear behind the stone wall. Phil glanced in the rearview but said nothing. A greeting card passed between vehicles at a cemetery. It could be anything. Phil had no reason to wonder.

"Take your time," Phil said. He was already settling back with the thermos. He'd wait. He always waited. Earl figured Phil used the cemetery runs as a break: an hour of sitting in a warm van listening to the radio while the building didn't need anything from him. Earl didn't blame him. The building needed too much from everyone.

Earl took the flowers and got out. The cold was worse standing in it. The leaves on the cemetery's oaks had gone amber and were falling in the wind, scraping across the pavement in clusters, collecting along the curbs and against the headstones like small drifts. He walked carefully because the asphalt had cracks and his knees weren't what they were, but his gait was steady. He'd been walking with bad knees since sixty-two, and he knew how to distribute his weight.

The cough came as he crossed the lot, a single bark followed by a tightness that he swallowed down. He cleared his throat and walked into the cemetery.

Maryann was in Section C, fifth row, nine stones in from the path. He knew the count cold: steps from the van to her plot (sixty-four), headstones between the path and hers (eight others, then hers), years since she'd died (four), years they'd been married (forty-three). The headstone was gray granite, modest. He'd picked it himself, the cheapest one that wasn't flimsy, $1,800 installed, paid in three installments that he'd finished six months after the funeral. MARYANN ELOISE WASHINGTON. 1944–2022. BELOVED WIFE AND MOTHER.

He'd wanted to add something else but couldn't think of what. The woman at the monument company had suggested "Forever In Our Hearts" and Earl had looked at her until she stopped talking. Maryann was in the ground, not in his heart. His heart was a muscle that pumped blood. She was here, in this dirt, in this box, and that plain, immovable, inarguable fact was what he came to visit. Not a feeling. A location.

He took the old flowers from the bronze vase mounted on the stone's base. They were last month's carnations, dried to brown paper, with the cellophane faded around them. He put the new ones in. The vase had an inch of rain-blackened water in it. He didn't change it. The flowers would sit in the old water for a month and nobody would know and it wouldn't matter. He put the dead flowers in the cellophane wrapper from the new ones and set the bundle on the ground to throw away on the way out.

Then he stood there.

"Keisha made honor roll again," he said. His voice was quiet but not a whisper. He talked to Maryann at normal volume. He'd never whispered here. "Second quarter straight. Denise said she wants to do some science program at the community college in the spring, the Saturday one. It costs something. I'll figure it out."

The wind moved the carnations in the vase. A leaf scraped across the base of the headstone and caught in the dead grass at the plot's edge.

"Jaylen got in a fight. Not a bad one. He pushed a kid who said something about his shoes. Denise had to go in and talk to the vice principal. He's not a bad kid. He's ten. You remember what I was like at ten." He paused. Maryann would have said something here. She always had something to say, always had a story or a correction or a piece of context that made the thing he'd said larger than he'd meant it. She'd have said, "Earl, you were worse than Jaylen and you know it," and she'd have been right, and he'd have looked at her and she'd have known he agreed without him saying so.

"Amara drew me a picture. Denise mailed it. It's a fish." He almost smiled. "A purple fish with legs. I don't know what it's supposed to be. She wrote my name on it. She wrote GRANDPA and the R is backward."

He coughed. Not a bad one, just the chest reminding him it was there, the way a foreman tapped you on the shoulder before he said something you didn't want to hear.

"Denise is tired," he said. "She's always tired. She doesn't say it but I can hear it. She sounds like you did, the last year. Not sick-tired. Life-tired." He stopped. He didn't want to say more about that. He didn't want to compare Denise to Maryann's last year, because Maryann's last year had an ending he could see from here: the headstone, the dirt, the dried flowers on the ground.

He stood with his hands at his sides. The cemetery was quiet. A maintenance truck idled somewhere on the far side, near the newer section, and a woman in a red coat was walking between headstones three rows over, but she was far enough away to be a shape, not a person. The oaks filtered the gray light, and the shadows of bare branches moved on the ground like something being drawn and erased, drawn and erased.

"I'm all right," he told her. It was the same answer he'd given Marcus: enough to end a conversation. Maryann would have known it was a lie. She always knew when he was lying, because he lied so rarely that the sound of it was distinctive, like a machine running rough, a noise you noticed because the machine usually ran clean.

He didn't tell her about the ring. He'd never told her about the ring, not in four years of monthly visits, and at first it was because the ring hadn't started yet. Then it was because he didn't know how to explain it to a woman who'd believed in rules as firmly as she'd believed in church. It wasn't because she was naive. The rules were supposed to protect people like them, and breaking them was a concession she'd have struggled with. Maybe she'd have understood eventually. Maybe Keisha's science program and Jaylen's after-school and Amara's purple fish would've been enough. But he wasn't sure, and so he didn't say it.

He didn't tell her about the cough.

He picked up the dead flowers and nodded once at the headstone. It was a small motion, barely visible, hardly more than an acknowledgment that he'd be back next month. Then he walked back toward the parking lot. Sixty-four steps. The envelope in his pocket. The flowers in the trash can by the maintenance shed. His knees holding.

Phil had the engine running. The radio was on a commercial. Earl climbed in and pulled the door shut and Phil backed out without asking how it went, because Phil had learned that too.

They drove back. Earl watched New Jersey go by through the window and ran through tomorrow's money order. The post office in the lobby had the forms, and the facility's outgoing mail went out Monday mornings. Four hundred dollars. Payable to Denise Washington. In the memo line he'd write "VA A&A Nov," same as every month: an abbreviation of a benefit he'd never applied for, a code that meant his daughter could pay for her children's afternoons.

---

That night, after dinner, Earl lay in bed and listened to Burt breathe. Dinner had been pork chop with applesauce, mashed potatoes from a box, green beans that had been green once.

The room was dim. The hallway light came under the door in its permanent stripe. Burt's oxygen concentrator hummed at a pitch Earl's ears had long ago filed as background. They'd done the same with the plant's compressors forty years earlier. Then he'd retired and found out silence could be louder.

The envelope hung in his jacket on the hook behind the bathroom door. Somewhere in Marcus's car, the greeting card was being inventoried and sorted for people whose insurance had failed them or never existed. Agnes kept the records. Harry kept the system running. Lola watched the door. Burt had been out of the count since the fall; now he was just the man in the other bed, breathing through the concentrator. Carmen had crossed a line to protect them and would cross another eventually. Earl could see it in her face, that old shop-floor look of somebody deciding whether to file a grievance. Some people moved toward the right thing slowly, testing each step. Carmen was testing.

The cough came.

He'd been waiting for it. It always came at night, worse than the morning, as if his chest saved it up during the day and released it in the dark. He rolled onto his side and pressed the handkerchief to his mouth and coughed until his ribs ached and his throat felt scraped raw and his eyes streamed. The sound was bad. Wet. Something deep in the left lung shifted and rattled, a noise he could feel in his back, between the shoulder blades, as if someone were shaking a box of broken parts inside him.

He coughed for two minutes. Maybe three. When it stopped, it didn't stop cleanly. It tapered off, each cough weaker than the last, his body losing the force to produce them. He lay on his side with his mouth open, breathing in small gulps, his hand closed around the handkerchief.

Burt slept through it. Burt slept through everything now.

Earl sat up. He reached for the lamp on the nightstand, a small lamp with a forty-watt bulb that made everything in the room the color of weak tea, and turned it on.

He opened the handkerchief.

White. Still white.

He checked every fold. He held it six inches from his face and examined each surface under the lamp's yellow light. He was looking for pink. For rust. For the stain that would mean the cough had crossed from nuisance to fact, from something he could ignore to something his body wouldn't let him. Hemoptysis. He'd looked up the word on the activity room computer, the same computer where he'd researched the VA benefit. He'd read the causes. He knew what the cough could be. He knew the names for it. He didn't want the name. He wanted the handkerchief to stay white, because white meant he could wait one more day.

If he went to Dr. Prasad and said the word "cough," certain things would happen. There'd be a chest X-ray and blood work, maybe a hospital run if the film showed anything ugly. Lakeview did med reconciliation every time somebody came back from outside care. Orders got compared, bottles got pulled, controlled meds got held till the paperwork matched. He'd watched it after Burt's fall: once the hospital touched Burt's chart, Agnes pulled Burt's prescriptions from the table and they never went back. If a scan found something in Earl's lung, his own oxycodone and alprazolam could disappear into that same process by tomorrow or next week. That part was fact.

What came after was the part he couldn't prove. Maybe Harry and Agnes could cover the loss for a month. Maybe Marcus could move less and Denise would still get her envelope. Maybe the whole thing would wobble and keep standing. Earl didn't know. Fear kept filling in the blanks anyway. In fear's version Denise lost the four hundred, Jaylen lost after-school, and Amara ended up with whatever neighbor Denise could find at six-thirty.

He sat on the edge of the bed with the handkerchief in his lap and his bare feet on the cold linoleum. His ankles were thin. His feet were ashy and the nails were thick and yellow, probably fungal. That was what happened when you were old and your blood didn't reach your feet the way it used to. Carmen trimmed them for him every two weeks, kneeling on the floor with clippers, not flinching, not making a face. He'd never asked her to. She just did it.

Telling someone meant running the list in his head, set out like a grievance chain. Carmen would want him to see the doctor. Harry would calculate the operational impact. Agnes would look at him with her pharmacist's eyes and know more from the sound of the cough than the X-ray would show. Denise would drive up from Trenton and sit in the chair by the window and cry, and then what? What would she do with the information? What could she do? She'd worry. She'd add his dying to the list of things she managed on fourteen dollars and eighty cents an hour. He'd give her that weight and take nothing off the pile.

He put the handkerchief back in his pocket. The jacket hung on the hook. The envelope was in the pocket. Tomorrow: the post office form, the money order, the memo line, the outgoing mail. Next month: the same. The month after: the same. As long as the handkerchief stayed white.

He went back instead to one February in the split-level in Piscataway, him at the kitchen table after second shift trying to hide a wad of brown-streaked tissues under the ashtray. Maryann had lifted the ashtray, opened the top tissue, and looked at him until he picked up the phone. She didn't make a speech or start an argument. She just shoved Dr. Klein's number across on the grocery pad and waited. It had turned out to be bronchitis. The antibiotics cost two missed shifts, and Maryann pulled the copay out of the grocery envelope without saying what else that week would go without. If she were here now, the lie would've lasted until breakfast.

Instead he talked to her grave once a month and kept the important parts for himself: no ring, no cough, no fear. He told her about the grandkids and the small facts of their lives, and there was nobody there to push back.

Earl turned off the lamp. He lay back down and pulled the blanket to his chest and closed his eyes. The cough was still there. He could feel it waiting, a heaviness behind his sternum, not ready to surface but not gone either. It would come again. It came every night now. And every morning he would check, and the handkerchief would be white or it wouldn't, and the day he saw color on the cloth would be the day the clock he'd been ignoring started ticking loud enough for everyone to hear.

Not today.

He kept one hand over his sternum and counted off the breaths that would get him to morning.


# Chapter 16

Agnes didn't touch people. That was the first thing Carmen had learned about her, back in September when she was still figuring out which residents bit and which ones just looked like they might — Agnes kept a pharmacist's distance from everyone, hands either sorting pill bottles or folded in her lap or gripping the armrests of her chair like she was about to stand but had decided against it. Agnes's hands were instruments, not social tools.

So when Agnes's fingers closed around Carmen's wrist at the sub-station just after eleven, Carmen stopped charting mid-word.

Agnes was in performance mode. Slack face, unfocused eyes, the institutional shuffle of someone who'd had a stroke and never fully recovered. She looked like she was wandering — an 11 PM bathroom trip, maybe, or one of those aimless hall-walks the genuinely confused residents did when their sleep cycles scrambled. But her grip was wrong. Too exact, too located. Two fingers on the inside of Carmen's wrist, right over the pulse point, the way a pharmacist takes hold of a patient who's about to do something stupid with a prescription.

"Mrs. Chen?" Carmen said, loud enough for Devon to hear. Devon was asleep at the main station with his phone propped against the keyboard, a hunting podcast droning through his earbuds. "You need something? Bathroom?"

Agnes's eyes flicked down the hall toward Wing A. Her grip tightened once, then released. She turned and walked toward Harry's room, her slippers scuffing the linoleum in that halting, post-stroke rhythm she'd perfected — a shuffle so convincing that Dr. Prasad had renewed her mobility restrictions twice without questioning them.

Carmen followed.

Room 114A was at the far end of Wing A, single occupancy because Harry had outlasted three roommates and the facility hadn't bothered to fill the second bed. The door was open four inches. Agnes pushed through without knocking. Carmen stepped in behind her and pulled it shut.

Harry was standing between his bed and the window. Not holding anything, not leaning — standing with his weight evenly distributed, his shoulders squared under the faded blue cardigan that he wore to every meeting, buttoned wrong tonight, one button off so the whole thing hung crooked on his thin frame. He was eighty-four years old and he looked it in a way that the performance usually hid — the pouches under his eyes, the scabbed knuckles where he'd knocked them on the bed rail, the white stubble he'd missed shaving because his right hand wasn't as steady as it used to be and that wasn't part of any act.

He was holding the shared prepaid phone.

"Read it," he said. Real voice. Short, flat, final.

Carmen took the phone. The screen was cracked across one corner — it had been cracked since August, since Earl dropped it getting out of the van at the cemetery. A text message, two hours old, from a number she didn't recognize. Not one of the ring's buyer contacts, not any number she'd seen in Agnes's notebooks.

*can do 200 oxy 5s and 100 bars wkly. same price. lmk about thurs*

Carmen read it once. Then again, because the numbers were wrong. They had to be wrong. The ring moved eighty, maybe ninety oxycodone a month. A hundred in a flush month when Dr. Prasad added a new script. Two hundred a week was eight hundred a month. And bars — a hundred Xanax a week, four hundred a month.

"He sent it to our phone," Agnes said. She was sitting on the radiator cover beneath the window, her spine very straight, her hands pressed flat against her thighs. Not folded, not sorting. Pressed, like she was holding them down. "He meant to send it to the buyer. He sent it here instead."

"Two hundred a week," Harry said. He wasn't looking at Carmen. He was looking at the photograph taped above his closet — the landscaping truck with ZIELINSKI & SON painted on the door in green letters. "We have never moved two hundred in a month."

"He is sourcing elsewhere," Agnes said. "Or he is promising volume he cannot deliver. Either scenario is a problem I did not agree to."

The radiator under Agnes clicked and hissed. Late October in central New Jersey, and Lakeview's heating system ran like it did everything else — unevenly, with long gaps between effort. The room smelled like Ben-Gay and the metallic dryness of forced air that wasn't quite warm enough to be comfortable.

"When did this come in?" Carmen asked.

"Nine fourteen," Harry said. "Agnes was checking the phone for a delivery confirmation from last week. She found this instead."

Carmen set the phone on the nightstand, next to Harry's water cup and the crossword puzzle book he never opened. Her fingers were tingling, which was stupid — her body reacting like this was a medical emergency, the adrenaline dump that happened when a fall alarm went off or a resident coded. But nobody was coding. Nobody was hurt. A text message on a cracked prepaid phone, and the numbers on the screen meant the thing she'd spent months trying to hold together had already come apart.

"Call him," Harry said.

Carmen picked the phone back up and dialed Marcus's number. It rang three times.

"Yo." She could hear a car engine, road noise, a bass line from his stereo cut short as he turned it down. "What's up?"

"It's Carmen. Harry needs you to come in tonight."

A pause. The engine noise smoothed out — he was coasting, slowing down. "Tonight? I got — it's almost midnight, I can't just —"

"One o'clock." Harry held out his hand. Carmen gave him the phone. "One o'clock, Marcus. Use the loading dock. Carmen will leave it unlocked."

"Harry, come on, man, what's —"

"One o'clock," Harry said, and ended the call.

He sat on the edge of his bed. The mattress groaned under him and his knees cracked — both of them, a sound like someone stepping on dry sticks. He set the phone down. He looked at Agnes. Agnes looked back at him, and something passed between them that Carmen couldn't read, something built over two years of midnight meetings and pill counts and arguments about expiration dates while the building slept around them.

"I will not sort for a reseller," Agnes said. "I told him that in October and I meant it."

"I know," Harry said.

"I will not touch product that ends up at a party in Edison where some twenty-year-old takes three pills because nobody explained opioid naivety to him. That is not what I do."

Harry nodded once. He kept his eyes on the photograph of Jack's truck above the closet instead of on Agnes or Carmen. His right hand flattened the blanket beside him, smoothed the same wrinkle twice, and went still.

"Get the others," Harry said. "Lola and Earl. Not Burt."

---

Lola arrived in the TV room wearing her housecoat and the gold crucifix she never took off, the one that caught the fluorescent light and threw a small bright point against the wall when she moved. Her tremor was bad tonight — her left hand shaking visibly against her thigh, the Parkinson's worsened by whatever Agnes had told her when she woke her up. She sat in the vinyl chair closest to the door, the lookout's chair, but she wasn't watching the hallway. She was watching Harry.

Earl came in behind her, tall and stooped in his bathrobe, clearing his throat the way he always did before speaking — buying time, settling whatever was in his lungs. His cough had been worse this month. Carmen had charted it twice and he'd waved her off both times. He sat down and folded his hands over his knees and didn't ask why they were here.

Carmen had unlocked the loading dock door at 12:40 and waited in the cold, her breath smoking in the parking lot light. October had turned hard this week — the courtyard's three trees had dropped most of their leaves, and the wind came off the highway with a flat, metallic edge. When Marcus's sedan pulled into the far end of the lot, headlights off, she felt something in her chest tip between anger and fear, thin as a coin on its edge.

Marcus came through the loading dock in a hoodie and jeans, moving the way he always moved in the building — quiet, deliberate, the CNA's muscle memory of navigating a sleeping facility. He smelled like the cold outside and something chemical, hand sanitizer or the cleaning spray his car always reeked of. He saw Carmen's face and stopped.

"What?" he said.

"TV room."

She walked him down the hall. He didn't ask questions. When they reached the TV room, Harry was sitting at the card table in his wheelchair — the performance wheelchair, the one he used as a prop, though Carmen noticed he hadn't stood up once since she'd gathered everyone. Maybe his knees were bad tonight. Maybe something else.

Marcus saw the group — Harry at the card table, Agnes standing by the window, Lola in her lookout chair, Earl in the far corner — and stopped with one hand still on the chair back. He knew what this was.

Harry set the prepaid phone on the card table and slid it across to Marcus. The phone scraped on the laminate. Marcus didn't pick it up.

"You sent that to the wrong number," Harry said.

Marcus looked at the phone. Then at Harry. Then at Agnes, who was watching him the way she watched medication interactions — with clinical attention and no sentiment.

"Shit," Marcus said quietly.

"Two hundred oxycodone a week," Harry said. "A hundred alprazolam. Weekly. We have never moved that volume in a month, Marcus. We have never moved half that in a month."

"Harry —"

"You told us you'd walk away from the volume buyer. Three weeks ago, in this room, you sat where you're sitting and you said you'd decline."

Marcus pulled out the chair across from Harry and sat down. He rubbed his face with both hands, the way he did when he was buying time, when the joke he wanted to make wasn't going to land and he knew it. When he dropped his hands, his mouth had flattened into a line.

Carmen had the stupid charting reflex to name what she was seeing — evasive, guarded, not forthcoming — as if documenting it could keep her separate from it. A shared phone, an outside source, her narcotics count under her login: that was the version that ended with her license gone.

"I did decline," Marcus said. "For about a week. Then he came back with better numbers and I thought about it and yeah, I took it."

"Without telling us," Harry said.

"Without telling you. Yeah."
