The radiator knocked in the corner. Out in the hall, the infomercial woman kept listing blades and bonus attachments in a voice too bright for one in the morning.

"Where are you getting two hundred a week?" Agnes asked. "We do not produce that. We have never produced that. What are you promising that you cannot source from us?"

"I've got other sources," Marcus said. "A guy at a pain clinic in Paterson. Another one through — look, it doesn't matter where. The point is I'm not taking more from you than I was before. Your end doesn't change."

"Our end," Harry repeated.

"Your supply. I'm still moving the same amount from Lakeview. The rest is separate."

Paterson meant another source. Carmen felt her stomach pull tight with the old med-room reflex to nail down dates, prescribers, and quantities, as if better documentation could make this a discrepancy instead of a conspiracy.

"The rest is separate," Agnes said. "You are running a distribution operation on top of ours. Using the same phone. The same contacts. The same car you drive here every Tuesday."

"It's a different phone," Marcus said. "Usually. I fucked up. I sent one text to the wrong —"

"One text," Harry said. "One text to the phone that sits in my nightstand. One text that Agnes read. One text that connects your volume operation to our operation to this building to these people." He pointed, a precise gesture, his index finger moving from Agnes to Lola to Earl. "One text."

Marcus leaned back in his chair. The vinyl squeaked. Carmen was standing by the door, her back against the frame, her hand on her badge — she'd been adjusting it without realizing, the nervous fidget she'd never been able to break.

"Okay," Marcus said. "Okay, I hear you. I messed up with the phone. That's on me. But the rest of it — Harry, come on. You knew this was coming."

"I didn't know anything."

"You knew I wasn't gonna just sit there making twelve hundred a month while some dude's offering me five times that. You knew the math didn't work for me the way it works for you."

"The math," Harry said.

"Yeah, the math. Your math. You send money to Jack, a couple hundred a month, and that's — that's fine for you, that's your grandson's truck payment or whatever. Earl sends Denise her four hundred. Agnes has an envelope under her mattress. You're all — you're taken care of, right? Your rooms are paid for, your food shows up, your heat works most of the time." Marcus gestured at the radiator, which chose that moment to click and go silent. "I'm in a basement apartment in North Brunswick. I drive a car with a hundred and sixty thousand miles on it. I don't have a pension, I don't have insurance, I don't have a bed I don't pay rent on."

"You agreed to the terms," Harry said.

"I agreed to terms that made sense when there wasn't a better option. There's a better option now."

Agnes turned from the window. "The better option is distributing opioids to a reseller who will sell them at street price to anyone with cash. No condition screening. No dosage information. No interaction checks."

Carmen hated that Marcus had found the part of her that still did rent math on instinct. Twelve hundred a month was nothing. A buyer taking unlabeled oxycodone from some stranger was not nothing either.

"That's his problem, not mine."

"It is everyone's problem when someone dies," Agnes said. "When a nineteen-year-old takes six oxycodone because he does not know what he bought and his mother finds him in the morning. That is my problem. That is pharmacology, Marcus. That is what I was trained for forty-two years to prevent."

Marcus looked at the ceiling. "Agnes, I respect you, I do, but you're not my pharmacist. You're — we're not a pharmacy. We're selling stolen pills out of a nursing home. That's what this is."

"They are not stolen," Agnes said. "They are prescribed to us and diverted by our consent."

"Okay, fine, diverted. Whatever word makes you feel better about it."

Lola shifted in her chair. She'd been silent the whole time, her hands pressed together in her lap, the tremor visible even from across the room. "Mijo," she said quietly. "Last week when you came in — Tuesday? — you were talking to the girl at the desk, the new one, and you had her laughing, and I was sitting in the hall thinking he doesn't even know I'm watching. He's just happy to be here." The tremor had reached her voice. "And the whole time you were already doing this other thing."

Marcus looked at her. His mouth opened, closed, and then settled.

"Lola, I'm sorry I didn't tell you," he said. "I should've. But you would've told Harry and Harry would've said no and we'd be right here anyway."

Carmen kept looking at Lola's hands because Lola's face was harder to take. The tremor had gotten so bad her thumbnail kept ticking against the plastic chair arm, tiny and fast.

"You don't know that," Lola said. "You didn't even — mijo, you didn't even ask."

"Because I already knew the answer."

Harry put both hands flat on the card table. His fingers were spread, the tendons standing out, an old man's hands — knobbed, liver-spotted, steady. "You're out," he said. "Effective now. You don't take product from this building, you don't visit as Lola's nephew, you don't come through that loading dock again."

Marcus stared at him. Then he laughed — short, hard, no humor in it. "And then what? Who's your distribution? Who's gonna carry the pills out the front door, Harry? Agnes? She's gonna shuffle down to the parking lot in her performance slippers and hand a bag of oxy to Rosa? Earl's gonna wheel the van to Paterson and make drops?"

"We'll figure it out."

"No you won't. Because you can't. Because the whole thing depends on somebody who can walk out of here, and that's me. That's been me for eighteen months. I'm the one in the car. I'm the one meeting people in parking lots with Schedule II narcotics in a backpack. If the cops pull me over, I go to prison. You know what happens to you? You get the newspaper story. You get the — " He waved his hand. "The cute story. Elderly residents outsmart the system. Poor old people, they didn't know what they were doing. You'd get sympathy. A human interest segment. I'd get a cell."

"That's not —" Carmen started.

"It's exactly what would happen and you know it." Marcus turned to her. "You know it, Carmen. You've worked this system. You know what a forty-four-year-old Black guy with a prior distribution charge looks like next to five white-haired grandparents in wheelchairs. Tell me I'm wrong."

Carmen didn't say anything. She couldn't, because he wasn't entirely wrong. The racial calculus of the criminal justice system wasn't something she could argue around, and Marcus knew it.

Marcus leaned forward, elbows on his knees. "I got fired from this place," he said. "I got fired for — I filed a complaint. About staffing. About the fact that there were twelve residents and one aide on the overnight and somebody was gonna fall and get hurt, and I was right, by the way, because Burt fell and got hurt, which is exactly what I said would happen. And they fired me. They called it insubordination and they fired me. I cared about these people and I got punished for it. So I found another way to — I came back and I found a way to still be useful, to still be part of something, and now you're sitting here telling me I can't look out for myself? That I'm supposed to make twelve hundred a month and be grateful because at least I get to be the one who takes all the risk?"

Nobody answered him. Without the infomercial bleeding under the door, the hall gave back every building sound: a toilet tank refilling somewhere down Wing B, a vent shuddering, the slow click and groan of forty years settling into cracked plaster.

Lola had both hands knotted in her lap. Earl hadn't moved from his corner. His hands were still folded over his knees, his expression unreadable.

"Marcus," Lola said. Her thumbs worked against each other, back and forth. "These people you're — the man at the clinic, whoever's on the other end of that phone — do they know you? Not your number. Do they know —" Her hands pressed together hard enough to still them for a second. "Mijo."

"Lola, I love you. I do. But I can't do this for love. Love doesn't pay my rent."

Harry said nothing. He kept both hands flat on the card table. The tendons stood out pale under the fluorescent light.

"We put this together," Harry said. "Agnes and Earl and Lola and me. We put it together because nobody in this building was going to protect us. It was never about the money."

"It was always about the money," Marcus said. "For me it was. You had the luxury of it being about something else."

Earl coughed. The deep, wet rattle that he covered with his fist, the one that had gotten worse every week — a sound like something was loose in his chest and shifting position. He cleared his throat.

"Then you're on your own," Earl said.

Earl lowered his hand and looked at Marcus for the first time that night.

Marcus flinched. It was small — a tightening around his eyes, a shift in his shoulders — but Carmen saw it. Earl saw it too and didn't move.

Marcus sat there for a moment. His hands were flat on his thighs, mirroring Harry's hands on the table. He looked at Lola, who was crying silently, the tears running down her face into the creases around her mouth, her tremor so bad now that the crucifix at her throat jumped against her housecoat. He looked at Agnes, who was watching him without blinking. He looked at Harry.

"Yeah," Marcus said. "Okay." He pushed back from the table and stood up. The chair screeched on the linoleum. "I'll stay away from the building. I won't use the phone again. You want me to — I don't know. You want me to return Lola's cookie tin?"

Nobody answered.

Marcus stood there for another few seconds, jaw working, one hand shoved deep in the hoodie pocket. He looked at Lola once more before he looked away.

"Tell Burt I said hey," he said. Then he walked out.

Carmen listened to his footsteps down the hall — the CNA's quiet walk, instinctive, even now. The loading dock door opened and closed. A minute later, an engine started in the parking lot, and headlights swept across the window and were gone.

Lola made a small sound, something between a word and a breath. Agnes walked to the TV and turned it off — the screen had been dark anyway, the infomercial block over, but she pressed the power button with the precision of someone who needed to turn off one thing she could control.

Earl stood up, coughed into his fist, and left without speaking. His bathrobe belt trailed behind him on the floor.

Lola wiped her face with the back of her hand. "Ay, Dios mio," she whispered, and followed him out, her slippers catching on the threshold.

---

Harry didn't move from the card table. Carmen sat down in the chair Marcus had left, the vinyl still warm. With Marcus gone, the TV room gave back its graveyard-shift noises — the ventilation kicking on, the wall clock above the blank screen, the faint moaning from Room 110 that never fully stopped.

The card table still had the phone on it. Carmen picked it up and set it on the windowsill, face down. When she turned back, Harry had both hands braced on the arms of the wheelchair.

He leaned forward like he was going to stand. His knees cracked. Nothing else moved.

"You need help getting back?" Carmen asked.

"No."

"You don't look fine."

Harry sat back. The cardigan was still buttoned wrong. One cuff had ridden up, showing the papery skin of his wrist and the blue vein under it.

"You knew," Carmen said.

"That he'd take the deal? Yeah. When he agreed too fast in October, I knew."

"Then why let him keep running product?"

"Because we can't leave the building, Carmen. That's the whole problem. That's always been the whole problem."

He gripped the wheel rim once, then let go. His right index finger tapped against the armrest, once, like he was entering a figure on an adding machine.

"I started this because they were cutting our medications," he said.

Carmen let out a breath through her nose. "Keeping your own pills is one thing. This isn't that. There are incident reports. Ombudsman complaints. Family calls. You don't go from a late refill to selling oxy out of a nursing home."

Harry looked at her then, really looked, and whatever answer he had prepared seemed to change shape in his mouth.

"Agnes did the polite version," he said. "She told the nurse. The charge nurse. Cliff. The pharmacy. Everybody in the chain got their chance."

He shifted in the chair and his mouth tightened. "They switched her hydrocodone to a generic she'd never been prescribed. Different manufacturer, different fillers, different absorption rate. She said it wasn't hitting right. They sent the same blister pack again. Two weeks she lay there with the inside of her cheek chewed raw because turning onto her bad side lit her up and she wasn't going to ring for a nurse who'd tell her it was the same drug."

Room 110's moaning cut off mid-drag. The stop made Carmen notice the radiator hiss and the dry ache in her jaw from how long she'd been clenching it.

"My oxycodone got delayed," Harry said. "Nine days. By day six I was using the plastic urinal because standing up to get to the toilet sent sparks through my hip. I slept twenty minutes at a time. They charted me as restless."

Carmen looked at his hands on the chair arms. She'd written that word before. Restless. Like it meant anything.

"Lola's Parkinson's prescription showed up three weeks late and the dose was wrong when it came. Coffee down the front of her gown every morning. Hands shaking so hard she couldn't get a fork to her mouth, and day shift wrote increased confusion because that's the box they had."

Harry's hands stayed on the chair arms. The skin over his knuckles tightened and went pale.

"So we started keeping our pills. Not selling them. Keeping them. Agnes set up a system. She inventoried what we had, checked interactions, tracked expiration dates. We did the job they were billing for."

"That explains the stash," Carmen said. "It doesn't explain Marcus in parking lots."

"That came six months later. Marcus was already fired by then. He came to visit Lola, and Lola told him Agnes had noticed the new pharmacy was auto-filling scripts that should've been reviewed. We had surplus. Marcus knew people who needed it. That's when it turned into business."

He stopped. The wall clock kept ticking. When the ventilation cycled off, Carmen could hear the call-light panel buzzing faintly at the station and thought of Devon asleep under his earbuds, half the wing depending on people who weren't awake.

Carmen thought about the parking lot outside — dark at five o'clock now, the overhead lights buzzing the sickly orange they always buzzed, the leaves from the courtyard trees scattered across the asphalt in wet brown clumps. She thought about Marcus driving away from Lakeview for the last time, or maybe not the last time, his headlights disappearing down Route 27 toward North Brunswick and his basement apartment and whatever deal he'd made with whoever was on the other end of that text.

"He's going to get caught," she said. "Moving that kind of volume."

Harry didn't respond to that. He was rubbing his hip now, absently, like he'd forgotten his hand was there.

"What do we do now?" Carmen asked.

Harry lifted his head. His eyes held on hers without drifting.

"We wait," he said. "Marcus will either come back or he won't. And if he doesn't, we figure out what we are without him."

"And if he gets caught?"

"Then we've got a different problem."

A call bell started ringing at the far end of the hall, thin and stubborn. Carmen stood before she knew she'd decided to.

"Go do your rounds," Harry said. "Devon's gonna wake up eventually."

She looked at the card table where Marcus had been sitting, the chair still pulled out at an angle. She pushed it back under the table. The scrape of the legs on the linoleum was loud in the empty room.

"Harry."

"Yeah."

"I'm sorry."

He looked at her. Then he looked at the dark TV screen, which reflected the room back at them — two people in a nursing home common area at two-thirty in the morning, one in scrubs and one in a wheelchair, the overhead fluorescents washing both of them the same flat institutional white.

"Go do your rounds, Carmen," he said.

She went. The bell kept needling from the far end of the hall, and somebody in 110 had started moaning again. At the nurse's station, Devon was still asleep, the hunting podcast finished, his phone screen dark. Carmen shook his shoulder once with more force than she usually used, pulled the clipboard she'd abandoned at eleven back under her arm, and went to answer the bell before it rolled into a second alarm.


# Chapter 17

Ten days later, Carmen was in the medication room doing the evening count when she heard it. At first it wasn't the words, just the cadence. Every nurse learned the difference between the TV room's daytime murmur and the local news anchor's emergency tone, the slight tightening in the broadcaster's delivery that meant something had happened nearby, something with a suspect, something with a mugshot.

She set down the clipboard and walked to the TV room.

It was mostly empty at 8:40 PM. Dinner was over, the last visitors had signed out at eight, and most of the Wing A residents were back in their rooms. Burt was dozing in the recliner nearest the screen, his mouth open, one hand curled around the armrest as if he were holding on to something in a dream. The volume was up too loud because nobody had adjusted it after Mrs. Petroski went back to her room, and Mrs. Petroski was functionally deaf.

The anchor described an arrest in Edison for prescription drug distribution. A man named DeShaun Briley, thirty-seven, had been picked up during a traffic stop with approximately four hundred tablets of oxycodone, eighty alprazolam, and an undetermined quantity of hydrocodone in the trunk of his vehicle. He was facing possession with intent to distribute and both state and federal charges. Bond had been set at seventy-five thousand.

Carmen didn't recognize the name. She didn't recognize the face in the mugshot, a heavy young man, scared-looking under the flat police lighting, his left eye slightly swollen as if he'd been rubbing it or hadn't slept. But she knew that number. Marcus's new buyer had been taking orders that size.

Her hand went to her badge and stayed there. The news anchor moved on to a car accident on Route 18.

She stood in the doorway another thirty seconds, watching the screen without seeing it, while her mind ran the chain from Briley to Marcus, from Marcus to Lakeview, from Lakeview to the count discrepancies documented under her name, her license number, her signature on every shift-end log for the past seven months. If Briley had Marcus's phone number in his contacts, if he had a text, a single text, with Marcus's name or a location or a time, investigators would pull Marcus's records. And Marcus had visited this building every week for the last fourteen months, signing in at the front desk as Lola's nephew, his name in a visitor log that no one cross-referenced because no one at Lakeview cross-referenced anything. The ring went back further than Marcus's visits. The paperwork did not.

Carmen turned off the TV room light on her way out. Burt didn't stir.

She went to Harry's room. He was sitting up in bed reading the Trenton Times, his faded blue cardigan buttoned wrong, the top button through the second hole, his reading glasses taped at the left temple where the arm had broken six weeks ago. He looked up when Carmen knocked on the open door, and his face told her he already knew.

"You saw it," she said.

"I saw it." He folded the paper and placed it in his lap. "Edison. That's twenty minutes from here."

"It's the volume buyer. The quantities match."

Harry took off his glasses. Without them, his eyes were sharper. The lenses were for reading, but they also softened his face, made it rounder, made him look more like the befuddled old man he played during daylight hours. "I've been trying Marcus's phone for an hour. His personal one. It goes straight to voicemail."

"Since when do you have Marcus's personal number?"

Harry gave her the flat look he reserved for obvious arithmetic. He didn't answer.

"When's the last time he came in?" Carmen asked.

"Ten days ago. He hasn't been here since the meeting." That was what they called it, the night Marcus had been confronted, argued back, and been cut off by Earl in a single sentence that ended more than the conversation. "Sit down, Carmen."

She didn't sit down. She kept one hand on the doorframe, nails pressing into the paint. "If they trace Briley to Marcus, any connection, a phone record, a text, anything, they follow Marcus here. They pull the visitor log and they see his name every week for fourteen months. Then they start asking why a supposed nephew was here that often. They look at the medication records and they see my count discrepancies, documented and signed. Every one of them is mine."

"Sit down."

"I can't sit down right now."

Harry put his glasses on the nightstand and lined them up parallel to the edge, beside a pen and a folded sheet of paper. When he nudged the half-open drawer shut with two fingers, something small and black clicked once against the wood inside. Carmen's eyes went there and away.

"We clean house," he said.

"Everything?"

"Everything we can touch: pills, Agnes's notebooks, the phone. The visitor log and your old count sheets stay where they are."

Carmen leaned against the doorframe. The hallway behind her smelled the way it always did at night, disinfectant and warm skin, the fading trace of dinner's meatloaf, hand sanitizer from the dispensers every fifteen feet, all of it familiar. She'd worked in this hallway for thirteen months and stopped noticing any of it, the same way she'd stopped noticing her own apartment most days. Now each layer came back sharp and separate because she might be leaving all of it under circumstances she didn't choose.

"I'll tell Agnes," she said.

"Tell her about the notebooks."

"I know."

She found Agnes at 9:15. Agnes was still awake. She rarely slept before midnight, a habit from four decades of pharmacy shifts that ended at ten. Her roommate, Doris, was asleep and snoring wetly in the bed nearest the door, the sound of a woman whose body worked hard just to breathe. Agnes sat upright in the far bed with a crossword puzzle book open on her lap. She hadn't filled in a single square.

"I heard it," Agnes said before Carmen could speak. "The volume on the TV was up. Mrs. Petroski's hearing."

"Harry wants everything gone tonight."

Agnes closed the crossword book. Her hands were steady. The left-side weakness from her stroke showed in her gait when she walked, a slight drag in the foot, but her grip had come back fully within the first year. She placed the book on the nightstand with the same deliberate precision she placed everything. She had pharmacist's hands; everything had its location. "My notebooks."

"Yeah."

"There are four composition books with black-and-white marbled covers." Agnes's voice was clinical, but the words came faster than usual, closer together, compressed under pressure. "They're under my mattress, under the fitted sheet, between the mattress and the mattress pad. They're not under the bed frame."

Carmen looked at her. Agnes's face was what it always was, precise, impatient, stripped clean. But her hands had moved from the crossword book to the mattress edge and were pressing down, her fingertips whitening against the hospital-grade fitted sheet.

"I'll come get them after Devon falls asleep," Carmen said. "Eleven-thirty."

"I'll have them out."

---

Devon was asleep by 11:45. Carmen checked twice, at 11:30 when he was still scrolling his phone with the audio off, and again fifteen minutes later when the phone was face-down on his chest and his breathing had gone slow and even. He'd picked up the overnight because the regular night aide had called out, and Devon worked overnights the way he worked every shift, minimally, resentfully, one earbud in, his attention dimmed to whatever frequency let him avoid getting fired for a third time. He was twenty-three. She thought he'd keep sleeping.

Carmen went to Agnes's room first.

Agnes was standing by her bed in a housecoat that had belonged to someone else. The facility distributed donated clothing to residents whose families didn't bring enough, and Agnes's daughter Helen sent the same three outfits in rotation, all a size too large because Helen still pictured the woman she'd been during stroke recovery, fifteen pounds lighter and unable to say the word "prescription" without it coming out wrong. The housecoat was a faded floral print with a zipper up the front. Agnes held the notebooks out in both hands, palms flat under them so the spines wouldn't bend.

They were the black-and-white marbled composition books sold at every CVS and Walgreens in New Jersey for two dollars and forty-nine cents, the same kind Carmen had bought for Reggie's school supplies in September.

She took them. They were heavier than they looked, filled cover to cover with no blank pages. The top one fell open in her arms and she saw Agnes's handwriting, small, square, pharmacist's print that could have been typed. Inside were columns of drug names with dosages, buyer codes instead of names, just two-letter abbreviations, and expiration dates noted in red ink. In the margins Agnes had written: *Do not combine w/ warfarin. Check renal function. Ro — skip if on new antibiotic, interaction risk. Jn — monitor for signs of tolerance escalation (none observed).* One page held a hand-drawn table of pill counts going back fourteen months, back to when Marcus had started coming weekly and the business needed a paper brain instead of Harry's head and Agnes's memory, each row dated, each column a different controlled substance, every number precise to the individual tablet.

Eighteen months of pharmaceutical judgment sat inside those fourteen months of notebooks. The ring had started before the recordkeeping did, back when it was just Harry and Agnes making sure people got what Lakeview and the pharmacy switch had failed to provide. These books were the later version, the bigger one, the one that could put all of them in prison. They were also the most careful, most specific, most conscientious pharmaceutical records in the building, better than what Carmen charted, better than what sat in the chart under Dr. Prasad's orders, better than anything the state survey had reviewed.

"I'll burn them behind the loading dock," Carmen said.

Agnes looked at the notebooks in Carmen's arms. Her mouth had gone flat, and for once her hands weren't sorting or straightening anything. She didn't say anything.

"Agnes."

"Go," Agnes said. Her voice was flat and fast.

Carmen went.

She started with the pills. Harry had the remaining stockpile in two shoeboxes in the back of his closet, behind a row of Reader's Digest condensed books that had come with the room. The shoeboxes were the part they could still erase, hidden stock that tied the signed discrepancies in the med binder to a real operation. She retrieved them while Harry sat in his wheelchair by the window, watching the dark parking lot.

"Thirty-six oxycodone," he said without turning around. "Fourteen alprazolam. Twenty-one tramadol. Some non-controlled."

"It doesn't matter anymore, Harry."

"No." He turned the wheelchair around. His hands were on the wheels, and the motion was smooth but slow. Carmen couldn't tell if the slowness was exhaustion or age or the performance settling back into his body. "I suppose it doesn't."

She carried the shoeboxes to the handicapped bathroom at the end of Wing A and locked the door. The first batches went down cleanly. Oxycodone, then alprazolam, then tramadol, then the loose metformin and lisinopril and rosuvastatin in their separate plastic baggies, each flush spaced a little because Harry was right about the pipes and the pipes were older than everybody left on this hall.

The fourth flush didn't take.

The bowl filled instead of emptied. Blue water climbed toward the rim, cloudy with chalk and half-softened tablets that knocked lightly against the porcelain. Carmen stared one half second, then dropped to her knees and reached in with gloved hands because if that water hit the tile the whole wing would smell medicinal by morning. The pipes hammered in the wall hard enough to shake the grab bar. Out in the hall, a chair scraped.

She froze. Devon.

A knuckle tapped twice on the door. Lola's whisper came through the wood, low and quick. "Mija. He's awake."

"Shit."

Carmen scooped what she could into a stainless emesis basin, yanked the remaining baggies back into the shoeboxes, unlocked the door, and stepped into the hall. Lola stood at the junction with her mug braced in both hands, her tremor sending little rings across the coffee. Earl was behind her, one hand on the wall, already breathing through his mouth.

"The toilet backed up," Carmen said. "I can't flush the rest."

Earl looked at the shoeboxes, then at her face. "What do you need?"

"The linen cart. Park it here. If Devon comes down this hall, tell him Burt shit through everything and I need supplies."

Earl nodded once and went, moving faster than Carmen had seen him move in months. The speed cost him immediately. By the time he reached the clean-linen alcove he had to brace himself against the cart handle, chest hitching hard enough that he stood there a second before shoving the cart down the hall anyway.

Lola tightened her grip on the mug. "He asked what the noise was."

"Keep him talking. Five minutes."

"About what?"

"Anything."

Lola gave a small, humorless breath that might once have been a laugh. "That's the part I know how to do."

Carmen abandoned the bathroom. She left Earl's linen cart crosswise in front of the door and shoved the shoeboxes into the soiled utility room, a narrow sour-smelling closet with the hopper sink bolted low into the floor. The hopper was built for bedpans, vomit basins, wash water streaked brown or pink or whatever color a human body turned the world once it stopped obeying. It could take this.

She dumped the tablets into a stainless basin, ran hot water over them, splashed in bleach from the housekeeping shelf, and worked the mess with a tongue depressor until the pills turned to gritty paste. The smell hit fast, pharmacy dust under public-pool chlorine, something chemical and wrong that clawed the back of her throat. She sluiced the sludge into the hopper in three loads, ran more water, and watched the gray-white paste vanish. The blister packs and torn baggies went into a red trash sack under two used incontinent briefs from the morning-care bin because this building generated so much shame and waste every day that one more bag disappearing into the dumpster wouldn't make a dent.

Someone said, from the hall, "Jesus Christ, what died?"

It was Devon.

Carmen stepped out of the utility room with bleach on her gloves and the red sack twisted shut in one hand. Devon was halfway up the corridor in wrinkled scrubs, earbud still in one ear, his hair flattened on one side from the desk. Earl stood between him and the blocked bathroom, one hand on the linen cart, shoulders still high from the run down the hall. Lola hovered at the junction with the coffee mug tight to her chest.

"Mr. Kovacs had an accident," Carmen said before Devon could ask again. She didn't rush it. She used her work voice, competent, tired, slightly annoyed. "Bathroom backed up while I was cleaning him. I need another pack of wipes from central and a clean fitted sheet for 116A."

Devon looked past her toward the cart, toward Earl, toward the closed bathroom door. His face pinched the way it always did around bodily labor, disgust first, then laziness, then the fast mental math of whether someone else could be made to do it.

"Burt did that?" he said.

"You want to come smell him and find out?"

He recoiled. "No."

"Then get the wipes."

He swore under his breath and shuffled toward the supply room. Carmen waited until he disappeared around the corner before she let herself breathe all the way out. Earl took one hand off the cart and pressed a folded handkerchief to his mouth. The cough hit him again, heavier this time, and he bent into it so hard the cart rattled. When it passed, he folded the handkerchief back up without looking first. Carmen saw the deliberate not-looking and felt something cold move through her gut.

"Earl."

"I'm fine," he said. He said it once, flat and finished.

"No, you are not."

He put the handkerchief away. "Five minutes, you said."

Carmen took the red sack to the back door, dumped it under kitchen trash and broken-down boxes in the dumpster, and came back inside. Lola was still at the junction, still holding the mug, though the coffee had long since gone cold.

"How much longer?" Lola asked.

"Almost done. Keep him away from the loading dock."

Lola nodded. Her crucifix clicked against the mug. "He thinks Burt shit himself."

"Good."

Carmen retrieved the notebooks from the supply-closet shelf and went out the back. The loading dock sat in the spill of a single yellow bulb, all concrete, rusting ramp, dumpster, chain-link fence, and dead grass, invisible from the front.

She dragged a metal trash can to the center of the dock, tore pages from the first notebook, and lit them with a match from the break room. The smoke went white and thin and smelled like school paper.

She was midway through the second notebook when Agnes appeared at the back door.

Carmen hadn't heard it open. Agnes stood at the edge of the loading dock in her donated housecoat and slippers, her arms at her sides, the cold November air moving through the thin fabric. Firelight caught her glasses and made her eyes disappear behind two small rectangles of reflected orange.

Carmen stopped tearing pages. "You should be inside. It's cold."

Agnes didn't move. She watched the fire. The pages curled, blackened, fell apart, fourteen months of handwriting, and behind that the four unrecorded months before the notebooks existed, when she and Harry had been carrying the whole thing in their heads, catalogues of medications and dosage calculations and interaction flags, the whole pharmaceutical architecture of the operation resisting for a second before it gave.

"I started those notebooks four months after we started," Agnes said. Her voice was faster than usual, the words closer together, more data per sentence. Her hands were at her sides and not sorting, not organizing, not giving themselves a job. "Harry had his arithmetic. I had mine. That was enough until Marcus began coming every week and enough stopped being enough. I put fourteen months of inventory on paper. It has been eighteen months since the first time I corrected a dangerous interaction in this building and understood nobody else was going to keep these people alive." She looked at the burning pages, not at Carmen. "Every tablet was accounted for. Every interaction was flagged. Every expiration date was tracked to the day. I maintained buyer profiles for Rosa's lupus schedule, Derek's insulin timing and when the metformin had to coordinate, and Janine's tolerance levels. I was monitoring for signs of escalating use, which I did not observe."

Carmen fed another fistful of pages into the can. The draft pulled the flame up hard enough to sting her knuckles.

"Nobody asked me to keep those records," Agnes said. "I kept them because that is what a pharmacist does."

The marbled cover caught at one corner, burned slow, then gave way all at once. Carmen could hear the faint crackle of glue in the binding.

"This is the only professional work I have done in three years," Agnes said.

Carmen looked at her. Agnes did not fold her arms against the cold or step back from the smoke. The toes of her slippers darkened against the damp concrete, and she kept watching the pages cave in.

Carmen went back to burning. By twenty past one, all four notebooks were ash. Agnes stayed there until the covers blackened, curled, and dropped into gray powder at the bottom of the can. Then she turned and went back inside without saying goodnight.

Earl appeared in the hallway as Carmen came through the back door, carrying Lola's cookie tin.

"Lola said to get rid of it," he said. He was leaning against the wall again, one hand braced on the linen-closet frame. His breathing was shallow and wheezy.

"I'll take it." She held out her hand and he gave her the tin.

"Devon's back at the desk."

"I know."

She dumped the tin in the dumpster, came back in, and found the prepaid phone in Harry's room where he'd left it under the Reader's Digest books. She deleted the seventeen texts in the medication room instead of outside because Devon was awake now and moving around. Then she carried the cheap flip phone to the loading dock, crushed it under her heel, snapped the SIM card in half, and threw both into the dumpster.

When she came back inside, her hands smelled like smoke and hot plastic and bleach. The orange antibacterial soap only rearranged the smell. She washed twice anyway.

---

By four in the morning, it was done, except for the handicapped bathroom still blocked off by a linen cart and a handwritten OUT OF ORDER note Carmen had peeled off a housekeeping clipboard.

She sat in the TV room with the overhead light off and the television dark. The room was lit only by the hallway fluorescents coming through the open door, a blue-white half-light that made the vinyl chairs look colorless and the walls look like they were receding. She could hear the building's heating system and Burt's snoring carrying down the hall from 116A.

Harry wheeled himself in at 4:15. He'd changed into his blue cardigan, the good one, the one he wore to every ring meeting. He parked by the card table where Agnes used to spread out her notebooks and the ziplock bags and the envelopes of cash. The table was bare except for an old coffee ring, and Harry looked at it before he looked at Carmen.

"Done?" he said.

"As done as it's getting," she said. "The bathroom backed up. I had to move the rest to the hopper. Devon woke up. Lola stalled him and Earl covered for me."

Harry's face changed very little, but it changed. "Did he see anything?"

"He saw enough to know something smelled bad and I was still cleaning at one in the morning."

Harry nodded once.

"This is over," Carmen said. "No more pills. No more buyers. No more pretending this was under control."

He looked at the empty table. "It was good while it lasted."

"No, it wasn't." The answer came out before she thought about it. "It was necessary. That's different."

Harry rubbed his thumb once over the seam of his wheelchair armrest. "Sometimes it isn't."

She laughed once through her nose, no humor in it. "Ask Agnes if that felt good. Ask Earl."

He didn't answer. Real Harry's silences were choices, not absences.

Carmen leaned forward, elbows on her knees. "Listen to me. In the morning I'm putting Earl in for a chest X-ray. Not next week. Not when he feels like it. Morning. And Agnes is getting a weight check and nutrition note because she's going to stop eating and act like it's not happening. If either one of them gives me trouble, you do not talk them out of it. You do not tell them to wait. You do not run a side operation out of the damage from this one."

Harry looked at her then, directly. "You're setting terms."

"I'm doing my job."

"You were doing your job before."

"No," Carmen said. "I was doing yours too."

The words sat there. She hadn't meant to say them that cleanly, and once they were out she couldn't soften them without lying.

Harry lowered his eyes to his hands. The skin over his knuckles looked almost translucent in the hallway light. "If Tammy asks questions?"

"I tell her the bathroom backed up and Burt shit himself and I was here cleaning it up. That's the lie available. I'm not building you a better one."

He gave the smallest nod. Agreement, or maybe just comprehension.

They sat a minute longer. The building settled around them, heating system cycling, a mattress squeaking somewhere down the hall, the small mechanical sounds of a facility built in the late seventies and maintained only enough to pass inspection. Through the window the parking lot was dark and empty. Beyond it, the courtyard's three trees stood bare against the brown suburban sky.

"What are you going to do?" Harry asked.

"Go home. Sleep a little. Drop off Reggie's lunch. Come back."

"That's not what I mean."

She knew what he meant. Leave, maybe. Apply out. Save herself while there was still a version of that available. She didn't answer. She tightened her ponytail with both hands and looked at the card table. Beside the coffee ring the laminate held a few neat dents where Agnes had pressed hard enough with her pen to mark the surface through the pages. Carmen set her thumb over them, then pulled her hand back.

"I'm going to check on everyone," she said, and stood.

"Carmen."

She stopped at the doorway.

Harry was looking at the bare table again, not at her. "Thank you," he said.

She didn't answer that either. She left.

Agnes was in bed, awake, staring at the ceiling with her glasses still on. The ceiling was acoustic tile, water-stained in one corner from a leak maintenance had patched but not painted. Doris's snoring was the only sound. Agnes's housecoat hung over the chair by the window, one sleeve dusted with gray ash. Carmen stood in the doorway a second, then moved on.

Lola's room. Lola was asleep, curled on her right side, the rosary wound through her left hand. Her right hand rested on the pillow near her face, and even in sleep the tremor was there, a fine vibration in her fingers that moved the beads against the cotton pillowcase with a faint clicking sound, something small and persistent ticking down.

Earl and Burt in 116A. Burt was asleep, had been asleep through everything, his decline so steady now that the ring ending meant nothing to a man who could no longer remember what the ring was. He lay on his back with his mouth open, his wispy white hair pressed flat against the pillow, his long musician's hands slack on the blanket. Earl was in the other bed with his eyes closed, but his breathing was wrong, too controlled, too careful, the breathing of a man pretending to sleep so nobody would ask how he was feeling. Carmen stood in the doorway and listened to that breathing and thought about the chest X-ray she was going to put in whether he liked it or not.

She went back to the nurse's station and did her charting. Overnight rounds: checked on Mr. Kovacs at eleven, one, and three. Checked on Mr. Washington, Mrs. Fernandez, Mrs. Chen. Medication count documented as correct for the cart and cabinet at end of shift. There was now also a maintenance slip for the blocked bathroom and a brief respiratory note on Mr. Washington that she had not run by Harry and did not intend to. The discrepancy reports from earlier shifts were still clipped in the binder under her name. What was gone was the off-book evidence, the shoeboxes in Harry's closet, Agnes's notebooks, Marcus's texts. She signed the log with the same signature she signed everything, C. Reyes, LPN, and put the clipboard back on its hook.

The front door beeped at 5:48 AM.

Tammy came around the corner in scrub pants and a winter coat, travel mug in one hand. She set the mug on the desk, hung her coat in the locker-room nook, clipped on her badge, and stopped when she saw Carmen.

"You're still here."

"Picked up the overnight." The excuse came out too easily. "Devon needed coverage and I was already—"

She stopped.

"I stayed," she said.

Tammy looked at the loose hair, the red around Carmen's eyes, the bleach stain on one scrub cuff she hadn't noticed, the smoke still caught in her scrubs. Then her gaze shifted to the note on the housekeeping clipboard, to the linen cart still blocking the bathroom down the hall, back to Carmen.

"What happened to Wing A?" Tammy asked.

"Bathroom backed up. Burt had an accident. It turned into a whole thing."

Tammy kept looking at her. Not skeptical exactly. Measuring.

"You smell like a bonfire," Tammy said.

Carmen lifted one shoulder. "Dumpster fire out back. Kitchen trash."

Tammy took that in, or pretended to. Then she picked up her mug again. "Right."

It wasn't agreement. It wasn't forgiveness either. It was the kind of word people used when they had decided not to help you tell the story cleaner than it was.

Tammy put her bag in her locker. She started her rounds.

Carmen gathered her things. She signed out on the overnight log. She put on her coat, the same black puffy Old Navy jacket she'd worn last November, zipper still broken, and slung her bag over her shoulder.

She passed Harry's room.

The door was mostly closed. Lakeview policy required doors to remain openable from the hall, not shut fully, but Harry had pulled it to within an inch. Through the gap, in the strip of visible room, Carmen saw him sitting in his wheelchair by the nightstand. He had something in his hand.

It wasn't a new object after all. It was the same black hinge she'd glimpsed in the nightstand drawer earlier, the thing that had clicked once against the wood when he told her they needed to clean house. It was a phone, not the prepaid, which was in pieces in the dumpster behind the building, but a different one, small and black, a flip phone of the same cheap kind. He was holding it to his ear.

He was talking. His voice was low and Carmen couldn't hear the words from the hallway, just the rhythm, the cadence of short sentences delivered with the controlled precision of his real register. It wasn't the performance, the wandering mumble about Eleanor, what day it was, and who these people were. It was the real Harry, at ten minutes to six in the morning, calling someone on a phone he'd kept out of the cleanup, saying things she couldn't hear to a person she couldn't identify.

She stood in the hallway for three seconds. She could have pushed the door open. She could have walked in and asked who he was talking to and why there was another phone and whether this call was a contingency, a warning, an apology, or the first move in starting the whole thing again.

She didn't.

She kept moving. At the front door she pushed out into the morning. The parking lot was dark and cold and the wind came across it flat and unbroken. The courtyard's bare trees did nothing, and the building didn't block it from this angle. Carmen walked to her car with her coat open because the zipper was broken and her hands in her pockets because they were cold and still smelled like smoke despite the soap, despite the bleach, despite the washing, despite the fact that the notebooks and the pills and the phone were all gone now and there was nothing left to smell like except the memory of having done it.

She got in the car and sat with her hands on the steering wheel and the engine off. The dashboard clock said 5:53. In an hour and seven minutes she needed to be at her mother's house to drop off Reggie's lunch, and before that she needed enough sleep to get through the next set of meds without making a mistake that lived on paper.

She started the car. She drove home.


# Chapter 18

A week after her last overnight, the day shift was louder than she remembered.

Carmen clocked in at seven and the hallway was already full — therapists wheeling equipment carts, dietary pushing breakfast trays, a family member arguing with the front desk about a missing sweater. Fluorescent lights at full power, no half-light mercy. The disinfectant smell was the same, but daylight made the building feel bigger and more crowded.

She hadn't worked a seven-to-three in over four months. The switch was supposed to ease the transition — the New Brunswick interview was next Thursday, and if the hospital wanted her, she'd be working days permanently. The pay would be better. The benefits would actually cover Reggie's dental. A forty-minute commute instead of twenty-five, but the math still worked.

She'd submitted the application two weeks ago, sitting in her car after dropping Reggie at school, typing on her phone with her thumbs. She'd written the cover letter in twelve minutes. It was the easiest professional document she'd ever produced because she didn't have to lie in it.

The med pass took longer in the morning. More residents awake, more questions, more family members hovering to make sure Mom or Dad got the right pills. Carmen worked her way down Wing A with the cart, popping blister packs, pouring water cups, charting each administration on her tablet. The routine was the same. Her hands knew it. Her body knew how to lean into the doorframe to steer the cart around the corner by Room 108.

Agnes was sitting up in bed. Her roommate, Doris, was snoring in the next bed with her mouth open, a thread of saliva connecting her lower lip to the pillow. Agnes had her wire-rimmed glasses on and was staring at the covered bridge print on the opposite wall.

"Morning, Mrs. Chen."

Agnes turned her head slowly. There was no blank act in it, only drag.

"Your morning medications." Carmen held out the cup. Lisinopril, rosuvastatin, the calcium supplement, a daily aspirin.

Agnes took the cup. She put the pills in her mouth, drank the water, and opened her mouth for Carmen to check.

"Did you eat breakfast?"

The scrambled eggs had been pushed across the plate. The toast was torn into pieces and left there. The juice cup was empty.

"I had the juice," Agnes said.

"Mrs. Chen, you need to eat. Dr. Prasad's doing rounds today, and she's going to ask me about your intake."

Agnes looked at her.

"The eggs are cold," Agnes said.

Carmen marked the tray as less than twenty-five percent consumed and moved on.

At Room 112, Lola was in her wheelchair by the window instead of parked in the hall. She took her medications without the wink, said no when Carmen offered to roll her out by the nurses' station, and asked at the door, "Is he — how's he doing?" Then she turned back to the bare courtyard with one hand around her crucifix.

In 116, Burt slept through the med pass with his mouth open and a damp half-moon darkening the pillowcase under his cheek. Earl sat beside him in khaki pants and a flannel shirt buttoned to the collar, reading a paperback western in the open. He wasn't hiding it inside a magazine now. His handkerchief was folded on the armrest.

"Good morning, Mr. Washington."

Earl looked up, nodded, took his medications from the cup, swallowed them with water, and handed the cup back.

Yesterday he'd let the transport aide wheel him to radiology, line the plate up behind his back, and tell him when to hold his breath. Earl cooperating felt worse than the cough.

The Hungarian-English dictionary was back on Burt's shelf, emptied out and ordinary again. The ring had ended around him, and he had slept through that too.

Carmen finished Wing A and parked the med cart at the station. She charted the last three administrations and initialed the controlled substance log. The count was correct. It had been correct for three weeks. Every shift, the numbers matched. No discrepancies, no documentation notes, no count that needed a second nurse to verify. The log was clean, cleaner than it should've been.

She opened the narc drawer again before lunch just because her body told her to. The hydrocodone was there. The alprazolam was there. The tramadol was there. Every blister card was full where it should be, every count line blank except for signatures. For three weeks she'd wanted the log clean. For three weeks the neatness had looked like a bed made too flat after somebody died in it.

She poured herself coffee from the staff pot and stood at the station while the morning moved around her. An aide pushed Mr. Hartwell past in his wheelchair, heading for speech therapy. A family member carried a vase of grocery-store chrysanthemums toward Room 104. Tammy came around the corner with a stack of linens and stopped.

"You're on days."

"Yeah. Switched last week."

"Huh." Tammy set the linens on the counter and started sorting them. She had the economy of motion of someone who'd been folding institutional linens for fifteen years — each pillowcase quartered and stacked in the same number of movements, every time. "Weird seeing you in daylight."

"Weird being here in daylight."

Tammy looked at her for a beat. She'd been paying closer attention for weeks now. Tammy noticed things. She always had. She'd noticed Carmen's changed behavior, her late nights, her proximity to certain residents. Carmen didn't know how much Tammy had put together, and she'd decided to stop trying to figure it out. Whatever Tammy knew or suspected, she hadn't acted on it, and Carmen wasn't going to be here long enough for it to matter.

"Mrs. Chen's not eating again," Carmen said, because it was true and because it was something to say that lived in the safe territory of the job.

"Yeah, I know. She picks at it. She drank two Ensures yesterday, though."

"That's something."

"Barely." Tammy squared the last pillowcase on the stack. "Dr. Prasad's coming at ten. I told dietary to put Agnes on a supplement tray. Whether she touches it." She shrugged.

Dr. Prasad arrived at ten-fifteen, moving fast, her white coat unbuttoned because she'd just come from another facility and would be at a third one by two. She was small and sharp and saw forty patients a day across three buildings. She relied on the nurses' assessments more than she should have had to, because there wasn't enough of her to go around, and she knew it, and she did the best she could with the hours she had. Carmen respected her for that.

They did rounds together. Dr. Prasad listened to Doris's lungs, adjusted Mr. Hartwell's metformin, reviewed the PT notes for a new admission. At Room 108, she looked at Agnes's tray.

"How long has she been like this?"

"Two weeks, maybe a little more. She's taking fluids. The Ensures sometimes."

"Weight?"

"Down four pounds since last month."

Dr. Prasad wrote something on her tablet. "Let's add a nutritional consult. And I want labs — CBC, metabolic panel, albumin. If she's not eating in a week, we'll talk about appetite stimulants." She looked at Carmen over the tablet. "Any behavioral changes? More withdrawn?"

"She's quieter."

"I'll order the labs," Dr. Prasad said, and moved on.

At Room 116, she listened to Earl's chest for a long time. Longer than she listened to most chests. She moved the stethoscope, asked him to breathe deeply, asked him to cough. Earl coughed. The sound filled the room — deep, liquid, wrong. Dr. Prasad listened again before she took the earpieces out.

"Mr. Washington, your X-ray results should be back by tomorrow. I want to review those before we talk next steps."

Earl nodded. He didn't ask what the results might show.

"Thank you, Mr. Washington," Dr. Prasad said.

"Yeah," Earl said.

Carmen stood in the doorway while Dr. Prasad made notes. Burt slept on. The room smelled like menthol cream and flannel and the faint sour edge of illness coming off Earl's skin.

At eleven-thirty dietary sent up Agnes's supplement tray: vanilla Ensure, cottage cheese, canned pears gone glossy with syrup, two packets of saltines still sealed in plastic. Carmen carried it in herself. Doris was awake now, asking if lunch was late.

"Mrs. Chen."

Agnes turned her head, slow and tired.

"Dr. Prasad wants you trying the supplement."

Carmen peeled back the foil and put the chilled bottle in Agnes's hand. Agnes smelled the vanilla, set it on the bedside table, and went back to looking at the bridge print.

"You need the calories."

"Put it there," Agnes said.

On the overbed table there was nothing now except the tray, Agnes's water cup, and the shallow dent in the fake wood where the composition notebooks had sat. Carmen stood there longer than she needed to. When she came back forty minutes later, condensation had ringed the table and Doris had eaten the pears.

The dayroom was usually loud by noon — game-show applause, bingo chatter, somebody hollering that the caller skipped B-12. Today the television was on mute and the activities aide was setting out cards to half a room. Two short-term rehab patients sat with their blankets over their knees. Mr. Hartwell was there, staring at the prize basket. Wing A's usual core was missing. Lola wasn't in her window spot. Harry wasn't there asking a question he already knew the answer to. Earl wasn't parked off to the side with his paperback shut on his lap.

"Quiet crowd," the aide said when Carmen passed.

"Yeah."

The aide held up the paper sack of prizes she'd been unpacking — lip balm, crossword books, a packet of sugar-free candy, a travel bottle of lotion. "I got these for your people and nobody wants them."

Carmen looked back down the hall toward 112, 114A, 116. Earl coughed somewhere out of sight.

"They're tired," she said.

The aide snorted. "Everybody in here's tired."

---

She found Harry at two o'clock, in his room, in his wheelchair. He was facing the window but the blinds were half-closed, the November light falling in stripes across his lap and the faded blue cardigan he wore to every meeting she'd attended. He wasn't reading, wasn't watching the small TV mounted in the upper corner of the room. He was sitting.

"Mr. Zielinski."

He turned his head. For a moment — just a moment — she saw the old sharpness. The pale eyes calculating, the posture adjusting, the mental assessment of who was at the door and what they wanted and what it would cost him. Then it slipped. It wasn't the act. It was something looser than that, as if the sharpness itself couldn't stay lit.

"Carmen." His voice was flat and present. "Close the door."

She closed it. She pulled the visitor chair to his bedside and sat down. His room was a single — 114A — and quiet. His things were sparse. A framed photograph of Eleanor on the dresser, much younger, laughing at something outside the frame. A stack of large-print Reader's Digests he never read. A calendar from the Elks Lodge stuck on September.

"I'm leaving," Carmen said. She'd meant to build up to it, but Harry had never been someone you built up to things with. He preferred information delivered without packaging.

"I know." He looked at her. "New Brunswick. Tammy mentioned it."

"It's a good hospital," she said. "Better hours."

"Better pay."

"Yeah."

They sat with that. The building hummed around them — a call bell down the hall, the rolling squeak of a laundry cart, someone's television playing a daytime court show at volume. The November light made the room feel like late afternoon even though it was barely past two.

"I never told you all of it," Harry said. "About how we started."

Carmen waited. Harry let the silence sit. She didn't fill it.

"I told you about the pharmacy switch," Harry said. "Meds got delayed. Orders got switched. Agnes went two weeks without hydrocodone. I got Tylenol for a hip that needed oxycodone. That started it. Not the reason."

He was looking at the photograph of Eleanor as he spoke. Not at Carmen. His hands were still in his lap, the knuckles swollen, the skin thin and spotted. His wedding ring was loose on his finger.

"Six months later they put Lola on cipro for a UTI," he said. "She was already on ropinirole."

Carmen felt the answer before she spoke it. Ciprofloxacin on top of ropinirole could shove the ropinirole too high, make Lola see things, drop her blood pressure, send her to the floor. With Lola's size and her bad baseline pressure, a fall could have cracked her skull or stopped her heart before anybody bothered to ask why.

"Agnes caught it?" Carmen asked.

Harry nodded once. "Before the first dose."

"And nobody changed it."

"The charge nurse said she'd pass it along. Cliff got told. Dr. Prasad's office got told. The pharmacy got told. Agnes wrote it down. She called. She waited. Nothing changed."

He rubbed his thumb once over the loose gold of his wedding ring.

"Lola took the cipro anyway?" Carmen asked.

"Whole course," he said. "Nothing happened you could prove. Agnes didn't call it luck."

He stopped looking at the photograph and looked at Carmen.

"After that she checked every card on the floor," he said. "Mine first. I had two hydrocodone hidden in a cough-drop tin. They'd left me hurting too many nights."

Carmen said nothing.

"She should've turned me in. She didn't. A month later Marcus came to see Lola. She vouched for him. He knew a woman named Janine. She'd had back surgery. Her refill wasn't till Friday."

Harry's hand went still on the armrest.

"He put two hundred cash on my dresser for eight oxycodone," he said. "Jack was behind on rent. I took it."

The baseboard heat clicked on under the window. Neither of them moved.

"Agnes hated that part," Harry said. "She counted the pills anyway. After that we weren't just checking. We were holding back and pricing it."

He adjusted the cardigan on his shoulders. The gesture was small, habitual, the kind of thing a person does when they've said something that costs them and need their hands to do something ordinary.

"The beginning was Agnes catching what four other people missed," Harry said. "Nobody fixed it. After that, nobody was coming. We knew it."

The room was quiet. The call bell had stopped. The court show was still going but it was muffled through the wall, somebody's verdict delivered in a room that didn't matter.

Carmen didn't say anything for a while. She was thinking about the ciprofloxacin and the ropinirole, about the charge nurse who said she'd pass it along, about Cliff and the suggestion box and the pharmacy that filled orders from a warehouse somewhere and never saw the people whose names were on the labels. She was thinking about Agnes in her room three years ago, a pharmacist with forty-two years of expertise, writing her concern on a piece of paper and dropping it into a slot that went nowhere.

"Is that why she stopped eating?" Carmen asked.

Harry didn't answer right away. That was its own answer.

"She had a purpose," he said. "She doesn't anymore." A beat. "None of us do."

Carmen looked at his calendar on the wall. September. Nobody had turned it. October and November stacked behind it, unseen. She thought about reaching over and flipping it forward but didn't.

"You're going to be okay, Harry."

"We'll see."

She stood up. She straightened the blanket. She checked that his call bell was within reach. She moved his water pitcher closer.

"Carmen."

She stopped at the door.

"You did right by us. I want you to know that."

She put her hand on the chipped doorframe and caught the old smoke still living in her scrub cuff. She didn't know if he was wrong or if there just wasn't a clean answer left.

"I'll see you before my last day," she said.

He nodded. She left the door open behind her because Harry liked to hear the hallway — the carts, the voices, the building moving. It was company, even if it was the wrong kind.

Lola was in the hall when Carmen came out, parked by the linen-cart alcove with a paper cup of water in both hands. She wasn't close enough to Harry's door to call it waiting. It was close enough anyway.

"How is he?" Lola asked.

"He's tired."

Lola looked past Carmen toward 114A. Her tremor tapped the cup hard enough to make the water quiver. "Yeah," she said. "He hates that."

The activities aide called down the hall that bingo was starting in five minutes. Lola didn't turn.

"You want me to take you in there?" Carmen asked.

"No," Lola said. "Just leave me where I am a while."

She finished her shift. She charted her last round, initialed the count log, handed off to the three-to-eleven nurse, a woman named Keisha who'd started last month and still had the overwhelmed look of someone learning how much of the job wasn't in the textbook. Carmen briefed her on the wing — Doris's PRN for anxiety, Mr. Hartwell's dinner-time insulin, Agnes's intake, Earl's pending X-ray results. It was a professional handoff, a clean transfer of responsibility. The last time she'd do this, maybe, or the second-to-last. The New Brunswick interview was Thursday. She was going to get the job because she was good at this work and her references were clean and nobody at the hospital would know about the nights.

She walked out through the lobby. The late afternoon was already going dark — five o'clock in November, the parking lot lit by the sickly orange of sodium lamps, the cold hitting her face like a correction. She pulled her coat tighter and walked to her car.

Reggie was at her mother's. She drove the fifteen minutes with the radio off, thinking about nothing she could name, letting her mind do the thing it did when a shift was over — the slow disentanglement from other people's bodies, other people's medications, other people's declining trajectories. She'd learned early in nursing school that the drive home was when you separated yourself from the work, or didn't, and the nurses who couldn't separate burned out or drank or both. Carmen separated. She was good at it. She folded the shift into a compartment and drove.

Gloria had made Reggie dinner. He was at the kitchen table with his math homework and a glass of milk, his backpack on the floor, his shoes kicked off by the door. He was seven and three-quarters and lately everything he said involved fractions because they'd started fractions in class and he was delighted by them. He'd told her last week that he was three-eighths done with his book about octopuses. She'd asked how he'd calculated that and he'd said he counted the chapters, which was close enough.

"Hey, baby."

"Mom." He didn't look up from his worksheet. "What's nine-tenths minus three-tenths?"

"You know what it is."

"Six-tenths." He wrote it down. "That's the same as three-fifths."

"Yeah, it is."

Gloria was in the kitchen doorway, drying her hands on a dish towel. She took one look at Carmen and held whatever question she had. Gloria Reyes had raised three children on a custodian's salary after Carmen's father left. She knew how to wait.

"You eat?" Gloria asked.

"I'll eat at home."

"I made arroz con pollo. Take some."

"Mami, I'm fine."

"You're skinny. Take the arroz con pollo."

Carmen took the container because arguing with her mother about food was an argument nobody had ever won. She kissed Gloria on the cheek and smelled sofrito and Pine-Sol and the lavender hand cream Gloria had used since Carmen was a child.

"Reggie, shoes. Let's go."

He packed his homework into his backpack with the disorganized urgency of a second-grader who'd been told to hurry and gathered his shoes from the door. Carmen helped him with his coat. His hands were cold. She held them for a second, warming them between hers, and he let her because at seven and three-quarters he still let her hold his hands but she could feel the approaching age when he wouldn't.

The drive home was twenty-five minutes. Reggie talked about school — his friend Jaylen had brought a hermit crab for show and tell, and the hermit crab had climbed out of its container and the teacher had screamed, which was the funniest thing that had ever happened in Reggie's life. He told the story twice, both times with the hermit crab getting bigger and the scream getting louder. Carmen laughed. She laughed because it was funny and because her son was telling her a story and because the car was warm and the road was familiar and this part of her life was something she hadn't broken.

Reggie fell asleep in the back seat ten minutes from home. He did this — the sudden slide from talking to silence to sleep, as though his battery had run out mid-sentence. She glanced at him in the rearview. His head was against the window, his mouth slightly open, his backpack clutched to his chest like a stuffed animal he'd outgrown.

Her phone buzzed in the cupholder.

She picked it up at the next red light. One voicemail, from a number she recognized. Not saved in her contacts — she'd deleted Marcus's number three weeks ago — but she'd dialed it enough times that she still knew the digits.

She played it. Marcus's voice was low and steady.

"Hey. It's Marcus. Look, don't just delete this. I'm working again. Different setup now. Just me on the route, no extra hands. Rosa wound up in the county ER after she tried to stretch her meds another week. I couldn't leave it there. Call me when you can."

The light turned green. Carmen put the phone face-down on the passenger seat. She drove.

Reggie shifted in his sleep. She looked at him in the mirror again. His breath fogged the window glass.

She passed the pharmacy where Agnes had spent forty-two years, the strip mall where Marcus had made his handoffs, the road to the cemetery where Earl put flowers on Maryann's grave. From the car they were only lit signs and wet pavement.

She carried Reggie inside with the phone shoved into her coat pocket. He woke enough to walk the last ten feet, his hand in hers, his backpack dragging. She put him to bed. She stood in his doorway and watched him resettle into sleep, pulling the covers to his chin with the serious expression of a person doing important work.

She closed his door and stood in the hallway of her apartment. The arroz con pollo was on the kitchen counter. The application to New Brunswick was in her email, waiting.

She ate standing up at the counter, her mother's rice and chicken still warm from the container, and the day came back in pieces. She saw Agnes's untouched eggs again and Lola at the window. She saw Earl letting the X-ray tech line him up. Burt had slept through the whole thing. Harry's calendar was still on September. Marcus had said he was working again.

When she took off her coat, the phone slid from the pocket and hit the counter beside the arroz con pollo container. The screen lit once with the voicemail badge and went dark.

She washed the fork. She put the leftover rice in the fridge. She checked on Reggie one more time, his breathing steady and small in the dark room.

Then she went to bed. The phone stayed on the counter, black glass, Marcus's message still inside it.
